%=================================================================
% SECTION 1: INTRODUCTION TO DESMOS ON THE SAT
%=================================================================

\section{Introduction to Desmos on the SAT}

\subsection{What Is the Desmos Graphing Calculator?}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Desmos Graphing Calculator} is a free, powerful, web-based graphing calculator that is embedded directly into the Digital SAT testing platform (Bluebook). Starting with the Digital SAT (launched in 2024), every student has access to Desmos on \textbf{all math questions in both modules} of the Math section. You do not need to bring your own calculator---Desmos is built right into the test interface.
\end{definition}

The SAT Math section on the Digital SAT consists of two modules, each with 22 questions and a 35-minute time limit. Every single question---whether it involves algebra, geometry, data analysis, or advanced math---can be attempted with the Desmos calculator open. This is a game-changer. Unlike the old paper SAT where calculator use was restricted to one section, the Digital SAT gives you Desmos everywhere.

Desmos is not just a basic calculator that adds and multiplies. It is a \textbf{graphing calculator} that can plot equations, find intersections, create tables of values, perform regression, evaluate expressions, and much more. Learning to use it strategically can save you enormous amounts of time and help you avoid algebraic mistakes that cost points.

The version of Desmos on the SAT is slightly modified from the full online version. Some features (like sliders and certain advanced functions) may be limited, but the core graphing, table, and computation features are fully available. The calculator appears as a pop-up window within the Bluebook testing app, and you can resize it or close it as needed.

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you're taking a spelling test, and the teacher says, ``You can use a dictionary for every single question.'' That dictionary won't \textit{give} you the answers directly, but if you know how to use it well, you can check your work, find words you're unsure about, and avoid silly mistakes. Desmos on the SAT is like that dictionary---it's an incredibly powerful tool, but only if you know \textit{how} to use it. A student who has practiced with Desmos can solve problems in 30 seconds that would take 3 minutes by hand.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Why Desmos Is Your Secret Weapon}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\arabic*.]
\item \textbf{Speed:} Graphing an equation takes 2 seconds in Desmos vs.\ 2 minutes by hand. On a timed test, this matters enormously.
\item \textbf{Accuracy:} Desmos doesn't make arithmetic mistakes. If you type the equation correctly, the graph and calculations are perfect every time.
\item \textbf{Visualization:} Many SAT questions become trivially easy when you can \textit{see} the graph. Finding the vertex of a parabola, the number of solutions to a system, or the behavior of a function becomes visual rather than algebraic.
\item \textbf{Verification:} Even if you solve a problem by hand, you can use Desmos to check your answer in seconds.
\item \textbf{Bypass Algebra:} Some problems that require complex algebraic manipulation can be solved by simply graphing both sides of an equation and reading the answer.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Historical Context:} Before the Digital SAT, students could bring approved graphing calculators (like the TI-84) to the test, but many students couldn't afford one or didn't know how to use one effectively. College Board's decision to embed Desmos into the Digital SAT was designed to level the playing field---now \textit{every} student has access to the same powerful tool, regardless of economic background. This means that learning Desmos is not optional; it's a core SAT skill.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Key Features Available on SAT Desmos}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Expression Line (Equation Input):} Type any equation or expression. Desmos will graph it instantly.
\item \textbf{Graphing:} Plot lines, parabolas, circles, absolute value functions, exponentials, and more.
\item \textbf{Tables:} Create tables of $x$ and $y$ values for any function.
\item \textbf{Points:} Plot individual points and find distances or midpoints visually.
\item \textbf{Intersection Finding:} Click on the intersection of two graphs to get exact coordinates.
\item \textbf{Zoom and Pan:} Adjust the viewing window to see relevant parts of the graph.
\item \textbf{Arithmetic:} Use expression lines as a basic calculator (type \texttt{2+3} and press Enter).
\item \textbf{Regression:} Fit lines or curves to data points.
\item \textbf{Restrictions:} Limit the domain of a graph using curly braces (e.g., $y=x^2\{0<x<5\}$).
\item \textbf{Special Functions:} Absolute value (\texttt{abs()}), square root (\texttt{sqrt()}), trigonometric functions, and logarithms.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Insight:} You do NOT need to master every feature of Desmos. For the SAT, there are about 8--10 core techniques that cover 95\% of what you'll need. This guide teaches you exactly those techniques with SAT-specific practice.
\end{insightbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Critical:} The Desmos calculator on the SAT does \textbf{NOT} have a Computer Algebra System (CAS). It cannot solve equations symbolically (e.g., it won't factor $x^2-5x+6$ for you algebraically). However, you can \textit{graph} $y=x^2-5x+6$ and find the $x$-intercepts, which gives you the solutions. The graphical approach replaces the algebraic approach.
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common Misconception:} Some students think ``I'll just use Desmos for everything and not study math.'' This is wrong. Desmos is a \textit{tool} that enhances your math skills---it doesn't replace understanding. You still need to know what the question is asking, how to set up the problem, and how to interpret Desmos's output. Think of Desmos as a superpower that makes your existing skills faster and more accurate.
\end{warningbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Quick Orientation:} Suppose you open Desmos on the SAT and see the expression line at the left side of the screen. You want to find the value of $\dfrac{3^4 + 7 \times 12}{5^2 - 1}$.

Simply type into the expression line:
\begin{center}
\texttt{(3\^{}4 + 7*12)/(5\^{}2 - 1)}
\end{center}
Desmos instantly displays: $\dfrac{81 + 84}{25 - 1} = \dfrac{165}{24} = 6.875$

No pencil work needed. No risk of arithmetic error.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
The answer is $6.875$ or equivalently $\dfrac{55}{8}$. This took about 5 seconds in Desmos versus potentially a minute or more by hand, especially under test pressure where arithmetic mistakes are common.
\end{solution}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 1.1}

On the Digital SAT, the Desmos graphing calculator is available for:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Only the calculator-allowed module
\item Only questions involving graphs
\item All questions in both math modules
\item Only the second, harder math module
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} On the Digital SAT, Desmos is embedded in the Bluebook app and available for \textit{every} math question in both Module 1 and Module 2. There is no ``no-calculator'' section on the Digital SAT.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 1.2}

Which of the following can Desmos on the SAT \textbf{NOT} do directly?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Graph $y = 2x + 3$
\item Factor the expression $x^2 - 9x + 20$
\item Find the intersection of two lines
\item Calculate $\sqrt{144} + 3
^4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos cannot factor algebraically---it doesn't show you ``$(x-4)(x-5)$'' as output. However, you can \textit{work around this} by graphing $y = x^2 - 9x + 20$ and finding the $x$-intercepts (which give you the roots, and therefore the factors). Options A, C, and D are all things Desmos can do directly.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 1.3}

A student wants to solve $3x + 7 = 22$ using Desmos. Which approach would work?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Type ``solve $3x + 7 = 22$'' into Desmos
\item Graph $y = 3x + 7$ and $y = 22$ and find their intersection
\item Type $3x + 7 = 22$ into the expression line and press Enter
\item Desmos cannot solve linear equations
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos is a \textit{graphing} calculator. To solve equations, you graph both sides as separate functions and find where they intersect. Option A doesn't work because Desmos has no ``solve'' command in the SAT version. Option C would just plot a vertical line or give an error. Option D is false---Desmos can solve them via graphing.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 1.4}

What is the primary advantage of using Desmos on the SAT?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item It solves every problem automatically
\item It eliminates arithmetic errors and provides visual confirmation of answers
\item It shows you the correct answer choice directly
\item It replaces the need to understand mathematical concepts
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The greatest advantage of Desmos is that it eliminates computational errors and lets you visually verify your work. It does \textit{not} solve problems automatically (A), show answer choices (C), or replace mathematical understanding (D). You still need to know how to set up problems correctly.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 1.5}

On the Digital SAT, approximately what percentage of math questions can benefit from strategic Desmos use?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item About 10--15\%
\item About 25--30\%
\item About 50--70\%
\item 100\% of all questions
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Experienced SAT tutors estimate that Desmos can be strategically helpful on roughly 50--70\% of Digital SAT math questions---either as the primary solving method or as a verification tool. Some questions (like those requiring conceptual reasoning or reading comprehension of math scenarios) benefit less from Desmos, but the majority can be approached or checked with it.
\end{highlightbox}

\subsection{Summary of Section 1}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Desmos is a free, embedded graphing calculator available on \textbf{every} Digital SAT math question.
\item It can graph functions, find intersections, compute expressions, create tables, and perform regressions.
\item It \textbf{cannot} do symbolic algebra (factoring, simplifying expressions as formulas).
\item The workaround for algebra: convert algebraic problems into graphical problems.
\item Desmos is a \textit{tool}---you still need mathematical understanding to use it effectively.
\item Strategic Desmos use can help on 50--70\% of SAT math questions.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 2: DESMOS INTERFACE AND ESSENTIAL OPERATIONS
%================================================================

\section{Mastering the Desmos Interface and Essential Operations}

\subsection{What Is the Desmos Interface?}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Desmos interface} on the Digital SAT consists of a \textbf{graphing area} (the coordinate plane on the right) and an \textbf{expression list} (the panel on the left where you type equations, expressions, and commands). The interface includes zoom controls, a settings gear, and clickable points on graphs.
\end{definition}

When you click the calculator icon during the Digital SAT, Desmos opens as an overlay on your screen. Understanding every part of this interface \textit{before} test day is essential---you don't want to waste precious seconds figuring out where things are.

\begin{analogy}
Think of the Desmos interface like a smartphone. The \textbf{expression list} on the left is like your text message area---it's where you type instructions. The \textbf{graph area} on the right is like the screen that shows you the result of what you typed. Just like you need to know where the keyboard, send button, and settings are on your phone, you need to know every part of Desmos before the test.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{The Expression List: Your Command Center}

The expression list is where all the magic happens. Here is everything you can type into it:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\arabic*.]
\item \textbf{Equations:} Type $y = 2x + 3$ to graph a line. Type $x^2 + y^2 = 25$ to graph a circle.
\item \textbf{Pure expressions (calculations):} Type \texttt{3\^{}4 + 7*12} and Desmos displays the numerical result immediately.
\item \textbf{Inequalities:} Type $y > 2x + 1$ to shade the region above the line.
\item \textbf{Points:} Type \texttt{(3, 5)} to plot a single point.
\item \textbf{Lists of points:} Type \texttt{(1, 2), (3, 4), (5, 6)} to plot multiple points.
\item \textbf{Tables:} Click the ``+'' button and select ``table'' to create an $x$/$y$ table.
\item \textbf{Sliders:} Type $y = ax + b$ and Desmos automatically offers to create sliders for $a$ and $b$.
\item \textbf{Piecewise functions:} Type conditions using curly braces.
\item \textbf{Notes:} You can add text notes to organize your work (not critical for SAT, but useful).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Type Common SAT Expressions in Desmos:}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item \textbf{Fractions:} Type the numerator, then press \texttt{/}, then the denominator. Desmos auto-formats it as $\dfrac{\text{numerator}}{\text{denominator}}$.
\item \textbf{Exponents:} Type the base, then press \texttt{\^{}}, then the exponent. For example, \texttt{x\^{}2} gives $x^2$.
\item \textbf{Square roots:} Type \texttt{sqrt(} and Desmos creates the radical symbol $\sqrt{\phantom{x}}$. Type the radicand inside.
\item \textbf{Absolute value:} Type \texttt{|x|} or use the \texttt{abs()} function.
\item \textbf{$\pi$:} Type \texttt{pi} and Desmos converts it to $\pi$.
\item \textbf{Parentheses:} Use \texttt{(} and \texttt{)} liberally. When in doubt, add more parentheses.
\item \textbf{Multiplication:} Use \texttt{*} or just type variables next to each other (e.g., \texttt{2x} works).
\item \textbf{Negative signs:} Use the minus key \texttt{-} before a number.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Parentheses Are Life-or-Death on the SAT!}

The single most common Desmos mistake is forgetting parentheses. Consider:
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{1/2x} means $\dfrac{1}{2} \cdot x = \dfrac{x}{2}$ \quad (Desmos interprets this as $\frac{1}{2}\cdot x$)
\item \texttt{1/(2x)} means $\dfrac{1}{2x}$ \quad (This is what you probably wanted!)
\end{itemize}
Similarly:
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{3\^{}2x} means $(3^2) \cdot x = 9x$
\item \texttt{3\^{}(2x)} means $3^{2x}$ \quad (Very different!)
\end{itemize}
\textbf{Rule: Always use parentheses around compound numerators, denominators, and exponents.}
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{The Graph Area: Reading Your Results}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{graph area} is the coordinate plane where Desmos displays all graphed equations, plotted points, shaded regions, and intersections. You can click on key features (intercepts, maxima, minima, intersections) and Desmos will display their exact coordinates.
\end{definition}

Key features of the graph area:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\arabic*.]
\item \textbf{Clicking on curves:} Click directly on a graphed curve and drag along it. Desmos shows the coordinates of every point as you move.
\item \textbf{Intersection points:} When two graphs cross, a gray dot appears at the intersection. Click it to see the exact coordinates.
\item \textbf{$x$-intercepts and $y$-intercepts:} These appear as gray dots on the axes. Click to see exact values.
\item \textbf{Maxima and minima:} For parabolas and other curves, Desmos shows gray dots at peaks and valleys.
\item \textbf{Zoom controls:} Use the $+$ and $-$ buttons (or pinch on a touchscreen) to zoom in and out.
\item \textbf{Home button:} Resets the viewing window to the default zoom.
\item \textbf{Wrench icon (Settings):} Lets you manually set the $x$-range and $y$-range of the viewing window.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Pro Tip: Adjusting the Viewing Window}

If you graph something and it looks like nothing appeared, the graph might be outside your viewing window. Click the \textbf{wrench icon} (settings gear) and manually set appropriate $x$-min, $x$-max, $y$-min, and $y$-max values. For example, if you're graphing $y = 1000(1.05)^x$ for a compound interest problem, you might need $y$-max $= 2000$ and $x$-max $= 20$ to see anything useful.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Essential Keyboard Shortcuts and Tricks}

\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{3.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Action} & \textbf{How to Type It} & \textbf{SAT Use Case} \\
\midrule
\endhead
Fraction & \texttt{numerator / denominator} & Any fraction computation \\
\midrule
Exponent & \texttt{base \^{} exponent} & Exponential growth/decay, power functions \\
\midrule
Square root & \texttt{sqrt(expression)} & Distance formula, simplifying radicals \\
\midrule
$n$th root & \texttt{nthroot(n, expression)} & Cube roots, etc. \\
\midrule
Absolute value & \texttt{|expression|} or \texttt{abs(expression)} & Absolute value equations \\
\midrule
Pi ($\pi$) & \texttt{pi} & Circle problems, trig \\
\midrule
Subscript & \texttt{variable\_1} (underscore) & $a_1$, $a_2$ for sequences \\
\midrule
Inequality & \texttt{y > expression} or \texttt{y >= expression} & Systems of inequalities \\
\midrule
Piecewise & \texttt{\{condition: expression\}} & Piecewise function questions \\
\midrule
Summation & \texttt{sum} then fill template & Series problems (rare on SAT) \\
\midrule
Log base 10 & \texttt{log(x)} & Logarithm problems \\
\midrule
Log base $n$ & \texttt{log\_n(x)} (underscore for base) & Change of base problems \\
\midrule
Natural log & \texttt{ln(x)} & Continuous growth/decay \\
\midrule
Trig functions & \texttt{sin(x)}, \texttt{cos(x)}, \texttt{tan(x)} & Trig equations and graphs \\
\midrule
Restrict domain & \texttt{expression \{min < x < max\}} & Viewing specific portions \\
\midrule
Create a point & \texttt{(a, b)} & Plotting specific points to check \\
\midrule
Slider & Type a parameter like $a$ & Exploring transformations \\
\midrule
Regression & Type a table, then use $\sim$ & Curve fitting (rare on SAT) \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Critical Typing Skill:} On the Digital SAT, you are using a \textit{built-in} Desmos calculator within the Bluebook testing app. You cannot copy-paste. You must be able to type expressions quickly and accurately. \textbf{Practice typing in Desmos before test day!} Go to \texttt{desmos.com/calculator} and practice entering expressions until it feels natural.
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{analogy}
Learning the Desmos interface is like learning where all the buttons are on a TV remote. At first, you fumble around looking for the right button. But after using it a few times, you can change channels, adjust volume, and switch inputs without even looking. The same thing happens with Desmos---once you practice, typing expressions and reading graphs becomes automatic.
\end{analogy}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 2.1}

To graph the function $f(x) = -2(x-3)^2 + 5$ in Desmos, you would type:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item \texttt{f(x) = -2(x-3)\^{}2 + 5}
\item \texttt{y = -2(x-3)\^{}2 + 5}
\item Both A and B would work
\item Neither---Desmos cannot graph vertex form
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos accepts both function notation \texttt{f(x) = ...} and standard \texttt{y = ...} notation. Both will produce the same graph. You can use whichever you prefer.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 2.2}

You graph $y = x^2 - 10x + 21$ in Desmos but see nothing on the screen. What should you do?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Delete the equation---it must be wrong
\item Click the wrench/settings icon and adjust the viewing window
\item Try typing the equation again more carefully
\item The equation has no graph because it has no real solutions
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The parabola $y = x^2 - 10x + 21$ has its vertex at $(5, -4)$, which might be outside the default viewing window depending on zoom level. Adjusting the window via the settings (wrench icon) will reveal the graph. Option D is wrong because $y = x^2 - 10x + 21$ is a parabola that definitely has a graph---it crosses the $x$-axis at $x = 3$ and $x = 7$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 2.3}

In Desmos, how do you enter the expression $\dfrac{x^2 + 3x}{2x - 1}$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item \texttt{x\^{}2 + 3x / 2x - 1}
\item \texttt{(x\^{}2 + 3x) / (2x - 1)}
\item \texttt{x\^{}2 + 3x $\div$ 2x - 1}
\item \texttt{frac(x\^{}2 + 3x, 2x - 1)}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} You \textbf{must} use parentheses to group the numerator and denominator separately. Option A would be interpreted as $x^2 + \frac{3x}{2x} - 1$ due to order of operations. Desmos uses the slash \texttt{/} for division, and parentheses ensure the correct grouping. Always use parentheses around the entire numerator and entire denominator!
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 2.4}

Which Desmos feature allows you to find the exact coordinates where a line crosses the $x$-axis?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item The zoom button
\item Clicking on the gray dot that appears on the $x$-axis
\item The settings/wrench icon
\item The undo button
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} When a graph crosses the $x$-axis, Desmos displays a small gray dot at that point. Clicking (or tapping) on this dot reveals the exact coordinates, such as $(3, 0)$. This is how you find $x$-intercepts (roots/zeros) visually in Desmos.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 2.5}

You want to see what happens to the graph of $y = a \cdot x^2$ as $a$ changes. In Desmos, you type \texttt{y = a * x\^{}2}. What happens next?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Desmos shows an error
\item Desmos asks you to define $a$
\item Desmos automatically creates a slider for $a$
\item Nothing happens until you also type a value for $a$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} When you use an undefined parameter (like $a$) in an expression, Desmos automatically offers to create a slider for it. This slider lets you drag left and right to change the value of $a$ and watch the graph change in real time. This is incredibly useful for understanding transformations.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 2.6}

On the Digital SAT, can you use your own physical graphing calculator instead of the built-in Desmos?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item No, only the built-in Desmos is allowed
\item Yes, you can use any calculator you want
\item Yes, but only approved graphing calculators from the College Board list
\item Yes, but only a basic scientific calculator
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} On the Digital SAT, you have the built-in Desmos available at all times, but you are \textit{also} allowed to bring an approved calculator from the College Board's list (which includes TI-84, TI-Nspire CAS excluded, etc.). However, mastering the built-in Desmos is recommended because it is always available, free, and extremely powerful.
\end{highlightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 3: SOLVING LINEAR EQUATIONS AND SYSTEMS
%================================================================

\section{Solving Linear Equations and Systems with Desmos}

Linear equations and systems of linear equations are the \textbf{most frequently tested} algebra topics on the SAT. Desmos turns these problems---which can involve tedious algebra---into quick, visual solutions.

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{linear equation} is an equation whose graph is a straight line. It can be written in several forms:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Slope-intercept form:} $y = mx + b$ (where $m$ is the slope and $b$ is the $y$-intercept)
\item \textbf{Standard form:} $Ax + By = C$
\item \textbf{Point-slope form:} $y -
y_1 = m(x - x_1)$ (where $(x_1, y_1)$ is a known point)
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{system of linear equations} is a set of two or more linear equations with the same variables. The \textbf{solution} to the system is the point (or points) where all equations are satisfied simultaneously---graphically, this is where the lines \textit{intersect}.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine two friends are walking across a field from different starting points in different directions. A system of equations is like asking: ``Will they ever be at the exact same spot at the same time?'' The intersection point on the graph is where they meet. If they walk in parallel (same slope, different intercept), they \textit{never} meet---that's ``no solution.'' If they walk along the exact same path, they're \textit{always} together---that's ``infinitely many solutions.''
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Method 1: Graphing Two Lines to Find the Intersection}

This is the \textbf{single most powerful Desmos technique} for the SAT. Any time you see a system of two equations, you can graph both and click the intersection.

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Steps to Solve a System of Equations in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item \textbf{Type the first equation} into Line 1 of the expression list. For example: \texttt{y = 2x + 3}
\item \textbf{Type the second equation} into Line 2. For example: \texttt{y = -x + 9}
\item \textbf{Look at the graph.} The two lines will appear, and Desmos will show a \textbf{gray dot} at their intersection point.
\item \textbf{Click the gray dot.} Desmos displays the exact coordinates, e.g., $(2, 7)$.
\item \textbf{Read your answer.} If the question asks for $x$, your answer is $2$. If it asks for $y$, your answer is $7$. If it asks for $x + y$, your answer is $9$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT-Style Problem:} What is the solution $(x, y)$ to the following system?
\[
\begin{cases} 3x + 2y = 12 \\ x - y = 1 \end{cases}
\]

\textbf{Desmos Approach:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: Type \texttt{3x + 2y = 12}
\item Line 2: Type \texttt{x - y = 1}
\item Desmos graphs both lines and shows the intersection point.
\item Click the intersection dot: $(2.8, 1.8)$ or equivalently $\left(\dfrac{14}{5}, \dfrac{9}{5}\right)$.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
The intersection point is $\left(\dfrac{14}{5}, \dfrac{9}{5}\right)$, so $x = \dfrac{14}{5} = 2.8$ and $y = \dfrac{9}{5} = 1.8$.

\textbf{Verification by algebra (optional):} From the second equation, $x = y + 1$. Substituting into the first: $3(y+1) + 2y = 12 \Rightarrow 3y + 3 + 2y = 12 \Rightarrow 5y = 9 \Rightarrow y = \dfrac{9}{5}$. Then $x = \dfrac{9}{5} + 1 = \dfrac{14}{5}$. \checkmark

Notice how Desmos gave us the answer in about 10 seconds, while the algebra took significantly longer.
\end{solution}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{SAT Speed Hack:} You do NOT need to rearrange equations into $y = mx + b$ form before typing them into Desmos. Desmos can graph equations in \textit{any} form: standard form ($Ax + By = C$), point-slope form, or even weird forms like $2(x+1) = 3(y-4) + 7$. Just type it exactly as given in the problem!
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Method 2: Using a Single Equation for Single-Variable Problems}

Many SAT problems give you a single equation like $3x + 7 = 22$ or $\dfrac{2x-1}{3} = 5$ and ask you to solve for $x$.

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Steps to Solve a Single Equation in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item \textbf{Separate the equation into two sides.} The left side becomes one function, the right side becomes another.
\item \textbf{Graph both sides:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: $y = \text{left side}$
\item Line 2: $y = \text{right side}$
\end{itemize}
\item \textbf{Find the intersection.} The $x$-coordinate of the intersection is your solution.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:} To solve $3x + 7 = 22$:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = 3x + 7}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 22}
\item Intersection at $(5, 22)$, so $x = 5$.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Alternative Quick Method:} For simple equations, you can also type the equation directly (e.g., \texttt{3x + 7 = 22}) and Desmos will draw a vertical line at the solution. However, this vertical line can be hard to read precisely. The two-line intersection method is more reliable and gives you exact coordinates when you click.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Special Cases: No Solution and Infinitely Many Solutions}

\begin{theorem}
For a system of two linear equations:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{One solution:} The lines intersect at exactly one point (different slopes).
\item \textbf{No solution:} The lines are parallel---same slope, different $y$-intercepts. In Desmos, you will see two lines that never touch.
\item \textbf{Infinitely many solutions:} The lines are identical---same slope AND same $y$-intercept. In Desmos, you will see what looks like one line (because they overlap completely).
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT-Style Problem:} How many solutions does the following system have?
\[
\begin{cases} 2x + 4y = 10 \\ x + 2y = 3 \end{cases}
\]

\textbf{Desmos Approach:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{2x + 4y = 10}
\item Line 2: \texttt{x + 2y = 3}
\item Observe: The lines are parallel (no intersection point appears).
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
The system has \textbf{no solution}. Notice that the first equation simplifies to $x + 2y = 5$ (dividing by 2), while the second equation is $x + 2y = 3$. These have the same left side but different right sides, meaning they are parallel lines that never intersect.

In Desmos, you would see two parallel lines with no gray intersection dot.
\end{solution}

\subsection{SAT-Specific Linear Equation Strategies}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{``Value of $a$ for No Solution'' Problems:} The SAT frequently asks: ``For what value of $a$ does the system have no solution?'' or ``\ldots infinitely many solutions?''

\textbf{Desmos Strategy:} Use sliders!
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type both equations with the unknown constant as a parameter (e.g., $a$).
\item Desmos will create a slider for $a$.
\item Drag the slider until the lines become parallel (no solution) or overlap (infinite solutions).
\item Read the value of $a$ from the slider.
\end{enumerate}
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT-Style Problem:} For what value of $a$ does the system below have no solution?
\[
\begin{cases} ax + 3y = 12 \\ 4x + 6y = 5 \end{cases}
\]

\textbf{Desmos Approach:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{ax + 3y = 12} (Desmos creates a slider for $a$)
\item Line 2: \texttt{4x + 6y = 5}
\item Drag the slider for $a$ until the two lines become parallel.
\item You'll find this happens at $a = 2$.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
For no solution, the lines must be parallel (same slope, different intercepts).

The second equation: $4x + 6y = 5 \Rightarrow y = -\dfrac{2}{3}x + \dfrac{5}{6}$, so slope $= -\dfrac{2}{3}$.

The first equation: $ax + 3y = 12 \Rightarrow y = -\dfrac{a}{3}x + 4$, so slope $= -\dfrac{a}{3}$.

For parallel lines: $-\dfrac{a}{3} = -\dfrac{2}{3} \Rightarrow a = 2$.

Check intercepts differ: $4 \neq \dfrac{5}{6}$ \checkmark

So $a = 2$ gives no solution. The Desmos slider approach finds this in seconds without any algebra.
\end{solution}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 3.1}

What is the $x$-coordinate of the solution to the system $y = 4x - 1$ and $y = -2x + 11$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x = 1$
\item $x = 2$
\item $x = 3$
\item $x = 5$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph both lines in Desmos. Line 1: \texttt{y = 4x - 1}. Line 2: \texttt{y = -2x + 11}. Click the intersection: $(2, 7)$. The $x$-coordinate is $2$.

Algebraically: $4x - 1 = -2x + 11 \Rightarrow 6x = 12 \Rightarrow x = 2$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 3.2}

The system $\begin{cases} 6x - 3y = 9 \\ -2x + y = -3 \end{cases}$ has:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Exactly one solution
\item No solution
\item Infinitely many solutions
\item Exactly two solutions
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph both in Desmos. They appear as the same line (they overlap completely). Algebraically: the first equation simplifies to $2x - y = 3$, and the second equation gives $-2x + y = -3$, which is $2x - y = 3$. They are the same equation, so infinitely many solutions.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 3.3}

If $5x + 2y = 20$ and $3x - y = 1$, what is the value of $x + y$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $5$
\item $7$
\item $8$
\item $9$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph both equations in Desmos. Click the intersection to get $(2, 5)$. Therefore $x + y = 2 + 5 = 7$. Wait---let's verify: $5(2) + 2(5) = 10 + 10 = 20$ \checkmark. $3(2) - 5 = 6 - 5 = 1$ \checkmark. So $x + y = 7$.

Actually the correct answer is \textbf{B}, $x + y = 7$. Let me correct: \textbf{Correct Answer: B}.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 3.4}

For what value of $k$ does the system $\begin{cases} 3x + ky = 15 \\ 6x + 10y = 7 \end{cases}$ have no solution?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $k = 3$
\item $k = 5$
\item $k = 7$
\item $k = 10$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, type both equations using $k$ as a slider. Drag the slider until the lines are parallel (no intersection). This
happens when the slopes are equal but $y$-intercepts differ. For no solution, we need $\frac{3}{6} = \frac{k}{10} \neq \frac{15}{7}$. So $\frac{k}{10} = \frac{1}{2}$, giving $k = 5$. Since $\frac{15}{7} \neq \frac{1}{2}$, the system has no solution when $k = 5$.

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{3x + ky = 15} and \texttt{6x + 10y = 7}. Desmos creates a slider for $k$. Drag until the lines are parallel. You'll see they become parallel at $k = 5$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 3.5}

A store sells notebooks for \$3 each and pens for \$1.50 each. Maria buys a total of 10 items and spends \$22.50. How many notebooks did she buy?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item 3
\item 4
\item 5
\item 6
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Let $x$ = notebooks and $y$ = pens. The system is:
\begin{align*}
x + y &= 10 \\
3x + 1.5y &= 22.5
\end{align*}
Graph both in Desmos: \texttt{x + y = 10} and \texttt{3x + 1.5y = 22.5}. Click the intersection: $(5, 5)$. Maria bought 5 notebooks.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 3.6}

Using Desmos, what is the fastest way to solve $7x - 3 = 2x + 12$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Type the equation directly and Desmos solves it
\item Graph $y = 7x - 3$ and $y = 2x + 12$, then find the intersection
\item Type \texttt{solve(7x - 3 = 2x + 12)}
\item Desmos cannot solve single-variable equations
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos doesn't have a ``solve'' command. The standard technique is to graph each side as a separate function ($y = 7x - 3$ and $y = 2x + 12$) and find the intersection. The $x$-coordinate of the intersection is the solution. Clicking the intersection gives $(3, 18)$, so $x = 3$.
\end{highlightbox}

\subsection{Desmos Strategy: Finding the Value of an Expression}

Many SAT questions don't just ask for $x$ or $y$---they ask for something like $3x - 2y$ or $x + y$. Here's a powerful Desmos strategy:

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Strategy: Finding Expression Values from Systems}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item Graph both equations to find the intersection point $(x_0, y_0)$.
\item Click the intersection to read off $x_0$ and $y_0$.
\item Open a new expression line and type the expression with the values substituted. For example, type \texttt{3(2) - 2(5)} if the intersection was $(2, 5)$.
\item Read the answer directly.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Even Faster Alternative:} After finding the intersection $(x_0, y_0)$, you can type the expression using the variables. For instance, after finding $x = 2$ and $y = 5$, type \texttt{3*2 - 2*5} to get $-4$.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Multi-Part System of Equations Problem}

A school fundraiser sells adult tickets for \$8 and student tickets for \$5. On opening night, 200 tickets were sold for a total of \$1,240.

\textbf{Part A:} Write a system of equations where $a$ = adult tickets and $s$ = student tickets.

\textbf{Part B:} Use Desmos to graph the system and find the number of each type of ticket sold.

\textbf{Part C:} If the school's goal was to sell at least 100 adult tickets, did they meet their goal?

\textbf{Part D:} What is the value of $3a - 2s$?

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}

\textbf{Part A:}
\begin{align*}
a + s &= 200 \\
8a + 5s &= 1240
\end{align*}

\textbf{Part B:} In Desmos, graph \texttt{a + s = 200} and \texttt{8a + 5s = 1240} (using $x$ for $a$ and $y$ for $s$, so type \texttt{x + y = 200} and \texttt{8x + 5y = 1240}). The intersection is $(80, 120)$. So 80 adult tickets and 120 student tickets were sold.

\textbf{Part C:} They sold 80 adult tickets, which is less than 100. They did \textbf{not} meet their goal.

\textbf{Part D:} $3(80) - 2(120) = 240 - 240 = 0$.
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary: Linear Equations and Systems}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways for Section 3:}
\begin{itemize}
\item To solve any equation in one variable, graph each side as a separate $y =$ equation and find the intersection's $x$-coordinate.
\item To solve a system, graph both equations and click the intersection.
\item Desmos handles slope-intercept, standard form, and point-slope form equally well.
\item For ``no solution'' or ``infinitely many'' questions, use a slider for the unknown constant and observe when lines become parallel or overlap.
\item Always click intersection points---don't just eyeball them.
\item For expression-value questions, read off coordinates first, then compute.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 4: QUADRATICS AND PARABOLAS
%================================================================

\section{Quadratics and Parabolas with Desmos}

Quadratic functions appear on \textbf{nearly every SAT}. Questions involve finding roots (zeros), vertex, axis of symmetry, and solving quadratic equations. Desmos makes all of these tasks visual and fast.

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{quadratic function} is a polynomial function of degree 2. It can be written in three standard forms:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Standard form:} $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$ (easiest to find the $y$-intercept: $c$)
\item \textbf{Vertex form:} $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$ (easiest to find the vertex: $(h, k)$)
\item \textbf{Factored form:} $f(x) = a(x - r_1)(x - r_2)$ (easiest to find the roots: $r_1$ and $r_2$)
\end{itemize}
The graph of a quadratic function is a \textbf{parabola}.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Think of a quadratic like a basketball shot. When you throw a basketball, it follows a curved path (a parabola). The highest point of the arc is the \textbf{vertex}. Where the ball leaves your hand and where it goes through the hoop are like the \textbf{roots} (where the parabola crosses a certain height). The shape of the arc depends on how hard and at what angle you throw---that's like the coefficient $a$.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Finding Roots (Zeros/Solutions) with Desmos}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{roots} (also called \textbf{zeros}, \textbf{solutions}, or \textbf{$x$-intercepts}) of a quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ are the $x$-values where the parabola crosses the $x$-axis.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Finding Roots of a Quadratic with Desmos}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item Type the quadratic into Desmos: \texttt{y = ax\^{}2 + bx + c} (with actual numbers).
\item Look at the graph. The points where the parabola touches or crosses the $x$-axis are the roots.
\item Click on each $x$-intercept. Desmos displays the exact coordinates.
\item The $x$-values of these points are your solutions.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{What if the parabola doesn't touch the $x$-axis?} Then the quadratic has \textbf{no real solutions} (the discriminant is negative).

\textbf{What if it just touches the $x$-axis at one point?} Then there is exactly \textbf{one real solution} (a repeated root).
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Find the solutions to $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$.}

In Desmos, type: \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 5x + 6}

The parabola crosses the $x$-axis at two points. Click each one:
\begin{itemize}
\item First intercept: $(2, 0)$
\item Second intercept: $(3, 0)$
\end{itemize}

The solutions are $x = 2$ and $x = 3$.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
This confirms the factored form: $x^2 - 5x + 6 = (x-2)(x-3) = 0$, giving $x = 2$ and $x = 3$. Desmos found these in about 5 seconds without any factoring.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Find the solutions to $2x^2 + 3x - 7 = 0$.}

This quadratic is hard to factor. In Desmos, type: \texttt{y = 2x\^{}2 + 3x - 7}

Click the $x$-intercepts:
\begin{itemize}
\item First intercept: approximately $(-2.766, 0)$
\item Second intercept: approximately $(1.266, 0)$
\end{itemize}

If the SAT asks for the positive root, the answer is approximately $1.266$. If answer choices are given, match to the closest one.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Using the quadratic formula: $x = \frac{-3 \pm \sqrt{9 + 56}}{4} = \frac{-3 \pm \sqrt{65}}{4}$. Since $\sqrt{65} \approx 8.062$, the positive root is $\frac{-3 + 8.062}{4} \approx 1.266$. Desmos gave us this instantly.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Finding the Vertex with Desmos}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Finding the Vertex of a Parabola with Desmos}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textbf{Step \arabic*:}]
\item Type the quadratic into Desmos.
\item Look for the highest point (if $a < 0$, the parabola opens downward) or the lowest point (if $a > 0$, the parabola opens upward).
\item Desmos automatically places a gray dot at the vertex. Click it.
\item Read the coordinates $(h, k)$---this is the vertex.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Find the vertex of $y = -2x^2 + 8x - 3$.}

Type into Desmos: \texttt{y = -2x\^{}2 + 8x - 3}

The parabola opens downward (since $a = -2 < 0$). Click the maximum point (gray dot at the top).

Desmos shows: $(2, 5)$.

The vertex is $(2, 5)$, meaning the maximum value of the function is $5$, occurring at $x = 2$.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Verification: $x_{\text{vertex}} = -\frac{b}{2a} = -\frac{8}{2(-2)} = -\frac{8}{-4} = 2$. Then $y = -2(2)^2 + 8(2) - 3 = -8 + 16 - 3 = 5$. Vertex is $(2, 5)$. \checkmark
\end{solution}

\subsection{Solving Quadratic Equations Set Equal to a Constant}

\begin{theorem}
To solve $ax^2 + bx + c = k$ using Desmos, graph $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ and $y = k$, then find their intersection(s). The $x$-coordinates of the intersections are the solutions.
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve $x^2 - 4x + 1 = 5$.}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 4x + 1}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 5}
\end{itemize}

Click the intersections:
\begin{itemize}
\item $(-0.828, 5)$ and $(4.828, 5)$
\end{itemize}

Wait---let me solve more carefully. $x^2 - 4x + 1 = 5 \Rightarrow x^2 - 4x - 4 = 0$. Using the quadratic formula: $x = \frac{4 \pm \sqrt{16 + 16}}{2} = \frac{4 \pm \sqrt{32}}{2} = 2 \pm 2\sqrt{2}$.

So $x \approx -0.828$ or $x \approx 4.828$. Desmos gives both instantly.
\end{example}

\subsection{The Discriminant: How Many Solutions?}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{discriminant} of $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ is $\Delta = b^2 - 4ac$.
\begin{itemize}
\item If $\Delta > 0$: Two distinct real solutions (parabola crosses $x$-axis twice)
\item If $\Delta = 0$: Exactly one real solution (parabola touches $x$-axis once)
\item If $\Delta < 0$: No real solutions (parabola doesn't touch $x$-axis)
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Desmos Shortcut for Discriminant Questions:} Instead of computing $b^2 - 4ac$ by hand, just graph the quadratic and \textit{look}. Count how many times it crosses the $x$-axis. Zero crossings = no real solutions. One touch = one solution. Two crossings = two solutions. This is faster and error-free.
\end{insightbox}

\begin{analogy}
Think of the discriminant like checking whether a basketball shot will hit the rim. If $\Delta > 0$, the ball goes through the hoop (crosses the $x$-axis twice). If $\Delta = 0$, it barely grazes the rim (touches once). If $\Delta < 0$, it's an air ball---misses completely (never touches the $x$-axis).
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Finding the Equation of a Quadratic from Its Graph}

Some SAT questions give you information about a parabola (roots, vertex, a point) and ask you to find the equation. Desmos can help you reverse-engineer the equation.

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Method: Using Sliders to Match a Quadratic}
\begin{enumerate}
\item If you know the roots $r$ and $s$, type: \texttt{y = a(x - r)(x - s)} and use the slider for $a$.
\item If you know the vertex $(h, k)$, type: \texttt{y = a(x - h)\^{}2 + k} and use the slider for $a$.
\item Adjust the slider for $a$ until the parabola passes through any additional given point.
\item Read off the value of $a$ from the slider.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{A parabola has roots at $x = -1$ and $x = 5$, and passes through the point $(1, -12)$. Find the equation.}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{y = a(x - (-1))(x - 5)} which is \texttt{y = a(x + 1)(x - 5)}
\item Type the point: \texttt{(1, -12)}
\item Adjust the slider for $a$ until the parabola passes through the point $(1, -12)$.
\item You'll find $a = 1.5$ or $a = \frac{3}{2}$.
\end{itemize}

So the equation is $y = \frac{3}{2}(x + 1)(x - 5)$.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Verification: At $x = 1$: $y = \frac{3}{2}(1 + 1)(1 - 5) = \frac{3}{2}(2)(-4) = \frac{3}{2}(-8) = -12$. \checkmark
\end{solution}

\subsection{Completing the Square via Desmos}

\begin{theorem}
The SAT often asks you to rewrite $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ in vertex form $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$. Instead of completing the square algebraically, use Desmos:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph the quadratic.
\item Click the vertex to read $(h, k)$.
\item The value of $a$ is the same as the coefficient of $x^2$ in the original equation.
\item Write the answer: $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Rewrite $y = 3x^2 - 12x + 7$ in vertex form.}

In Desmos, type: \texttt{y = 3x\^{}2 - 12x + 7}

Click the vertex: $(2, -5)$.

Since $a = 3$ (same as original), the vertex form is:
$$y = 3(x - 2)^2 - 5$$
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Verification: $3(x-2)^2 - 5 = 3(x^2 - 4x + 4) - 5 = 3x^2 - 12x + 12 - 5 = 3x^2 - 12x + 7$. \checkmark
\end{solution}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 4.1}

What are the solutions to $x^2 + 2x - 15 = 0$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x = -5$ and $x = 3$
\item $x = 5$ and $x = -3$
\item $x = -5$ and $x = -3$
\item $x = 5$ and $x = 3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = x\^{}2 + 2x - 15} in Desmos. The parabola crosses the $x$-axis at $(-5, 0)$ and $(3, 0)$. So $x = -5$ and $x = 3$. Algebraically: $(x+5)(x-3) = 0$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 4.2}

What is the vertex of $y = x^2 - 6x + 13$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(3, 4)$
\item $(-3, 4)$
\item $(3, -4)$
\item $(6, 13)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 6x + 13} in Desmos. Click the minimum point. The vertex is at $(3, 4)$. Note that this parabola has no $x$-intercepts (discriminant $= 36 - 52 = -16 < 0$), but the vertex is still visible.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 4.3}

For what value of $c$ does $x^2 - 8x + c = 0$ have exactly one real solution?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $c = 8$
\item $c = 16$
\item $c = 32$
\item $c = 64$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 8x + c}. Use the slider for $c$. Drag until the parabola just barely touches the $x$-axis (one solution). This happens at $c = 16$. Algebraically: discriminant $= 64 - 4c = 0 \Rightarrow c = 16$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 4.4}

If $f(x) = -x^2 + 4x + 5$, what is the maximum value of $f(x)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $4$
\item $5$
\item $9$
\item $13$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = -x\^{}2 + 4x + 5} in Desmos. Since $a = -1 < 0$, the parabola opens downward. Click the maximum (vertex). The vertex is at $(2, 9)$. The maximum value is $9$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 4.5}

The equation $2x^2 + bx + 8 = 0$ has roots $x = \frac{1}{2}$ and $x = c$. What is the value of $c$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $c = -8$
\item $c = 4$
\item $c = 8$
\item $c = -4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A} (Correction below)

\textbf{Rationale:} By Vieta's formulas for $2x^2 + bx + 8 = 0$: product of roots $= \frac{8}{2} = 4$. So $\frac{1}{2} \cdot c = 4 \Rightarrow c = 8$.

\textbf{Corrected Answer: C}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{y = 2(x - 0.5)(x - c)} and expand. You need the constant term to equal $8$: $2(0.5)(c) = c = 8$ when you set $2 \cdot (-0.5)(-c) = 2(0.5c) = c$. Set $c = 8$: constant $= 2(0.5)(8) = 8$. \checkmark
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 4.6}

Which of the following is equivalent to $y = 2x^2 + 12x + 22$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $y = 2(x + 3)^2 + 4$
\item $y = 2(x + 6)^2 + 4$
\item $y = 2(x + 3)^2 + 22$
\item $y = 2(x - 3)^2 + 4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = 2x\^{}2 + 12x + 22} in Desmos. Click the vertex: $(-3, 4)$. Since $a = 2$, vertex form is $y = 2(x + 3)^2 + 4$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Extended Quadratic Challenge (Multi-Part):}

A ball is launched upward from a platform. Its height $h$ (in feet) after $t$ seconds is given by $h(t) = -16t^2 + 64t + 80$.

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item What is the initial height of the ball (when $t = 0$)?
\item At what time does the ball reach its maximum height?
\item What is the maximum height?
\item At what time does the ball hit the ground?
\item For how many seconds is the ball above 100 feet?
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Approach:} Type \texttt{y = -16x\^{}2 + 64x + 80} (using $x$ instead of $t$).

\textbf{Solutions:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item At $t = 0$: $h(0) = 80$ feet. (The $y$-intercept on the graph.)
\item Click the vertex: $(2, 144)$. Maximum height occurs at $t = 2$ seconds.
\item Maximum height is $144$ feet (the $y$-value of the vertex).
\item The ball hits the ground when $h = 0$. Click the positive $x$-intercept: $t = 5$ seconds. (The other intercept is $t = -1$, which is not physically meaningful.)
\item Graph \texttt{y = 100} as a second line. Find the two intersections with the parabola. Desmos shows approximately $t = 0.354$ and $t = 3.646$. The ball is above 100 feet for $3.646 - 0.354 \approx 3.29$ seconds.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

%================================================================
% SECTION 5: POLYNOMIAL AND RATIONAL FUNCTIONS
%================================================================

\section{Polynomial and Rational Functions with Desmos}

Beyond quadratics, the SAT tests higher-degree polynomials and occasionally rational functions. Desmos is extraordinarily powerful for these because solving cubic or higher-degree equations by hand is extremely difficult, but graphing them takes seconds.

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{polynomial function} is a function of the form:
$$f(x) = a_nx^n + a_{n-1}x^{n-1} + \cdots + a_1x + a_0$$
where $n$ is a non-negative integer (the \textbf{degree}) and the $a_i$ are real number coefficients.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{rational function} is a function that can be written as the ratio of two polynomials:
$$f(x) = \frac{p(x)}{q(x)}$$
where $q(x) \neq 0$. The values where $q(x) = 0$ create \textbf{vertical asymptotes} or \textbf{holes} in the graph.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
A polynomial is like stacking building blocks of increasing size---$x$, $x^2$, $x^3$---each one adding more complexity to the shape. A rational function is like dividing one stack of blocks by another, which can create ``forbidden zones'' (vertical asymptotes) where the function shoots off to infinity.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Graphing Polynomials to Find Roots}

\begin{theorem}
The \textbf{roots} (or \textbf{zeros}) of a polynomial $f(x) = 0$ are the $x$-intercepts of the graph $y = f(x)$. In Desmos, graph the polynomial and click each $x$-intercept to read the roots.
\end{theorem}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Finding All Roots of a Polynomial with Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the polynomial into the expression line: e.g., \texttt{y = x\^{}3 - 6x\^{}2 + 11x - 6}
\item Look at the graph and identify all $x$-intercepts (gray dots on the $x$-axis).
\item Click each gray dot to read the exact $x$-value.
\item These $x$-values are the roots/solutions.
\item If the graph touches but doesn't cross the $x$-axis, that root has even multiplicity (it's a repeated root).
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Find all solutions to $x^3 - 6x^2 + 11x - 6 = 0$.}

In Desmos, type: \texttt{y = x\^{}3 - 6x\^{}2 + 11x - 6}

Click the three $x$-intercepts:
\begin{itemize}
\item $(1, 0)$, $(2, 0)$, $(3, 0)$
\end{itemize}

The solutions are $x = 1$, $x = 2$, and $x = 3$.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
This means $x^3 - 6x^2 + 11x - 6 = (x-1)(x-2)(x-3)$. Trying to factor a cubic by hand is time-consuming. Desmos gave us all three roots in about 10 seconds.
\end{solution}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{SAT Superpower:} Many SAT problems ask ``How many real solutions does [equation] have?'' or ``What is one possible value of $x$?'' With Desmos, you just graph and count or click. You don't need to factor, use synthetic division, or apply the rational root theorem.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Remainder and Factor Theorem via Desmos}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Factor Theorem:} $(x - r)$ is a factor of polynomial $p(x)$ if and only if $p(r) = 0$. In other words, $r$ is a root.

\textbf{Remainder Theorem:} When $p(x)$ is divided by $(x - r)$, the remainder is $p(r)$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Using Desmos for Remainder/Factor Problems:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph $y = p(x)$.
\item To check if $(x - r)$ is a factor: see if the graph passes through $(r, 0)$. If yes, it's a factor.
\item To find the remainder when dividing by $(x - r)$: evaluate $p(r)$ by typing \texttt{p(r)} directly, or clicking the graph at $x = r$ and reading the $y$-value.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{When $p(x) = 2x^3 - 3x^2 + x
- 5$ is divided by $(x - 2)$, what is the remainder?}

In Desmos, type: \texttt{p(x) = 2x\^{}3 - 3x\^{}2 + x - 5}

Then type: \texttt{p(2)}

Desmos outputs: $p(2) = 2(8) - 3(4) + 2 - 5 = 16 - 12 + 2 - 5 = 1$.

The remainder is $1$. Since the remainder is not zero, $(x - 2)$ is \textit{not} a factor of $p(x)$.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
By the Remainder Theorem, the remainder when $p(x)$ is divided by $(x-2)$ equals $p(2)$. Desmos computed $p(2) = 1$ instantly. No long division needed!
\end{solution}

\subsection{Matching Polynomial Equations to Graphs}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{SAT Strategy: Multiple Choice Polynomial Identification}

When the SAT gives you a graph and asks which equation matches:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Note the $x$-intercepts from the graph.
\item Note whether the graph opens up or down (leading coefficient sign).
\item Note any repeated roots (where the graph touches but doesn't cross).
\item Graph each answer choice in Desmos and see which one matches the described features.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Watch Out for Multiplicity!} If a graph \textit{touches} the $x$-axis at $x = 3$ but doesn't cross it, the factor is $(x-3)^2$ (squared), not just $(x-3)$. If it crosses the axis, the factor has odd multiplicity. Desmos shows this behavior clearly---look for the ``bounce'' vs.\ ``cross'' pattern.
\end{warningbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 5.1}

How many real solutions does $x^4 - 5x^2 + 4 = 0$ have?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = x\^{}4 - 5x\^{}2 + 4} in Desmos. You'll see four $x$-intercepts at $x = -2, -1, 1, 2$. The equation factors as $(x^2-4)(x^2-1) = (x-2)(x+2)(x-1)(x+1) = 0$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 5.2}

If $f(x) = x^3 + 2x^2 - 5x - 6$, which of the following is a factor of $f(x)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(x + 4)$
\item $(x - 3)$
\item $(x + 1)$
\item $(x - 5)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, graph \texttt{y = x\^{}3 + 2x\^{}2 - 5x - 6}. Click the $x$-intercepts: $x = -3, -1, 2$. Since $x = -1$ is a root, $(x + 1)$ is a factor. Alternatively, type \texttt{f(-1)} and verify it equals $0$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 5.3}

What is the remainder when $p(x) = x^3 - 4x + 2$ is divided by $(x - 3)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-7$
\item $5$
\item $17$
\item $29$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type \texttt{p(x) = x\^{}3 - 4x + 2} in Desmos, then type \texttt{p(3)}. Result: $27 - 12 + 2 = 17$. By the Remainder Theorem, the remainder is $17$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 5.4}

The graph of $y = (x-1)^2(x+2)$ does which of the following at $x = 1$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Crosses the $x$-axis going upward
\item Crosses the $x$-axis going downward
\item Touches the $x$-axis but does not cross it
\item Does not touch the $x$-axis
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} The factor $(x-1)$ has exponent $2$ (even multiplicity), so the graph \textit{touches} the $x$-axis at $x = 1$ but bounces back without crossing. Graph it in Desmos to confirm: \texttt{y = (x-1)\^{}2 * (x+2)}.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 5.5}

For what value of $c$ does $f(x) = x^2 + 6x + c$ have exactly one real root?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $c = 6$
\item $c = 8$
\item $c = 9$
\item $c = 12$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 + 6x + c} and use the slider for $c$. Drag $c$ until the parabola just barely touches the $x$-axis (one root). This happens at $c = 9$. Algebraically: discriminant $= 36 - 4c = 0 \Rightarrow c = 9$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 5.6}

If $g(x) = 2x^3 - x^2 - 13x - 6$, what is the sum of all roots of $g(x) = 0$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-\frac{1}{2}$
\item $\frac{1}{2}$
\item $\frac{13}{2}$
\item $3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = 2x\^{}3 - x\^{}2 - 13x - 6} in Desmos. Click the three $x$-intercepts: $x = -2$, $x = -\frac{1}{2}$, and $x = 3$. Sum: $-2 + (-\frac{1}{2}) + 3 = \frac{1}{2}$. By Vieta's formulas, the sum of roots $= \frac{-(-1)}{2} = \frac{1}{2}$. \checkmark
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Extended Problem: Polynomial Detective Work}

A polynomial $p(x)$ has the following properties:
\begin{itemize}
\item It is a cubic (degree 3) with leading coefficient $1$.
\item It has roots at $x = -3$ and $x = 4$.
\item $p(0) = 24$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Part A:} If the third root is $r$, write $p(x) = (x+3)(x-4)(x-r)$.

\textbf{Part B:} Use the condition $p(0) = 24$ to find $r$. In Desmos, type \texttt{p(x) = (x+3)(x-4)(x-r)} and set up a slider for $r$. Evaluate \texttt{p(0)} and adjust $r$ until $p(0) = 24$.

\textbf{Part C:} Write the final equation and verify by graphing.

\textbf{Solution:}

$p(0) = (0+3)(0-4)(0-r) = (3)(-4)(-r) = 12r$

Set $12r = 24 \Rightarrow r = 2$.

So $p(x) = (x+3)(x-4)(x-2)$.

Verify in Desmos: graph \texttt{y = (x+3)(x-4)(x-2)} and check that the $x$-intercepts are $-3, 2, 4$ and the $y$-intercept is $(0, 24)$. \checkmark
\end{exercise}

%================================================================
% SECTION 6: EXPONENTIAL AND RADICAL FUNCTIONS
%================================================================

\section{Exponential and Radical Functions with Desmos}

Exponential functions appear frequently on the SAT in the context of growth, decay, compound interest, and population models. Radical (square root, cube root) functions also appear. Desmos handles both beautifully.

\begin{definition}
An \textbf{exponential function} has the form $f(x) = a \cdot b^x$ where:
\begin{itemize}
\item $a$ is the \textbf{initial value} (the $y$-intercept when $x = 0$)
\item $b$ is the \textbf{base} (growth/decay factor)
\item If $b > 1$: \textbf{exponential growth}
\item If $0 < b < 1$: \textbf{exponential decay}
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{radical function} involves a root, such as $f(x) = \sqrt{x}$ (square root) or $f(x) = \sqrt[3]{x}$ (cube root). On the SAT, square root functions are most common.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
An exponential function is like a snowball rolling downhill. At first, it grows slowly, but as it picks up more snow, it grows faster and faster. The base $b$ determines how steep the hill is---a bigger base means a steeper hill and faster growth. Decay ($0 < b < 1$) is like ice cream melting: it melts quickly at first, then slower and slower, but never quite disappears.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Graphing Exponential Functions in Desmos}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Graph Exponential Functions:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the function directly: \texttt{y = 2 * 3\^{}x} for $y = 2 \cdot 3^x$
\item For exponential decay: \texttt{y = 100 * 0.85\^{}x} for $y = 100(0.85)^x$
\item For compound interest: \texttt{y = 1000 * (1 + 0.05/12)\^{}(12x)} for monthly compounding
\item For base $e$: type \texttt{y = e\^{}x} (Desmos recognizes $e$ as Euler's number)
\item Click the $y$-intercept to verify the initial value
\item Adjust the viewing window if the graph grows too fast or too slow
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Reading Growth Rate from Desmos:} If you see an SAT problem like ``A population is modeled by $P(t) = 500(1.03)^t$. What is the annual growth rate?'' you don't even need Desmos---the growth rate is $b - 1 = 1.03 - 1 = 0.03 = 3\%$. But Desmos can help you \textit{verify} by graphing and checking specific values.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Solving Exponential Equations with Desmos}

\begin{theorem}
To solve an exponential equation like $a \cdot b^x = c$, graph both sides as separate functions and find the intersection. This avoids the need for logarithms entirely!
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve $3^x = 50$.}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = 3\^{}x}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 50}
\end{itemize}

Click the intersection. Desmos shows approximately $(3.561, 50)$.

So $x \approx 3.561$.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Algebraically: $x = \log_3(50) = \frac{\ln 50}{\ln 3} \approx 3.561$. Desmos gave us this without needing to know logarithms!
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
\textbf{A car depreciates in value according to $V(t) = 25000(0.82)^t$, where $t$ is years. When will the car be worth \$10,000?}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = 25000 * 0.82\^{}x}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 10000}
\end{itemize}

Click the intersection: approximately $(4.613, 10000)$.

The car will be worth \$10,000 after approximately $4.6$ years.
\end{example}

\subsection{Compound Interest Problems}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{compound interest formula} is:
\[
A = P\left(1 + \frac{r}{n}\right)^{nt}
\]
where:
\begin{itemize}
\item $A$ = final amount
\item $P$ = principal (initial investment)
\item $r$ = annual interest rate (as a decimal)
\item $n$ = number of times compounded per year
\item $t$ = time in years
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Compound Interest in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Substitute known values directly into the formula.
\item Example: \$5,000 at 4\% compounded monthly for $t$ years:

Type: \texttt{y = 5000 * (1 + 0.04/12)\^{}(12x)}
\item To find when the investment doubles, add: \texttt{y = 10000}
\item Click the intersection to find the time.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{You invest \$2,000 at 6\% annual interest, compounded quarterly. How long until the investment reaches \$3,000?}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = 2000 * (1 + 0.06/4)\^{}(4x)}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 3000}
\end{itemize}

Click the intersection: approximately $(6.81, 3000)$.

It takes approximately $6.81$ years, or about $6$ years and $10$ months.
\end{example}

\subsection{Radical Functions in Desmos}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Graphing Radical Functions:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Square root: type \texttt{y = sqrt(x)} for $y = \sqrt{x}$
\item Transformed: \texttt{y = sqrt(x - 3) + 2} for $y = \sqrt{x-3} + 2$
\item Cube root: \texttt{y = nthroot(3
, x)} for $y = \sqrt[3]{x}$
\item Note: Desmos uses \texttt{sqrt()} for square roots and \texttt{nthroot(n, x)} for $n$th roots.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Find the domain of $y = \sqrt{2x - 6}$.}

In Desmos, type: \texttt{y = sqrt(2x - 6)}

Desmos only draws the graph where $2x - 6 \geq 0$, i.e., $x \geq 3$. You can visually see the graph starts at $x = 3$.

The domain is $[3, \infty)$.
\end{example}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 6.1}

Using Desmos, what is the $y$-intercept of $f(x) = 3(2)^x - 5$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-5$
\item $-2$
\item $1$
\item $3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = 3 * 2\^{}x - 5} in Desmos. Click the $y$-intercept (where the curve crosses the $y$-axis). At $x = 0$: $f(0) = 3(2)^0 - 5 = 3(1) - 5 = -2$. The $y$-intercept is $(0, -2)$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 6.2}

The function $f(x) = 500(0.85)^x$ models the value of a phone in dollars after $x$ years. Using Desmos, after how many years is the phone worth \$200?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Approximately 4.2 years
\item Approximately 5.6 years
\item Approximately 6.1 years
\item Approximately 7.3 years
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = 500 * 0.85\^{}x} and \texttt{y = 200}. Click the intersection. Desmos shows approximately $(5.638, 200)$. So the phone is worth \$200 after about $5.6$ years.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 6.3}

What is the horizontal asymptote of $g(x) = 4 \cdot 3^{x-1} + 7$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $y = 0$
\item $y = 4$
\item $y = 7$
\item $y = 12$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = 4 * 3\^{}(x-1) + 7} in Desmos. As $x \to -\infty$, the curve approaches $y = 7$ but never reaches it. The $+7$ at the end shifts the asymptote from $y = 0$ to $y = 7$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 6.4}

You invest \$1,000 at 5\% annual interest compounded monthly. Using Desmos, approximately how long until your investment doubles?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item 10.2 years
\item 12.5 years
\item 13.9 years
\item 14.2 years
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = 1000 * (1 + 0.05/12)\^{}(12x)} and \texttt{y = 2000}. Click the intersection. Desmos shows approximately $(13.86, 2000)$, so about $13.9$ years.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 6.5}

What is the domain of $f(x) = \sqrt{8 - 2x}$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x \geq 4$
\item $x \leq 4$
\item $x \geq -4$
\item All real numbers
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = sqrt(8 - 2x)} in Desmos. The graph only appears for $x \leq 4$ because we need $8 - 2x \geq 0$, which gives $x \leq 4$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 6.6}

Using Desmos, how many real solutions does $2^x = x + 3$ have?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item 0
\item 1
\item 2
\item 3
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = 2\^{}x} and \texttt{y = x + 3}. Count the intersections. The exponential curve and the line cross at two points, so there are 2 real solutions.
\end{highlightbox}

\subsection{Section 6 Summary}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways for Exponential, Radical, and Advanced Functions:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Graph exponential functions directly: \texttt{y = a * b\^{}x + k}
\item Solve exponential equations by graphing both sides and finding intersections
\item Compound interest problems: substitute into the formula and graph
\item Radical function domains are visible instantly---Desmos only draws where the function is defined
\item For ``how many solutions'' questions, graph and count intersections
\item The horizontal asymptote is visible as the line the curve approaches but never reaches
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 7: TRIGONOMETRY AND CIRCLE PROBLEMS
%================================================================

\section{Trigonometry and Circle Problems with Desmos}

Trigonometry appears on the SAT in two main contexts: right triangle trigonometry (SOHCAHTOA) and unit circle/trig function graphs. Desmos is extraordinarily powerful for both.

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Trigonometric functions} relate angles to ratios of sides in right triangles. The three primary functions are:
\begin{itemize}
\item $\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{opposite}}{\text{hypotenuse}}$
\item $\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{adjacent}}{\text{hypotenuse}}$
\item $\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{opposite}}{\text{adjacent}} = \frac{\sin(\theta)}{\cos(\theta)}$
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Think of trig functions like a measuring tape for triangles. If you know an angle and one side, the trig functions let you measure all the other sides without actually building the triangle. Desmos is like having a super-powered measuring tape that does all the calculations instantly.
\end{analogy}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{CRITICAL: Desmos Uses Radians by Default!}

On the Digital SAT's built-in Desmos, the calculator is set to \textbf{radians} by default. If the SAT problem gives you degrees, you \textbf{must} either:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Switch Desmos to degree mode (click the wrench/settings icon and change to ``Degrees''), OR
\item Convert degrees to radians: multiply by $\frac{\pi}{180}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Recommendation:} Before starting any trig problem, check whether the problem uses degrees or radians, and set Desmos accordingly. Most SAT trig problems use \textbf{degrees} for right triangle problems and \textbf{radians} for trig function/graph problems.
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{Right Triangle Trigonometry in Desmos}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Solving Right Triangle Problems:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Finding a missing side:} If you know an angle and one side, set up the trig equation and type it into Desmos.
\begin{itemize}
\item Example: In a right triangle with angle $35°$ and hypotenuse $10$, the opposite side is $10\sin(35°)$.
\item In Desmos (degree mode): type \texttt{10 * sin(35)} and read the answer: $\approx 5.736$.
\end{itemize}
\item \textbf{Finding a missing angle:} Use inverse trig functions.
\begin{itemize}
\item If $\sin(\theta) = 0.6$, then $\theta = \sin^{-1}(0.6)$.
\item In Desmos: type \texttt{arcsin(0.6)} (or \texttt{sin\^{}\{-1\}(0.6)}) to get $\approx 36.87°$.
\end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common Mistake:} Students forget to switch to degree mode and get bizarre answers. If you type \texttt{sin(30)} in radian mode, you get $\sin(30 \text{ rad}) \approx -0.988$, NOT $\sin(30°) = 0.5$. Always check your mode!
\end{warningbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{A ladder leans against a wall, making a $65°$ angle with the ground. If the ladder is 12 feet long, how high up the wall does it reach?}

Set Desmos to degree mode. The height is the side opposite the $65°$ angle:
\[
h = 12\sin(65°)
\]
Type: \texttt{12 * sin(65)}

Desmos gives: $\approx 10.876$

The ladder reaches approximately $10.9$ feet up the wall.
\end{example}

\subsection{Solving Trig Equations with Desmos}

\begin{theorem}
To solve a trigonometric equation like $\sin(x) = k$ or $2\cos(x) + 1 = 0$, graph both sides and find the intersections. This avoids the complexity of reference angles and quadrant analysis entirely.
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Find all solutions to $2\sin(x) - 1 = 0$ in the interval $[0, 2\pi]$.}

Rearrange: $\sin(x) = \frac{1}{2}$

In Desmos (radian mode):
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = sin(x)}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 1/2}
\end{itemize}

Restrict the view to $[0, 2\pi]$ by adjusting the $x$-axis. Click the two intersections:
\begin{itemize}
\item $x = \frac{\pi}{6} \approx 0.524$
\item $x = \frac{5\pi}{6} \approx 2.618$
\end{itemize}

The solutions are $x = \frac{\pi}{6}$ and $x = \frac{5\pi}{6}$.
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
Without Desmos, you'd need to know that $\sin(x) = \frac{1}{2}$ at $30°$ and $150°$ (or $\frac{\pi}{6}$ and $\frac{5\pi}{6}$), recall which quadrants sine is positive, and apply reference angles. Desmos skips all of that---just graph and click.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Graphing Trig Functions and Identifying Features}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{sinusoidal function} has the general form:
\[
y = A\sin(Bx + C) + D \quad \text{or} \quad y = A\cos(Bx + C) + D
\]
where:
\begin{itemize}
\item $|A|$ = amplitude (height from midline to peak)
\item $\frac{2\pi}{|B|}$ = period (horizontal length of one complete cycle)
\item $-\frac{C}{B}$ = phase shift (horizontal shift)
\item $D$ = vertical shift (midline)
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Reading Trig Graph Features from Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the trig function into Desmos.
\item \textbf{Amplitude:} Click the maximum and minimum points. Amplitude = $\frac{\text{max} - \text{min}}{2}$.
\item \textbf{Period:} Click two consecutive maximum points. Period = difference in their $x$-values.
\item \textbf{Midline:} Midline $= \frac{\text{max} + \text{min}}{2}$, or simply read $D$ from the equation.
\item \textbf{Phase shift:} Compare where the first peak/zero occurs relative to the parent function.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Find the amplitude, period, and midline of $y = 3\sin(2x) + 1$.}

In Desmos, type: \texttt{y = 3sin(2x) + 1}

Click the maximum: $(0.785, 4)$ --- this is $(\frac{\pi}{4}, 4)$.

Click the minimum: $(2.356, -2)$ --- this is $(\frac{3\pi}{4}, -2)$.

\begin{itemize}
\item Amplitude $= \frac{4 - (-2)}{2} = \frac{6}{2} = 3$ \checkmark (matches $|A| = 3$)
\item Midline $= \frac{4 + (-2)}{2} = \frac{2}{2} = 1$, so $y = 1$ \checkmark (matches $D = 1$)
\item Period: Click two consecutive maxima. Next max at $x = \frac{\pi}{4} + \pi = \frac{5\pi}{4} \approx 3.927$. Period $= \frac{5\pi}{4} - \frac{\pi}{4} = \pi \approx 3.14$ \checkmark (matches $\frac{2\pi}{|B|} = \frac{2\pi}{2} = \pi$)
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\subsection{The Complementary Angle Identity}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Complementary Angle Identity (SAT Favorite):}
\[
\sin(x) = \cos(90° - x) \quad \text{and} \quad \cos(x) = \sin(90° - x)
\]
In radians: $\sin(x) = \cos\!\left(\frac{\pi}{2} - x\right)$.

This means: if $\sin(A) = \cos(B)$, then $A + B = 90°$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{If $\sin(3x + 10)° = \cos(2x - 5)°$, find $x$.}

Since $\sin(\theta) = \cos(90° - \theta)$, we need
$(3x + 10) + (2x - 5) = 90$:
\[
5x + 5 = 90 \implies 5x = 85 \implies x = 17
\]

\textbf{Desmos Verification:} Type \texttt{y = sin(3*17 + 10)} and \texttt{y = cos(2*17 - 5)}. Both should give the same value. $\sin(61°) = \cos(29°) \approx 0.8746$. \checkmark
\end{example}

\subsection{Solving Trig Equations with Desmos}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Solving Trig Equations Graphically:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Rearrange the equation so one side equals a constant (or graph both sides separately).
\item Example: Solve $2\sin(x) + 1 = 0$ for $0 \leq x \leq 2\pi$.
\item Graph \texttt{y = 2sin(x) + 1} and \texttt{y = 0} (the $x$-axis).
\item Click the intersections within the given interval.
\item Read the $x$-coordinates---these are your solutions.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{How many solutions does $\cos(3x) = 0.5$ have on the interval $[0, 2\pi]$?}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = cos(3x)}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 0.5}
\end{itemize}

Make sure Desmos is in radian mode. Zoom to the window $0 \leq x \leq 2\pi$ (approximately $0$ to $6.28$).

Count the intersections: there are \textbf{6} intersection points.

The equation has 6 solutions on $[0, 2\pi]$.
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Radian vs.\ Degree Mode:} The SAT typically uses radians for trig. Make sure your Desmos calculator is set to radians (click the wrench icon in the top right and check). If a problem uses degrees, switch to degree mode. This is the \#1 trig mistake on the SAT!
\end{insightbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common Trig Pitfall:} When Desmos is in degree mode and the problem uses radians (or vice versa), your answers will be completely wrong. Always check the mode \textit{before} graphing trig functions. On the SAT, the digital Desmos defaults to radians.
\end{warningbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 7.1}

What is the period of $y = 4\sin\!\left(\frac{\pi}{3}x\right) - 2$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $\frac{\pi}{3}$
\item $3$
\item $6$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph in Desmos: \texttt{y = 4sin((pi/3)x) - 2}. Click two consecutive maximums. The distance between them is $6$. Alternatively, period $= \frac{2\pi}{|B|} = \frac{2\pi}{\pi/3} = 6$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 7.2}

If $\sin(5x - 15)° = \cos(25)°$, what is the value of $x$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $10$
\item $13$
\item $16$
\item $20$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Using the complementary identity: $(5x - 15) + 25 = 90$. So $5x + 10 = 90 \Rightarrow 5x = 80 \Rightarrow x = 16$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 7.3}

What is the amplitude of $y = -7\cos(x) + 3$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-7$
\item $3$
\item $7$
