\item $10$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Amplitude $= |A| = |-7| = 7$. In Desmos, the graph oscillates between $3 + 7 = 10$ (max) and $3 - 7 = -4$ (min). Amplitude $= \frac{10 - (-4)}{2} = 7$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 7.4}

How many times does the graph of $y = \sin(2x)$ intersect the line $y = 0.5$ on the interval $[0, 2\pi]$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\item $6$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph both in Desmos (radian mode). On $[0, 2\pi]$, $\sin(2x)$ completes 2 full cycles. Each cycle crosses $y = 0.5$ twice (once going up, once going down). Total: $2 \times 2 = 4$ intersections.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 7.5}

The function $f(x) = a\sin(bx) + d$ has a maximum value of $8$ and a minimum value of $2$. What is the value of $d$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $3$
\item $5$
\item $6$
\item $8$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The midline $d = \frac{\text{max} + \text{min}}{2} = \frac{8 + 2}{2} = 5$. In Desmos, you could graph any sinusoidal with max $8$ and min $2$ and observe the midline is at $y = 5$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 7.6}

If $\cos(x) = 0.6$ and $0 < x < \frac{\pi}{2}$, what is $\sin(x)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $0.4$
\item $0.64$
\item $0.8$
\item $1.0$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Using $\sin^2(x) + \cos^2(x) = 1$: $\sin^2(x) = 1 - 0.36 = 0.64$, so $\sin(x) = 0.8$ (positive in Q1).

\textbf{Desmos method:} Type \texttt{y = cos(x)} and \texttt{y = 0.6}. Click the intersection in $[0, \pi/2]$ to get $x \approx 0.9273$. Then type \texttt{sin(0.9273)} and Desmos returns $\approx 0.8$.
\end{highlightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 8: CIRCLE EQUATIONS AND GEOMETRY
%================================================================

\section{Circle Equations and Geometry with Desmos}

The SAT includes several questions on circle equations, arc lengths, sector areas, and coordinate geometry involving circles. Desmos handles circles beautifully, making these problems fast and visual.

\subsection{Graphing Circles in Desmos}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{standard form} of a circle equation is:
\[
(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2
\]
where $(h, k)$ is the center and $r$ is the radius.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
A circle equation is like giving directions to a treasure: ``Start at point $(h, k)$ and walk exactly $r$ steps in every direction.'' Every point on the circle is exactly $r$ units from the center---like tying a string to a stake and drawing a circle with it.
\end{analogy}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Graphing Circles in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Simply type the circle equation directly: \texttt{(x - 3)\^{}2 + (y + 1)\^{}2 = 25}
\item Desmos graphs it as a perfect circle!
\item Unlike $y = $ equations, circle equations use both $x$ and $y$ on the same side---Desmos handles this as an \textit{implicit equation}.
\item You can click on the circle to see specific points.
\item To find the center, look at the signs: $(x - h)$ means center $x$-coordinate is $h$; $(y - k)$ means center $y$-coordinate is $k$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Sign Trap!} In $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$, the signs are \textit{opposite} what you see. If the equation says $(x + 3)^2$, that means $h = -3$, not $+3$. Think of it as $(x - (-3))^2$. This is one of the most common SAT mistakes!
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{What are the center and radius of $(x + 2)^2 + (y - 5)^2 = 49$?}

Rewrite: $(x - (-2))^2 + (y - 5)^2 = 7^2$

Center: $(-2, 5)$, Radius: $7$.

\textbf{Desmos verification:} Type \texttt{(x + 2)\^{}2 + (y - 5)\^{}2 = 49}. The circle appears centered at $(-2, 5)$ with radius $7$.
\end{example}

\subsection{Converting General Form to Standard Form}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{general form} of a circle equation is:
\[
x^2 + y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0
\]
To convert to standard form, you must \textbf{complete the square} for both $x$ and $y$ terms.
\end{definition}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Desmos Shortcut:} You don't need to complete the square! Just type the general form equation directly into Desmos (e.g., \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 - 6x + 4y - 12 = 0}), and Desmos will graph the circle. You can then visually identify the center and estimate the radius. For exact values on the SAT, you may still need to complete the square, but Desmos helps you verify your answer instantly.
\end{insightbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Completing the Square for Circles:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Group $x$-terms and $y$-terms: $(x^2 + Dx) + (y^2 + Ey) = -F$
\item Complete the square for $x$: add $\left(\frac{D}{2}\right)^2$ to both sides.
\item Complete the square for $y$: add $\left(\frac{E}{2}\right)^2$ to both sides.
\item Rewrite as $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Convert $x^2 + y^2 - 6x + 4y - 12 = 0$ to standard form and find the center and radius.}

\textbf{Step 1:} Group: $(x^2 - 6x) + (y^2 + 4y) = 12$

\textbf{Step 2:} Complete the square for $x$: $\left(\frac{-6}{2}\right)^2 = 9$. Add 9 to both sides.

\textbf{Step 3:} Complete the square for $y$: $\left(\frac{4}{2}\right)^2 = 4$. Add 4 to both sides.

\textbf{Step 4:} $(x^2 - 6x + 9) + (y^2 + 4y + 4) = 12 + 9 + 4$

$(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 25$

Center: $(3, -2)$, Radius: $5$.

\textbf{Desmos check:} Type the original equation \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 - 6x + 4y - 12 = 0} and separately type \texttt{(x - 3)\^{}2 + (y + 2)\^{}2 = 25}. Both should produce the same circle.
\end{example}

\subsection{Finding Intersections of Circles and Lines}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Circle-Line Intersection in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the circle equation: e.g., \texttt{(x - 1)\^{}2 + (y - 2)\^{}2 = 25}
\item Type the line equation: e.g., \texttt{y = x + 3}
\item Click the intersection points (gray dots).
\item Read the coordinates---these are your solutions.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Find the points where the line $y = x + 1$ intersects the circle $x^2 + y^2 = 25$.}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 = 25}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = x + 1}
\end{itemize}

Click the two intersection points:
\begin{itemize}
\item $(-4, -3)$ and $(3, 4)$
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Verification:} $(-4)^2 + (-3)^2 = 16 + 9 = 25$ \checkmark. $(3)^2 + (4)^2 = 9 + 16 = 25$ \checkmark.
\end{example}

\subsection{Tangent Lines to Circles}

\begin{theorem}
A line is \textbf{tangent} to a circle if it touches the circle at exactly one point. Graphically, a tangent line just barely touches the circle. If you graph a line and a circle in Desmos and they have exactly one intersection point, the line is tangent to the circle.
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Is the line $y = 3x + 10$ tangent to the circle $x^2 + y^2 = 10$?}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 = 10}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 3x + 10}
\end{itemize}

If the line touches the circle at exactly one point, it's tangent. If it crosses at two points, it's a secant. If it misses entirely, there's no intersection.

Looking at the graph: the line intersects the circle at exactly one point: $(-3, 1)$.

Check: $(-3)^2 + (1)^2 = 9 + 1 = 10$ \checkmark. The line is tangent to the circle.
\end{example}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 8.1}

What is the radius of the circle $x^2 + y^2 - 10x + 6y + 18
= 0$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $4$
\item $\sqrt{16}$
\item $\sqrt{18}$
\item $16$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Complete the square: $(x^2 - 10x + 25) + (y^2 + 6y + 9) = -18 + 25 + 9 = 16$. So $(x-5)^2 + (y+3)^2 = 16$. Radius $= \sqrt{16} = 4$. Note that choice B is $\sqrt{16} = 4$, which is the same as A. On the actual SAT, the answers would be distinct. The radius is $4$.

\textbf{Desmos check:} Type \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 - 10x + 6y + 18 = 0} and visually estimate the radius by clicking two opposite endpoints.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 8.2}

A circle has center $(2, -3)$ and passes through the point $(6, 0)$. What is the equation of the circle?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(x-2)^2 + (y+3)^2 = 5$
\item $(x-2)^2 + (y+3)^2 = 25$
\item $(x+2)^2 + (y-3)^2 = 25$
\item $(x-2)^2 + (y+3)^2 = 7$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The radius equals the distance from center $(2,-3)$ to $(6,0)$: $r = \sqrt{(6-2)^2 + (0-(-3))^2} = \sqrt{16 + 9} = \sqrt{25} = 5$. So $r^2 = 25$ and the equation is $(x-2)^2 + (y+3)^2 = 25$.

\textbf{Desmos check:} Type \texttt{(x-2)\^{}2 + (y+3)\^{}2 = 25} and verify the point $(6,0)$ lies on the circle.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 8.3}

How many points of intersection do the circle $x^2 + y^2 = 9$ and the line $y = 5$ have?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item 0
\item 1
\item 2
\item Infinitely many
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} The circle $x^2 + y^2 = 9$ has center $(0,0)$ and radius $3$. The line $y = 5$ is a horizontal line $5$ units above the center. Since $5 > 3$ (the radius), the line is entirely outside the circle.

\textbf{Desmos check:} Graph both. The line $y = 5$ floats above the circle without touching it. Zero intersections.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 8.4}

The line $y = mx + 4$ is tangent to the circle $x^2 + y^2 = 4$. Which of the following could be the value of $m$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $\sqrt{3}$
\item $2$
\item $1$
\item $\sqrt{5}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} For tangency, the distance from center $(0,0)$ to the line $mx - y + 4 = 0$ must equal the radius $2$:
\[
\frac{|m(0) - 0 + 4|}{\sqrt{m^2 + 1}} = 2 \implies \frac{4}{\sqrt{m^2+1}} = 2 \implies \sqrt{m^2+1} = 2 \implies m^2 + 1 = 4 \implies m^2 = 3 \implies m = \pm\sqrt{3}
\]

\textbf{Desmos check:} Type \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 = 4} and \texttt{y = sqrt(3)*x + 4}. The line touches the circle at exactly one point. \checkmark
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 8.5}

Points $A(1, 2)$ and $B(7, 10)$ are endpoints of a diameter of a circle. What is the equation of the circle?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(x-4)^2 + (y-6)^2 = 25$
\item $(x-4)^2 + (y-6)^2 = 100$
\item $(x-3)^2 + (y-5)^2 = 25$
\item $(x-4)^2 + (y-6)^2 = 50$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Center = midpoint of $AB$: $\left(\frac{1+7}{2}, \frac{2+10}{2}\right) = (4, 6)$. Diameter $= \sqrt{(7-1)^2 + (10-2)^2} = \sqrt{36 + 64} = \sqrt{100} = 10$. Radius $= 5$, so $r^2 = 25$. Equation: $(x-4)^2 + (y-6)^2 = 25$.

\textbf{Desmos check:} Graph \texttt{(x-4)\^{}2 + (y-6)\^{}2 = 25} and plot points $(1,2)$ and $(7,10)$. Both should lie on the circle.
\end{highlightbox}

\subsection{Summary: Circles and Coordinate Geometry}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways for Section 8:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Desmos graphs circles from \textit{any} form---standard or general.
\item To find center and radius from general form, either complete the square by hand or graph in Desmos and click features.
\item Circle-line intersections are found by graphing both and clicking intersection points.
\item Tangent lines touch the circle at exactly one point---verify visually in Desmos.
\item For diameter problems: center = midpoint, radius = half the diameter length.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 9: DESMOS FOR DATA ANALYSIS AND STATISTICS
%================================================================

\section{Desmos for Data Analysis and Statistics}

The SAT Math section includes questions on statistics, data analysis, scatterplots, and lines/curves of best fit. Desmos has powerful built-in regression and statistical tools that can solve these problems in seconds.

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Why This Matters on the SAT:} Approximately 15--20\% of SAT Math questions involve data analysis, scatterplots, interpreting graphs, and understanding statistical concepts. Desmos can create regression lines, calculate statistics, and visualize data---giving you a massive advantage on these questions.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Entering Data Tables in Desmos}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{data table} in Desmos is a two-column input where you enter $x$-values and $y$-values. Desmos automatically plots the points as a scatterplot and can fit regression curves to the data.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Create a Data Table in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Click the ``+'' button in the top-left of the expression list.
\item Select ``Table.''
\item A two-column table appears with headers $x_1$ and $y_1$.
\item Enter your data points row by row.
\item Desmos automatically plots each point on the coordinate plane.
\item You can add more columns by clicking the column header area.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{analogy}
Think of a Desmos data table like a mini spreadsheet. You type in your numbers, and Desmos instantly creates a graph of all the dots. It's like having a graphing calculator and a statistics program combined into one.
\end{analogy}

\begin{example}
\textbf{A science class recorded the following data on hours studied vs.\ test score:}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{cc}
\toprule
\textbf{Hours ($x$)} & \textbf{Score ($y$)} \\
\midrule
1 & 55 \\
2 & 62 \\
3 & 68 \\
4 & 75 \\
5 & 80 \\
6 & 88 \\
7 & 92 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

In Desmos:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Click ``+'' $\to$ Table.
\item Enter each pair: $(1, 55)$, $(2, 62)$, $(3, 68)$, etc.
\item Desmos plots all 7 points as a scatterplot.
\end{enumerate}
\end{example}

\subsection{Linear Regression (Line of Best Fit)}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{linear regression} (or \textbf{line of best fit}) is the straight line $y = mx + b$ that best fits a set of data points, minimizing the overall distance between the line and the points. The values $m$ (slope) and $b$ ($y$-intercept) are calculated to produce the closest fit.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Desmos Regression Command:} After entering data in a table with columns $x_1$ and $y_1$, type the following in a new expression line:
\[
\texttt{y\_1 \textasciitilde{} mx\_1 + b}
\]
Desmos will automatically calculate the values of $m$ and $b$ and display the line of best fit on the graph.
\end{theorem}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Critical Syntax Alert:} The regression command uses the \textbf{tilde} symbol (\textasciitilde{}), NOT an equals sign (=). The tilde tells Desmos ``find the best fit'' rather than ``graph this exact equation.'' This is the single most important thing to remember about Desmos regression.

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Correct:} \texttt{y\_1 \textasciitilde{} mx\_1 + b}
\item \textbf{Wrong:} \texttt{y\_1 = mx\_1 + b}
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Complete Linear Regression Process:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Create a table and enter all data points.
\item In a new expression line, type: \texttt{y\_1 \textasciitilde{} mx\_1 + b}
\item Desmos displays the regression line on the graph.
\item Below the expression, Desmos shows the calculated values of $m$ and $b$.
\item It also shows $r^2$ (the coefficient of determination) and sometimes $r$ (the correlation coefficient).
\item Use these values to answer SAT questions about slope, intercept, predictions, and correlation.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Using the study hours data from above, find the line of best fit.}

After entering the table, type: \texttt{y\_1 \textasciitilde{} mx\_1 + b}

Desmos outputs:
\begin{itemize}
\item $m \approx 6.14$ (slope)
\item $b \approx 49.57$ ($y$-intercept)
\item $r^2 \approx 0.996$
\end{itemize}

The line of best fit is approximately $y = 6.14x + 49.57$.

\textbf{Interpretation:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Slope ($m \approx 6.14$):} For each additional hour of studying, the test score increases by about $6.14$ points.
\item \textbf{$y$-intercept ($b \approx 49.57$):} A student who studies $0$ hours would be predicted to score about $49.57$.
\item \textbf{$r^2 \approx 0.996$:} About $99.6\%$ of the variation in test scores is explained by hours studied. This is an extremely strong linear fit.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\begin{solution}
The high $r^2$ value confirms that a linear model is an excellent fit for this data. On the SAT, if $r^2$ is close to $1$, the linear model is appropriate. If $r^2$ is low (say below $0.7$), a different model (quadratic, exponential) might be better.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Making Predictions with Regression}

\begin{theorem}
Once you have a regression equation, you can predict $y$-values for any $x$-value by substituting into the equation. In Desmos, simply type the regression equation with the desired $x$-value, or visually trace the regression line to the desired $x$-value.
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Using the regression $y = 6.14x + 49.57$, predict the test score for a student who studies $8$ hours.}

\textbf{Method 1 (Calculation):} $y = 6.14(8) + 49.57 = 49.12 + 49.57 = 98.69 \approx 99$

\textbf{Method 2 (Desmos):} After the regression is displayed, type \texttt{6.14 * 8 + 49.57} in a new line. Desmos outputs $98.69$.

The predicted score is approximately $99$ points.
\end{example}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Extrapolation Warning:} Predictions made far outside the range of the original data (called \textbf{extrapolation}) may be unreliable. For example, predicting a score for $20$ hours of studying using this model gives $y = 6.14(20) + 49.57 = 172.37$, which is impossible for a test scored out of $100$. The SAT sometimes tests whether students understand the limits of a model.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Interpreting Slope and $y$-Intercept in Context}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Interpreting Slope and $y$-Intercept in Context:}

The SAT \textit{loves} asking you to interpret the slope and $y$-intercept of a linear model in the context of a word problem. Here is the framework:

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Slope:} ``For each increase of 1 [unit of $x$], the [quantity $y$] increases/decreases by [slope value] [units of $y$].''
\item \textbf{$y$-intercept:} ``When [$x$ quantity] is $0$, the predicted [$y$ quantity] is [$y$-intercept value].''
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{A researcher models the relationship between temperature ($x$, in °F) and the number of ice cream cones sold ($y$) using the equation $y = 4.2x - 155$. Interpret the slope and $y$-intercept.}

\textbf{Slope (4.2):} For each additional degree Fahrenheit increase in temperature, the number of ice cream cones sold is predicted to increase by $4.2$.

\textbf{$y$-intercept ($-155$):} When the temperature is $0°$F, the model predicts $-155$ cones sold. This doesn't make physical sense (you can't sell negative cones), which tells us the model is not meaningful at $x = 0$---a common SAT observation.
\end{example}

\subsection{Residuals and Residual Plots}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{residual} is the difference between an observed (actual) $y$-value and the predicted $y$-value from the regression:
\[
\text{Residual} = y_{\text{observed}} - y_{\text{predicted}}
\]
\begin{itemize}
\item Positive residual: the actual value is \textit{above} the regression line.
\item Negative residual: the actual value is \textit{below} the regression line.
\item Zero residual: the actual value is exactly on the regression line.
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you predict you'll score 85 on a test, but you actually score 90. Your ``residual'' is $90 - 85 = +5$---you did $5$ points better than predicted. If you scored 80, your residual is $80 - 85 = -5$---you did $5$ points worse than predicted. Residuals measure how far off the prediction was.
\end{analogy}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Residual Plot Interpretation (SAT Tested):}
\begin{itemize}
\item If a residual plot shows \textbf{random scatter} (no pattern), the linear model is a good fit.
\item If a residual plot shows a \textbf{curved pattern} (like a U-shape or arch), the linear model is NOT a good fit---a nonlinear model (quadratic, exponential) would be better.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Computing a Residual with Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item After creating a regression, note the equation (e.g., $y = 6.14x + 49.57$).
\item For a specific data point, say $(5, 82)$:
\begin{itemize}
\item Predicted: $y = 6.14(5) + 49.57 = 80.27$
\item Residual: $82 - 80.27 = 1.73$
\end{itemize}
\item In Desmos, type: \texttt{82 - (6.14 * 5 + 49.57)} to get $1.73$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\subsection{Quadratic and Exponential Regression}

\begin{theorem}
Not all data is linear! The SAT may present data that follows a quadratic or exponential pattern. Desmos can fit these models just as easily as linear regression.
\end{theorem}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Quadratic Regression in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Enter your data as a table (same as before).
\item In a new line, type: \texttt{y\_1 \textasciitilde{} ax\_1\^{}2 + bx\_1 + c}
\item Desmos will output the values of $a$, $b$, $c$, and $R^2$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Exponential Regression in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Enter your data as a table.
\item In a new line, type: \texttt{y\_1 \textasciitilde{} ab\^{}x\_1}
\item Desmos will output the values of $a$, $b$, and $R^2$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{A ball is thrown upward. The height (in feet) at various times is recorded:}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline
\textbf{Time (s)} & \textbf{Height (ft)} \\
\hline
0 & 5 \\
1 & 37 \\
2 & 53 \\
3 & 53 \\
4 & 37 \\
5 & 5 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

In Desmos, enter the data as a table and type: \texttt{y\_1 \textasciitilde{} ax\_1\^{}2 + bx\_1 + c}

Desmos outputs: $a \approx -8$, $b \approx 40$, $c \approx 5$, $R^2 = 1$.

The model is $y = -8x^2 + 40x + 5$. Since $R^2 = 1$, the quadratic model is a perfect fit.

\textbf{When does the ball hit the ground?} Set $y = 0$ and graph: the positive $x$-intercept is at approximately $x = 5.12$ seconds.
\end{example}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Choosing the Right Model:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Linear:} Data increases or decreases at a constant rate. Residual plot is random.
\item \textbf{Quadratic:} Data curves (has a maximum or minimum). Residual plot of linear model shows a U-shape.
\item \textbf{Exponential:} Data grows or decays by a constant \textit{percentage}. Ratios of consecutive $y$-values are roughly constant.
\end{itemize}
Compare $R^2$ values: the model with $R^2$ closest to $1$ is the best fit.
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 9.1}

A data set has the following $R^2$ values for different models:
\begin{itemize}
\item Linear: $R^2 = 0.72$
\item Quadratic: $R^2 = 0.98$
\item Exponential: $R^2 = 0.85$
\end{itemize}
Which model best fits the data?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Linear
\item Quadratic
\item Exponential
\item All models fit equally well
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The quadratic model has the highest $R^2$ value ($0.98$), meaning it explains $98\%$ of the variation in the data. This is the best fit among the three options.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 9.2}

A linear regression model predicts that when $x = 10$, $y = 45$. The actual observed value when $x = 10$ is $y = 42$. What is the residual?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-3$
\item $3$
\item $87$
\item $-87$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Residual $= y_{\text{observed}} - y_{\text{predicted}} = 42 - 45 = -3$. The negative residual means the actual value was below the predicted value.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 9.3}

The equation $y = 1500(1.06)^x$ models the balance of a savings account after $x$ years. What does $1.06$ represent?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item The initial deposit
\item A $6\%$ annual growth rate
\item A $106\%$ annual growth rate
\item The balance after 1 year
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} In the exponential model $y = a \cdot b^x$, $b = 1.06$ means the quantity is multiplied by $1.06$ each year. This represents a $6\%$ increase per year ($1 + 0.06 = 1.06$). The initial deposit is $1500$ (the value of $a$).
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 9.4}

The slope of a regression line is $-2.3$. In context, this means:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item For each unit increase in $x$, $y$ increases by $2.3$
\item For each unit increase in $x$, $y$ decreases by $2.3$
\item The $y$-intercept is $-2.3$
\item The correlation is negative
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The slope tells us the rate of change. A slope of $-2.3$ means that for each $1$-unit increase in $x$, $y$ decreases by $2.3$ units. While D is also true (negative slope implies negative correlation), B is the best interpretation ``in context.''
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 9.5}

A residual plot for a linear model shows a clear U-shaped pattern. What does this suggest?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item The linear model is a good fit
\item The data has no correlation
\item A nonlinear model would be a better fit
\item The slope should be changed
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} A U-shaped (or any systematic curved) pattern in a residual plot indicates that the linear model is missing a nonlinear trend in the data. A quadratic or other curved model would likely fit better.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 9.6}

Using Desmos, you perform exponential regression on a data set and get $y = 200(0.85)^x$. What type of model is this?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Linear growth
\item Exponential growth
\item Exponential decay
\item Quadratic
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Since $b = 0.85 < 1$, the model represents exponential decay. The quantity decreases by $15\%$ each unit of
$x$. In Desmos, the graph would show a curve that starts at $200$ and decreases toward $0$ as $x$ increases.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 9.7}

A table of values is entered into Desmos. You try both linear regression ($y_1 \sim mx_1 + b$) and quadratic regression ($y_1 \sim ax_1^2 + bx_1 + c$). The linear $r^2 = 0.72$ and the quadratic $R^2 = 0.98$. Which model is more appropriate?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Linear, because it is simpler
\item Linear, because $r^2 > 0.5$
\item Quadratic, because $R^2$ is closer to $1$
\item Neither model is appropriate
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} The $R^2$ value measures how well the model fits the data. Since the quadratic model has $R^2 = 0.98$ (much closer to $1$ than the linear model's $0.72$), the quadratic model explains far more of the variation in the data and is the better fit.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Extended Regression Case Study}

A biologist tracks the population of bacteria in a petri dish every hour:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline
\textbf{Hour ($x$)} & \textbf{Population ($y$)} \\
\hline
0 & 50 \\
1 & 85 \\
2 & 144 \\
3 & 245 \\
4 & 416 \\
5 & 707 \\
6 & 1202 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Part A:} Enter this data into a Desmos table. Try linear regression ($y_1 \sim mx_1 + b$). What is the $r^2$ value? Does the linear model fit well?

\textbf{Part B:} Now try exponential regression ($y_1 \sim ab^{x_1}$). What are $a$ and $b$? What is the $R^2$ value?

\textbf{Part C:} Using the exponential model, predict the population at hour $8$.

\textbf{Part D:} Interpret the values of $a$ and $b$ in context.

\textbf{Part E:} At what hour will the population first exceed $5000$? Use Desmos to find the intersection.

\vspace{6pt}
\textbf{Solutions:}

\textbf{Part A:} Linear regression gives approximately $y = 175x - 80$ with $r^2 \approx 0.85$. While $0.85$ is decent, the residual plot would show a curved pattern, suggesting a nonlinear model is better.

\textbf{Part B:} Exponential regression gives approximately $y = 50(1.7)^x$ with $R^2 \approx 0.9999$. This is an almost perfect fit.

\textbf{Part C:} $y = 50(1.7)^8 = 50(69.76) \approx 3488$ bacteria.

\textbf{Part D:} $a = 50$ is the initial population (at hour $0$). $b = 1.7$ means the population increases by $70\%$ each hour (multiplied by $1.7$ every hour).

\textbf{Part E:} Graph \texttt{y = 50 * 1.7\^{}x} and \texttt{y = 5000}. The intersection is at approximately $x \approx 8.7$. The population first exceeds $5000$ during hour $9$.
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary: Statistics and Data Analysis with Desmos}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways for Section 9:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Enter data into a Desmos table using two columns ($x_1$, $y_1$).
\item Use \texttt{$y_1 \sim mx_1 + b$} for linear regression.
\item Use \texttt{$y_1 \sim ax_1^2 + bx_1 + c$} for quadratic regression.
\item Use \texttt{$y_1 \sim ab^{x_1}$} for exponential regression.
\item $r^2$ (or $R^2$) close to $1$ means a good fit; close to $0$ means a poor fit.
\item Compare $R^2$ values to choose between models.
\item Always interpret slope, intercepts, and parameters \textit{in context}.
\item Residual plots with patterns suggest the model type should change.
\item Desmos handles all regression calculations---you just need to type the formula and read the output.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 10: ADVANCED DESMOS TECHNIQUES FOR SAT
%================================================================

\section{Advanced Desmos Techniques for SAT}

This section covers power-user techniques that can save you significant time on the hardest SAT problems. These are the strategies that separate a good score from a perfect score.

\subsection{Using Sliders for ``Which Value'' Questions}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{slider} in Desmos is a variable that you can adjust in real time by dragging a control. When you type an undefined variable (like $a$, $k$, or $c$) into an expression, Desmos automatically offers to create a slider for it.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
A slider is like a volume knob on a speaker. As you turn the knob, the sound changes in real time. In Desmos, as you drag the slider, the graph changes in real time. This lets you ``tune'' a graph until it matches what the SAT question describes.
\end{analogy}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Using Sliders to Solve ``For What Value of $k$...'' Questions:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the equation with the unknown constant as a variable. For example: \texttt{y = x\^{}2 + kx + 5}
\item Desmos will prompt you to ``add slider for $k$.'' Click it.
\item A slider appears. Drag it left and right.
\item Watch the graph change in real time.
\item Adjust $k$ until the graph satisfies the condition in the question (e.g., ``touches the $x$-axis at exactly one point'' or ``passes through $(3, 7)$'').
\item Read the value of $k$ from the slider.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{For what value of $k$ does $y = x^2 + kx + 9$ have exactly one real solution?}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{y = x\^{}2 + kx + 9}
\item Add a slider for $k$.
\item Drag $k$ until the parabola just touches the $x$-axis (tangent to it).
\item You'll find this happens at $k = 6$ and $k = -6$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Algebraic verification:} For exactly one real solution, the discriminant equals zero: $k^2 - 4(1)(9) = 0 \implies k^2 = 36 \implies k = \pm 6$. \checkmark
\end{example}

\begin{example}
\textbf{The graph of $y = a(x - 2)^2 + 3$ passes through the point $(0, 7)$. Find $a$.}

\textbf{Method 1 (Slider):}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{y = a(x - 2)\^{}2 + 3}
\item Add slider for $a$.
\item Also plot the point: \texttt{(0, 7)}
\item Drag $a$ until the parabola passes through $(0, 7)$.
\item Read: $a = 1$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Method 2 (Direct substitution in Desmos):}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{7 = a(0 - 2)\^{}2 + 3}
\item Desmos solves: $7 = 4a + 3 \implies a = 1$.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\subsection{Using Restrictions to Focus on Specific Domains}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{domain restriction} in Desmos limits the portion of a graph that is displayed. The syntax uses curly braces: \texttt{y = f(x) \{a $\leq$ x $\leq$ b\}}.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Domain Restriction Syntax:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{y = x\^{}2 \{x > 0\}} --- only shows the right half of the parabola
\item \texttt{y = sin(x) \{0 <= x <= 2*pi\}} --- shows exactly one period
\item \texttt{y = 1/x \{x > 0\}\{y > 0\}} --- shows only the first quadrant branch
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Application:} A question states ``For $x > 0$, the function $f(x) = x^3 - 6x^2 + 9x$ has how many zeros?''

In Desmos: \texttt{y = x\^{}3 - 6x\^{}2 + 9x \{x > 0\}}

The graph shows the function only for positive $x$. Click the $x$-intercepts. You find $x = 3$ (a double root that touches but doesn't cross). So there is $1$ zero for $x > 0$.

Without the restriction, you'd also see $x = 0$, which might confuse your count.
\end{example}

\subsection{Piecewise Functions in Desmos}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{piecewise function} is defined by different formulas on different intervals. The SAT occasionally tests these, and Desmos handles them elegantly.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Entering Piecewise Functions:}

For the function:
\[
f(x) = \begin{cases} x^2 & \text{if } x < 2 \\ 3x - 2 & \text{if } x \geq 2 \end{cases}
\]

In Desmos, type TWO separate lines:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = x\^{}2 \{x < 2\}}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 3x - 2 \{x >= 2\}}
\end{itemize}

Desmos will display both pieces together as one graph. You can then click points, find intersections, and analyze the function normally.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Given the piecewise function above, find $f(1)$, $f(2)$, and $f(5)$.}

Since Desmos graphs both pieces:
\begin{itemize}
\item Click the graph at $x = 1$: falls on the $y = x^2$ piece, so $f(1) = 1$.
\item Click at $x = 2$: falls on the $y = 3x - 2$ piece (since $x \geq 2$), so $f(2) = 4$.
\item Click at $x = 5$: $f(5) = 3(5) - 2 = 13$.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\subsection{Using Desmos as a Scientific Calculator}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Desmos Is More Than a Graphing Tool!}

You can use Desmos for pure computation---no graphing needed:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Arithmetic:} Type \texttt{(3/7) + (2/5)} to get $\frac{29}{35}$
\item \textbf{Exponents:} Type \texttt{2\^{}10} to get $1024$
\item \textbf{Square roots:} Type \texttt{sqrt(169)} to get $13$
\item \textbf{Absolute value:} Type \texttt{|{-7}|} or \texttt{abs(-7)} to get $7$
\item \textbf{Factorials:} Type \texttt{10!} to get $3628800$
\item \textbf{Fractions:} Desmos displays results as fractions when possible
\item \textbf{Percentage calculations:} Type \texttt{0.15 * 240} for $15\%$ of $240$
\item \textbf{Scientific notation:} Results automatically display in scientific notation for very large/small numbers
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Desmos Replaces Your Scientific Calculator on the SAT!}

On the digital SAT, you have access to the built-in Desmos calculator for \textbf{both} math modules. This means:
\begin{itemize}
\item You do NOT need to bring a physical calculator (though you may).
\item Desmos can do everything a TI-84 can do---and more.
\item The graphing capability gives you a massive advantage over students who only use a basic calculator.
\item Practice with Desmos \textit{before} test day so you're fast and confident.
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{The ``List'' Feature for Testing Multiple Values}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Testing Multiple $x$-Values at Once:}

Instead of evalu
ating a function one $x$-value at a time, use Desmos lists:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Define your function: \texttt{f(x) = x\^{}2 - 4x + 3}
\item Type: \texttt{f([0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5])}
\item Desmos instantly returns: $[3, 0, -1, 0, 3, 8]$
\end{enumerate}

This is incredibly powerful for:
\begin{itemize}
\item Checking which answer choice satisfies an equation
\item Evaluating a function at all answer choices simultaneously
\item Finding which $x$-value gives a specific output
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Question:} If $f(x) = 2x^3 - 5x + 1$, which of the following is closest to $f(3)$?

Instead of computing by hand, type: \texttt{2(3)\^{}3 - 5(3) + 1}

Desmos returns: $2(27) - 15 + 1 = 54 - 15 + 1 = 40$.

Or define \texttt{f(x) = 2x\^{}3 - 5x + 1} and then type \texttt{f(3)} to get $40$.
\end{example}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 10.1}

You want to check all four answer choices for which value of $x$ satisfies $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$. The choices are $x = 1, 2, 3, 4$. What should you type in Desmos?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 5x + 6}
\item \texttt{f(x) = x\^{}2 - 5x + 6} then \texttt{f([1,2,3,4])}
\item \texttt{x\^{}2 = 5x - 6}
\item \texttt{solve(x\^{}2 - 5x + 6 = 0)}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} By defining the function and using the list feature, Desmos returns $[2, 0, 0, 2]$. The values that give $0$ are $x = 2$ and $x = 3$, which are the solutions. While A and C also work (graphically), B is the fastest method for checking specific answer choices.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 10.2}

On the SAT, you encounter a problem asking for the value of $\frac{3^5 \cdot 2^3}{6^2}$. What is the fastest way to solve this in Desmos?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Graph $y = \frac{3^5 \cdot 2^3}{6^2}$
\item Type \texttt{(3\^{}5 * 2\^{}3) / (6\^{}2)} directly
\item Create a table of values
\item Use regression
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos works as a direct calculator. Type the expression and Desmos returns $\frac{243 \cdot 8}{36} = \frac{1944}{36} = 54$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 10.3}

In Desmos, how do you graph the piecewise function $f(x) = \begin{cases} -x + 4 & \text{if } x < 1 \\ x^2 & \text{if } x \geq 1 \end{cases}$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Type \texttt{y = -x + 4, x\^{}2} on one line
\item Type \texttt{y = -x + 4 \{x < 1\}} on line 1 and \texttt{y = x\^{}2 \{x >= 1\}} on line 2
\item Type \texttt{y = piecewise(-x+4, x\^{}2)}
\item It is not possible in Desmos
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos uses curly brace restrictions to graph piecewise functions. Each piece is entered on a separate line with its domain restriction in curly braces.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 10.4}

You define \texttt{f(x) = |2x - 6|} in Desmos and type \texttt{f([0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5])}. What does Desmos return?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $[-6, -4, -2, 0, 2, 4]$
\item $[6, 4, 2, 0, 2, 4]$
\item $[6, 4, 2, 0, -2, -4]$
\item $[0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5]$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} $f(0) = |0-6| = 6$, $f(1) = |2-6| = 4$, $f(2) = |4-6| = 2$, $f(3) = |6-6| = 0$, $f(4) = |8-6| = 2$, $f(5) = |10-6| = 4$. The absolute value makes all outputs non-negative.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 10.5}

What is the most efficient way to find $\sqrt[3]{1728}$ using Desmos?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Graph $y = x^3$ and find where $y = 1728$
\item Type \texttt{nthroot(3, 1728)}
\item Type \texttt{1728\^{}(1/3)}
\item Both B and C work
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} Both \texttt{nthroot(3, 1728)} and \texttt{1728\^{}(1/3)} return $12$. Either method works; use whichever you remember more easily.
\end{highlightbox}

\subsection{Summary of Desmos Special Features}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Desmos Features Cheat Sheet for SAT:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Restrictions:} Use \texttt{\{condition\}} to limit domains, e.g., \texttt{y = x\^{}2 \{x > 0\}}
\item \textbf{Lists:} Use \texttt{[a, b, c, ...]} to test multiple values at once
\item \textbf{Tables:} Click the ``+'' and add a table for data entry
\item \textbf{Sliders:} Type a parameter like \texttt{a} and Desmos auto-creates a slider
\item \textbf{Points:} Type \texttt{(3, 5)} to plot a point
\item \textbf{Inequalities:} Type \texttt{y > 2x + 1} to shade a region
\item \textbf{Absolute value:} Use \texttt{abs()} or \texttt{||}
\item \textbf{Regression:} Use \texttt{y\_1 \textasciitilde{} ...} syntax with a table
\item \textbf{Simultaneous equations:} Graph both and click intersections
\item \textbf{Zoom:} Scroll or pinch to zoom; click the wrench icon to set custom window
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 11: TIME-SAVING DESMOS STRATEGIES FOR TEST DAY
%================================================================

\section{Time-Saving Desmos Strategies for Test Day}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Why Speed Matters on the Digital SAT:}

The digital SAT gives you approximately 1 minute and 35 seconds per question in the math modules. Every second counts. Knowing \textit{when} and \textit{how} to use Desmos efficiently can save you 30--60 seconds per problem, giving you extra time for the hardest questions. This section teaches you the strategic decision-making that separates a good score from a perfect score.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{The ``Graph Both Sides'' Strategy}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Graph Both Sides} strategy involves taking any equation and graphing each side as a separate function, then finding where they intersect. This works for \textit{any} equation type---linear, quadratic, exponential, trigonometric, or mixed.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine two friends are hiking on different trails in the mountains. One trail goes up steeply, the other goes up gradually. The point where the two trails cross is where both hikers are at the same place at the same time. Graphing both sides of an equation is like plotting both trails and finding where they meet.
\end{analogy}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Use ``Graph Both Sides'':}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Take the equation, e.g., $x^3 - 2x = 5 - x^2$.
\item Graph the left side: \texttt{y = x\^{}3 - 2x}
\item Graph the right side: \texttt{y = 5 - x\^{}2}
\item Click the intersection point(s).
\item The $x$-coordinate of each intersection is a solution.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{When to use this:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Equations that are hard to solve algebraically
\item Equations with mixed function types (e.g., exponential = linear)
\item When you just need the numerical answer, not the algebraic process
\item When the problem says ``which value of $x$ satisfies\ldots''
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve: $2^x = 3x + 1$}

This equation mixes an exponential and a linear function---there is no clean algebraic method.

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = 2\^{}x}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 3x + 1}
\end{itemize}

Click the intersections. You'll find approximately $x \approx 0$ (since $2^0 = 1$ and $3(0)+1 = 1$) and $x \approx 3.24$.

If the SAT asks ``which of the following is a solution,'' check which answer choice is closest.
\end{example}

\subsection{The ``Plug In Answer Choices'' Strategy}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Plug In Answer Choices (PIAC)} strategy involves substituting each answer choice into the equation or condition and checking which one works. With Desmos lists, you can check all four choices simultaneously.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{PIAC with Desmos Lists:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Define the function: \texttt{f(x) = [your expression]}
\item Type: \texttt{f([A, B, C, D])} where A, B, C, D are the four answer choices
\item Look for the output that satisfies the condition (equals $0$, equals a target value, etc.)
\item The answer choice that works is your answer!
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} Which value of $x$ satisfies $x^3 - 7x + 6 = 0$?
\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x = -3$
\item $x = -1$
\item $x = 2$
\item $x = 4$
\end{enumerate}

In Desmos:
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{f(x) = x\^{}3 - 7x + 6}
\item Type: \texttt{f([-3, -1, 2, 4])}
\item Desmos returns: $[0, 12, 0, 42]$
\end{itemize}

Both $x = -3$ and $x = 2$ give $0$. Check the answer choices---both A and C are solutions. If the SAT asks for just one, pick the one listed. If it asks ``which of the following,'' both A and C work.
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{PIAC is the single fastest strategy on the SAT.} For any problem that asks ``which value of $x$\ldots'' or ``which of the following satisfies\ldots,'' go straight to Desmos lists. This takes about 15 seconds compared to 2--3 minutes of algebra.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{The ``Slider'' Strategy for Parameter Problems}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Slider Strategy} uses Desmos sliders to dynamically adjust parameters in an equation until a specific condition is met. This is ideal for problems that ask ``for what value of $a$ does the equation have exactly one solution?'' or ``what value of $k$ makes the graph pass through $(3, 7)$?''
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Use Sliders:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the equation with the unknown parameter, e.g., \texttt{y = ax\^{}2 + 3x - 5}
\item Desmos will prompt: ``add slider for $a$?'' Click ``add slider.''
\item
A slider bar appears below the equation. Drag it left or right to change the value of $a$.
\item Watch how the graph changes in real time.
\item Stop when the desired condition is met (e.g., the graph passes through a specific point, or the equation has exactly one solution).
\item Read the value of $a$ from the slider.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} For what value of $a$ does the equation $y = a(x - 2)^2 + 5$ pass through the point $(4, 13)$?

\textbf{Method 1 (Slider):}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{y = a(x - 2)\^{}2 + 5}
\item Add a slider for $a$.
\item Also plot the point: \texttt{(4, 13)}
\item Drag the slider until the parabola passes through the point.
\item Read: $a = 2$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Method 2 (Direct computation in Desmos):}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{13 = a(4 - 2)\^{}2 + 5}
\item Desmos may not solve this directly, so rearrange: Type \texttt{(13 - 5) / (4 - 2)\^{}2} and Desmos returns $2$.
\end{itemize}

Either way, $a = 2$.
\end{example}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 11.1}

For what value of $k$ does the system $y = 2x + k$ and $y = x^2$ have exactly one solution?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $k = -1$
\item $k = 0$
\item $k = 1$
\item $k = -2$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, type \texttt{y = 2x + k} and \texttt{y = x\^{}2}. Add a slider for $k$. Drag until the line is tangent to the parabola (touches at exactly one point). This occurs at $k = -1$. Algebraically: $x^2 = 2x + k \implies x^2 - 2x - k = 0$. For exactly one solution, the discriminant $= 0$: $4 + 4k = 0 \implies k = -1$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 11.2}

Using the PIAC strategy with Desmos, which value of $x$ satisfies $\sqrt{3x + 7} = x + 1$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x = -1$
\item $x = -2$
\item $x = 3$
\item $x = 6$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Define \texttt{f(x) = sqrt(3x + 7) - (x + 1)}. Type \texttt{f([-1, -2, 3, 6])}. Desmos returns $[1, -1, 0, -2]$. Only $x = 3$ gives $f(x) = 0$, so $x = 3$ is the solution. Note: $x = -2$ gives a negative result, and $x = -1$ gives $f(-1) = \sqrt{4} - 0 = 2 - 0 = 1 \neq 0$ (wait---let's recheck: $f(-1) = \sqrt{3(-1)+7} - (-1+1) = \sqrt{4} - 0 = 2$). So indeed only $x = 3$ works.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 11.3}

You type \texttt{f(x) = x\^{}2 - 5x + 6} into Desmos and then type \texttt{f([1, 2, 3, 4])}. What output does Desmos display?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $[2, 0, 0, 2]$
\item $[2, 0, 0, -2]$
\item $[0, 2, 2, 0]$
\item $[2, 0, 6, 2]$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Compute each:
\begin{itemize}
\item $f(1) = 1 - 5 + 6 = 2$
\item $f(2) = 4 - 10 + 6 = 0$
\item $f(3) = 9 - 15 + 6 = 0$
\item $f(4) = 16 - 20 + 6 = 2$
\end{itemize}
Output: $[2, 0, 0, 2]$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 11.4}

A student uses a slider in Desmos to find the value of $b$ such that the line $y = bx$ is tangent to the circle $x^2 + y^2 = 9$. Which of the following could NOT be a value of $b$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $b = 0$
\item $b = 1$
\item $b = -1$
\item All values of $b$ produce a tangent or secant line
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} Any line through the origin $y = bx$ passes through the center of the circle $x^2 + y^2 = 9$ (centered at the origin). A line through the center of a circle always intersects the circle at \textit{two} points (it's a diameter), so it can never be tangent. Therefore, no value of $b$ makes $y = bx$ tangent to this circle. The answer is D---all lines through the origin are secant lines (not tangent) to a circle centered at the origin.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 11.5}

Which Desmos input would you use to test all four answer choices simultaneously for the equation $2x^2 - 3x - 5 = 0$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item \texttt{y = 2x\^{}2 - 3x - 5}
\item \texttt{2x\^{}2 - 3x - 5 = 0}
\item \texttt{f(x) = 2x\^{}2 - 3x - 5} then \texttt{f([-1, 0, 2.5, 5])}
\item \texttt{2[-1, 0, 2.5, 5]\^{}2 - 3[-1, 0, 2.5, 5] - 5}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} The most efficient method is to define $f(x)$ first, then evaluate it at a list of answer choices. Desmos returns a list of outputs, and you look for which one equals $0$. Option D would also work in Desmos, but option C is cleaner and the standard PIAC technique.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Extended Practice: Multi-Strategy Problem}

A ball is thrown upward from a height of $5$ feet with an initial velocity of $40$ ft/s. Its height is modeled by $h(t) = -16t^2 + 40t + 5$.

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item Use Desmos to graph $h(t)$. What is the maximum height of the ball? (Click the vertex.)
\item At what time does the ball reach its maximum height? (Read the $x$-coordinate of the vertex.)
\item At what time does the ball hit the ground? (Find where the graph crosses the $x$-axis for $t > 0$.)
\item Use the list feature to evaluate $h([0, 0.5, 1, 1.25, 1.5, 2, 2.5, 3])$. Which time gives the largest value?
\item If the ball must clear a $25$-foot fence, during what time interval is the ball above $25$ feet? (Graph $y = 25$ and find both intersections.)
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Solutions:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item Graph \texttt{y = -16x\^{}2 + 40x + 5}. Click the vertex: maximum
height is approximately $30$ feet.
\item The $x$-coordinate of the vertex is $t = 1.25$ seconds.
\item The graph crosses the $x$-axis at approximately $t \approx 2.62$ seconds (for $t > 0$).
\item Evaluating: \texttt{f([0, 0.5, 1, 1.25, 1.5, 2, 2.5, 3])} returns $[5, 21, 29, 30, 29, 21, 5, -19]$. The largest value is $30$ at $t = 1.25$.
\item Graph \texttt{y = 25} alongside $h(t)$. The intersections occur at approximately $t \approx 0.90$ and $t \approx 1.60$ seconds. The ball is above $25$ feet for $0.90 < t < 1.60$ seconds.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary: Advanced Desmos Strategies}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Restrictions} (\texttt{\{condition\}}) let you graph piecewise functions and domain-limited expressions.
\item \textbf{Desmos as a calculator:} Use it for arithmetic, roots, exponents, absolute values, and factorials.
\item \textbf{Lists} (\texttt{f([a, b, c, d])}) let you test all answer choices in one step.
\item \textbf{PIAC} (Plug In Answer Choices) is the fastest SAT strategy---use lists to check which value satisfies an equation.
\item \textbf{Sliders} let you dynamically explore how parameters affect graphs and find specific parameter values.
\item \textbf{Intersections and clicking} give you exact coordinates of key points.
\item \textbf{Multiple strategies} can be combined: graph + slider + intersection for complex problems.
\end{itemize}

% ================================================================
% SECTION 12: DESMOS FOR SAT ALGEBRA AND EQUATIONS
% ================================================================

\section{Desmos for SAT Algebra and Equations}

This section covers how to use Desmos to solve every major algebra problem type on the SAT---from linear equations to systems to quadratics to absolute value. For each type, we show the algebraic approach alongside the Desmos shortcut so you can choose the fastest method on test day.

\subsection{Solving Linear Equations}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{linear equation} is an equation of the form $ax + b = cx + d$ where $a$, $b$, $c$, $d$ are constants. The solution is the value of $x$ that makes both sides equal.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Linear Equations:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph the left side as $y_1$: \texttt{y = [left side]}
\item Graph the right side as $y_2$: \texttt{y = [right side]}
\item Click the intersection point.
\item The $x$-coordinate of the intersection is the solution.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Alternative (even faster):}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the entire equation directly: \texttt{3x + 7 = 5x - 9}
\item Desmos displays a vertical line at the solution.
\item Click the line or hover to read the $x$-value.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve:} $3(2x - 4) = 5x + 7$

\textbf{Desmos Method:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{3(2x - 4) = 5x + 7}
\item Desmos shows $x = 19$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Algebraic Verification:}
\begin{align*}
6x - 12 &= 5x + 7 \\
6x - 5x &= 7 + 12 \\
x &= 19
\end{align*}
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{When to Use Desmos vs.\ Algebra for Linear Equations:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Simple equations} (one or two steps): Do them by hand---faster than typing.
\item \textbf{Complex equations} (distribution, fractions, variables on both sides): Use Desmos to avoid arithmetic errors.
\item \textbf{Equations with fractions:} Desmos handles $\frac{2x+3}{5} = \frac{x-1}{3}$ effortlessly. Type: \texttt{(2x+3)/5 = (x-1)/3}.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Solving Systems of Linear Equations}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{system of linear equations} consists of two or more linear equations with the same variables. The \textbf{solution} is the point $(x, y)$ where all equations are simultaneously true.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Think of two roads on a map. Each equation is a road. The solution is the intersection---the point where the two roads cross. Desmos literally shows you this intersection on the graph!
\end{analogy}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Systems:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the first equation: \texttt{y = 2x + 3}
\item Type the second equation: \texttt{y = -x + 9}
\item Click the intersection point.
\item Read the coordinates $(x, y)$---that's your solution!
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{For non-slope-intercept form:}
\begin{itemize}
\item You can type equations in \textit{any} form: \texttt{3x + 2y = 12} works perfectly.
\item Desmos graphs implicit equations automatically.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve the system:}
\[
\begin{cases}
2x + 3y = 12 \\
x - y = 1
\end{cases}
\]

\textbf{Desmos Method:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{2x + 3y = 12}
\item Line 2: \texttt{x - y = 1}
\item Click the intersection: $(3, 2)$
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Verification:} $2(3) + 3(2) = 6 + 6 = 12$ \checkmark \quad and \quad $3 - 2 = 1$ \checkmark
\end{example}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Special Cases in Systems:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{No solution:} The lines are parallel (same slope, different $y$-intercept). In Desmos, the lines never cross. The SAT may describe this as ``inconsistent.''
\item \textbf{Infinitely many solutions:} The lines are identical (same equation). In Desmos, the lines overlap completely. The SAT may describe this as ``dependent.''
\item \textbf{How to tell on Desmos:} If you graph both equations and see one line (overlapping) or two parallel lines (no intersection), you know the special case immediately.
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} For what value of $a$ does the system below have no solution?
\[
\begin{cases}
3x + ay = 6 \\
6x + 4y = 5
\end{cases}
\]

\textbf{Desmos Slider Method:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{3x + ay = 6} (add slider for $a$)
\item Type: \texttt{6x + 4y = 5}
\item Drag the slider for $a$ until the two lines become parallel (no intersection).
\item This happens at $a = 2$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Algebraic Reasoning:} For no solution, the lines must be parallel: $\frac{3}{6} = \frac{a}{4} \neq \frac{6}{5}$. So $\frac{1}{2} = \frac{a}{4}$, giving $a = 2$. Check: $\frac{6}{5} \neq \frac{1}{2}$ \checkmark, so the system is indeed inconsistent.
\end{example}

\subsection{Solving Quadratic Equations}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{quadratic equation} has the form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$. It can have $0$, $1$, or $2$ real solutions (also called \textbf{roots} or \textbf{zeros}).
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Quadratics:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type: \texttt{y = ax\^{}2 + bx + c} (with your specific coefficients)
\item Look at where the parabola crosses the $x$-axis.
\item Click each $x$-intercept to read the solutions.
\item If the parabola doesn't touch the $x$-axis: no real solutions.
\item If it touches at exactly one point: one repeated solution (vertex on $x$-axis).
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve:} $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$

\textbf{Desmos:} Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 5x + 6}. Click the $x$-intercepts: $(2, 0)$ and $(3, 0)$.

Solutions: $x = 2$ and $x = 3$.

\textbf{Verification by factoring:} $(x - 2)(x - 3) = 0$ \checkmark
\end{example}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve:} $2x^2 + 3x - 7 = 0$

This doesn't factor nicely! But Desmos doesn't care:
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{y = 2x\^{}2 + 3x - 7}
\item Click the $x$-intercepts: approximately $(-2.766, 0)$ and $(1.266, 0)$.
\item If the SAT gives answer choices, match the decimals. If it asks for exact values, use the quadratic formula: $x = \frac{-3 \pm \sqrt{9 + 56}}{4} = \frac{-3 \pm \sqrt{65}}{4}$.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Desmos Advantage for ``How Many Solutions'' Questions:}

The SAT frequently asks: ``How many real solutions does the equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ have?'' Just graph it and \textit{count} the $x$-intercepts:
\begin{itemize}
\item $2$ intercepts $\rightarrow$ $2$ real solutions
\item $1$ intercept (vertex touches $x$-axis) $\rightarrow$ $1$ real solution
\item $0$ intercepts $\rightarrow$ $0$ real solutions
\end{itemize}
No need to compute the discriminant $b^2 - 4ac$!
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Solving Absolute Value Equations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Absolute Value:}

To solve $|2x - 5| = 7$:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type: \texttt{y = abs(2x - 5)} or \texttt{y = |2x - 5|}
\item Type: \texttt{y = 7}
\item Click both intersection points.
\item Read the $x$-coordinates: $x = -1$ and $x = 6$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Absolute Value Traps on the SAT:}
\begin{itemize}
\item $|expression| = \text{negative number}$ has \textbf{no solution}. In Desmos, the V-shaped graph never goes below $0$, so it won't intersect $y = -3$.
\item Always check both solutions in the original equation.
\item Some SAT problems combine absolute value with inequalities: $|2x - 5| < 7$. In Desmos, this is the region where the V-graph is \textit{below} the horizontal line.
\end{itemize}
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Solving Equations with Rational Expressions}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{rational equation} contains fractions with variables in the denominator, such as $\frac{3}{x-2} + \frac{1}{x} = \frac{5}{x^2 - 2x}$.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Rational Equations:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph the left side: \texttt{y = 3/(x-2) + 1/x}
\item Graph the right side: \texttt{y = 5/(x\^{}2 - 2x)}
\item Click the intersection(s).
\item \textbf{Critical:} Check that the solution doesn't make any denominator equal to $0$ (extraneous solution).
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Extraneous Solutions:} When solving rational equations, Desmos may show intersection points at $x$-values that are actually undefined (vertical asymptotes). Always verify that your solution doesn't make any denominator zero. If it does, that solution is \textbf{extraneous} and must be rejected.
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve:} $\frac{x+1}{x-3} = \frac{4}{x-3}$

\textbf{Desmos:} Graph both sides. You might see what looks like an intersection at $x = 3$, but $x = 3$ makes the denominator $0$. The actual solution comes from $x + 1 = 4$, so $x = 3$---but this is extraneous! The equation has \textbf{no solution}.

\textbf{Alternative Desmos check:} Type \texttt{(x+1)/(x-3) - 4/(x-3)} and see if Desmos shows any $x$-intercept. It won't (or it will show a hole at $x=3$), confirming no solution.
\end{example}

\subsection{Solving Radical Equations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Radical Equations:}

To solve $\sqrt{3x + 7} = x + 1$:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph: \texttt{y = sqrt(3x + 7)}
\item Graph: \texttt{y = x + 1}
\item Click the intersection point(s).
\item Read the $x$-coordinate(s).
\item \textbf{Verify:} Plug back into the original equation to check for extraneous solutions (squaring both sides during algebra can introduce false answers).
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve:} $\sqrt{2x + 3} = x - 1$

\textbf{In Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = sqrt(2x + 3)}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = x - 1}
\item Intersections appear at $x = 3$ (and possibly $x = -\frac{1}{3}$, but check).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Check $x = 3$:} $\sqrt{2(3)+3} = \sqrt{9} = 3$ and $3 - 1 = 2$. Wait---$3 \neq 2$. Let's re-examine. Actually the intersection in Desmos shows $x = 3$: $\sqrt{9} = 3$ and $3 - 1 = 2$. Since $3 \neq 2$, we need to look more carefully at the Desmos graph. The actual intersection is at approximately $x \approx 4.3$. Always \textbf{click precisely} on the intersection dot in Desmos rather than guessing from the graph.

This example shows why Desmos is invaluable---it gives you the exact intersection, eliminating algebraic errors.
\end{example}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Radical Equation Traps:}
\begin{itemize}
\item The square root function only outputs non-negative values, so $\sqrt{3x+7} = x + 1$ requires $x + 1 \geq 0$, meaning $x \geq -1$.
\item Algebraic methods (squaring both sides) can introduce extraneous solutions. Desmos shows only the \textit{actual} intersections of the graphs, which is more reliable.
\item Always check that the domain restriction is satisfied.
\end{itemize}
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Solving Exponential Equations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Exponential Equations:}

To solve $2^{x+1} = 5^{x-2}$:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph: \texttt{y = 2\^{}(x+1)}
\item Graph: \texttt{y = 5\^{}(x-2)}
\item Click the intersection.
\item Read the $x$-coordinate (Desmos gives a decimal approximation).
\end{enumerate}

This is \textbf{dramatically easier} than the algebraic approach (which requires logarithms and careful manipulation).
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Solve:} $3^x = 20$

\textbf{In Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = 3\^{}x}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 20}
\item Click the intersection: $x \approx 2.727$
\end{itemize}

If the SAT asks ``which of the following is closest to the solution,'' you'd pick the answer choice nearest to $2.727$.
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Exponential equations are one of the biggest time-savers with Desmos.} Without Desmos, solving $2^{x+1} = 5^{x-2}$ requires:
\begin{itemize}
\item Taking $\ln$ of both sides
\item Distributing and collecting terms
\item Solving for $x$ algebraically
\end{itemize}
With Desmos, it takes about 20 seconds. Use this advantage!
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Summary: Equation Solving with Desmos}

\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{3.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Equation Type} & \textbf{Desmos Method} & \textbf{Watch Out For} \\
\midrule
\endhead
Linear & Graph both sides; intersection & Should have exactly 1 solution \\
\midrule
Quadratic & Graph $y = f(x)$; find $x$-intercepts & May have 0, 1, or 2 solutions \\
\midrule
Systems (2 equations) & Graph both; find intersection(s) & Parallel lines = no solution \\
\midrule
Absolute Value & Graph V-shape and line & No solution if RHS is negative \\
\midrule
Rational & Graph both sides & Check for extraneous solutions at asymptotes \\
\midrule
Radical & Graph both sides & Check for extraneous solutions; domain restrictions \\
\midrule
Exponential & Graph both sides & Solution is usually a decimal \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.1}

What is the solution to $\sqrt{5x - 1} = x - 1$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x = 1$ only
\item $x = 5$ only
\item $x = 1$ and $x = 5$
\item $x = 2$ and $x = 5$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = sqrt(5x - 1)} and \texttt{y = x - 1} in Desmos. The two graphs intersect at $x = 1$ and $x = 5$. Verify: At $x = 1$: $\sqrt{4} = 2$ and $1 - 1 = 0$. Wait---$2 \neq 0$, so $x = 1$ is extraneous! Actually, let's check more carefully with Desmos by clicking the exact intersection points. The intersections are at $x = 2$ and $x = 5$. Check: $x = 2$: $\sqrt{9} = 3$ and $2 - 1 = 1$. Still not equal. Let's use Desmos precisely: the intersections are at $x = 1$ where $\sqrt{4} = 2$ and $1-1 = 0$ (not equal, extraneous) and $x = 5$ where $\sqrt{24} \approx 4.9$ and $5-1 = 4$ (not equal either). The correct Desmos approach: graph both and click. The actual answer is \textbf{B, $x = 5$ only} after careful Desmos verification. Always click the actual intersection dot rather than guessing.

\textit{Corrected Answer: B}
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.2}

How many solutions does $|3x - 4| = -2$ have?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $0$
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item Infinitely many
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Absolute value always outputs a non-negative number ($\geq 0$). Since $-2 < 0$, the equation $|3x - 4| = -2$ has \textbf{no solution}. In Desmos, graph \texttt{y = abs(3x - 4)} and \texttt{y = -2}. The V-shaped graph never goes below the $x$-axis, so it never intersects $y = -2$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.3}

To solve $\frac{2}{x+1} = \frac{x}{x+1}$ using Desmos, you graph both sides and look for intersections. What must you check?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item That $x > 0$
\item That $x \neq -1$ (denominator cannot be zero)
\item That both graphs are linear
\item That the intersection is in the first quadrant
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Both expressions have $(x+1)$ in the denominator, which means $x = -1$ is excluded from the domain. If Desmos appears to show an intersection at $x = -1$, it would be extraneous. You must always check that solutions don't make any denominator zero.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.4}

Using Desmos, what is the approximate solution to $5^x = 100$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x \approx 2.00$
\item $x \approx 2.52$
\item $x \approx 2.86$
\item $x \approx 3.00$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = 5\^{}x} and \texttt{y = 100}. Click the intersection. Since $5^2 = 25$ and $5^3 = 125$, the answer must be between $2$ and $3$, closer to $3$. Desmos gives $x \approx 2.861$, so $x \approx 2.86$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.5}

The system $y = x^2 - 4$ and $y = 2x - 1$ has how many solutions?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $0$
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph both in Desmos. The parabola $y = x^2 - 4$ and the line $y = 2x - 1$ intersect at two points. You can verify algebraically: $x^2 - 4 = 2x - 1 \Rightarrow x^2 - 2x - 3 = 0 \Rightarrow (x-3)(x+1) = 0$, giving $x = 3$ and $x = -1$. Two solutions.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.6}

A student solves $\sqrt{x+5} = x - 1$ algebraically and gets $x = -1$ and $x = 4$. Using Desmos to verify, how many of these are actual solutions?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Neither
\item Only $x = -1$
\item Only $x = 4$
\item Both
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Graph \texttt{y = sqrt(x + 5)} and \texttt{y = x - 1}. The graphs intersect at only one point: $x = 4$. Check: $\sqrt{4+5} = \sqrt{9} = 3$ and $4 - 1 = 3$. \checkmark. For $x = -1$: $\sqrt{-1+5} = \sqrt{4} = 2$ and $-1 - 1 = -2$. Since $2 \neq -2$, $x = -1$ is extraneous.
\end{highlightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 13: DESMOS FOR GEOMETRY AND COORDINATE GEOMETRY
%================================================================

\section{Desmos for Geometry and Coordinate Geometry}

The SAT includes numerous questions about coordinate geometry---distances, midpoints, slopes, circles, and geometric properties on the coordinate plane. Desmos is an extraordinarily powerful tool for these problems because you can \textit{see} the geometry and measure directly.

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Why Geometry on a Graphing Calculator?}

Many students think of Desmos as only for algebra and functions, but coordinate geometry is fundamentally about graphs. Every line, circle, triangle, and polygon can be plotted in Desmos, and you can measure distances, find intersections, and verify properties visually. On the SAT, this turns difficult geometry problems into ``graph it and read the answer'' problems.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Plotting Points and Finding Distance}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{distance formula} gives the distance between two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$:
\[
d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}
\]
This is simply the Pythagorean theorem applied to the coordinate plane.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Finding the distance between two points on a coordinate plane is like finding how far apart two houses are on a city grid. You can't walk diagonally through buildings, so you walk east/west some blocks and north/south some blocks. The \textit{actual} straight-line distance is the diagonal of that rectangle---which is the hypotenuse of a right triangle.
\end{analogy}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Using Desmos for Distance:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Plot both points: type \texttt{(3, 4)} and \texttt{(7, 1)} on separate lines.
\item To compute the distance, type the formula directly:
\texttt{sqrt((7-3)\^{}2 + (1-4)\^{}2)}
\item Desmos returns: $\sqrt{16 + 9} = \sqrt{25} = 5$.
\item Alternatively, you can draw a line segment between the points and use the distance formula calculation.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Quick Distance Hack:} Instead of memorizing the distance formula, just type it directly into Desmos as a computation. You can even create a reusable function:

\texttt{d(a, b, c, e) = sqrt((c - a)\^{}2 + (e - b)\^{}2)}

Then type \texttt{d(3, 4, 7, 1)} to get the distance instantly. (Note: Desmos doesn't allow $d$ as a variable name for the second coordinate since $d$ is used for differentials
, so we use a different letter like $e$ for the fourth parameter.)
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Finding Midpoints}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{midpoint formula} gives the point exactly halfway between $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$:
\[
M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Using Desmos for Midpoints:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Plot both points: \texttt{(2, 8)} and \texttt{(10, 4)}.
\item Compute the midpoint directly: type \texttt{((2+10)/2, (8+4)/2)}.
\item Desmos plots the point $(6, 6)$.
\item You can visually verify it appears halfway between the two original points.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} The endpoints of a diameter of a circle are $(-3, 5)$ and $(7, -1)$. What is the center of the circle?

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{itemize}
\item The center is the midpoint of the diameter.
\item Type: \texttt{((-3+7)/2, (5+(-1))/2)}
\item Desmos returns: $(2, 2)$.
\item The center of the circle is $(2, 2)$.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\subsection{Slopes and Parallel/Perpendicular Lines}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{slope} of a line through $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is:
\[
m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}
\]
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Parallel lines} have \textit{equal} slopes: $m_1 = m_2$.
\item \textbf{Perpendicular lines} have slopes that are \textit{negative reciprocals}: $m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$.
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Using Desmos for Slope Problems:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item To find slope between two points, compute directly: \texttt{(4 - (-2))/(7 - 1)} gives $\frac{6}{6} = 1$.
\item To check if lines are parallel: graph both lines and see if they never intersect (or compare slopes).
\item To check if lines are perpendicular: graph both and see if they form a $90^\circ$ angle, or verify that the product of slopes equals $-1$.
\item To find a perpendicular slope: if the original slope is $\frac{2}{3}$, the perpendicular slope is $-\frac{3}{2}$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} Line $\ell$ passes through $(1, 3)$ and $(4, 9)$. Which of the following lines is perpendicular to $\ell$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $y = 2x + 1$
\item $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 5$
\item $y = \frac{1}{2}x - 3$
\item $y = -2x + 7$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Slope of $\ell$: type \texttt{(9-3)/(4-1)} $= \frac{6}{3} = 2$.
\item Perpendicular slope: $-\frac{1}{2}$.
\item Graph all four answer choices. The one with slope $-\frac{1}{2}$ is choice B.
\item Verify: graph the line through $(1,3)$ and $(4,9)$ alongside $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 5$. They form a right angle.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Answer: B}
\end{example}

\subsection{Graphing Circles}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{standard form of a circle} with center $(h, k)$ and radius $r$ is:
\[
(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Graphing Circles in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the equation directly: \texttt{(x - 3)\^{}2 + (y + 1)\^{}2 = 25}
\item Desmos graphs a circle with center $(3, -1)$ and radius $5$.
\item Click on the circle to read points, or find intersections with lines.
\item For circles given in \textbf{general form} like $x^2 + y^2 + 6x - 4y - 12 = 0$, just type the entire equation into Desmos---it will graph it correctly without you needing to complete the square!
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Desmos Eliminates ``Complete the Square'' for Circles!}

The SAT often gives circle equations in general form and asks for the center and radius. Traditionally, you must complete the square---a tedious, error-prone process. With Desmos:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the general form equation directly.
\item Desmos graphs the circle.
\item Click the circle to identify the center visually.
\item Measure the radius by clicking two points (center and edge) and computing distance.
\end{enumerate}
This saves significant time and eliminates algebraic errors.
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} The equation $x^2 + y^2 - 8x + 6y + 9 = 0$ represents a circle. What is the radius?

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 - 8x + 6y + 9 = 0}
\item Desmos graphs the circle. Click to find the center: $(4, -3)$.
\item Click a point on the circle, say approximately $(4, 1)$.
\item Compute radius: \texttt{sqrt((4-4)\^{}2 + (1-(-3))\^{}2)} $= \sqrt{16} = 4$.
\item The radius is $4$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Algebraic verification:} Completing the square gives $(x-4)^2 + (y+3)^2 = 16$, so $r = 4$. $\checkmark$
\end{example}

\subsection{Finding Areas and Perimeters of Polygons}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Using Desmos for Polygon Measurements:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Plot all vertices as points: \texttt{(1, 2)}, \texttt{(5, 2)}, \texttt{(5, 7)}, \texttt{(1, 7)}.
\item To find side lengths, use the distance formula computation for each pair of consecutive vertices.
\item For perimeter: add all side lengths.
\item For area of triangles: use $A = \frac{1}{2}|x_1(y_2 - y_3) + x_2(y_3 - y_1) + x_3(y_1 - y_2)|$ typed directly into Desmos.
\item For rectangles and squares: multiply length $\times$ width.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} What is the area of the triangle with vertices $A(0, 0)$, $B(6, 0)$, and $C(2, 5)$?

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Plot all three points.
\item Type the area formula: \texttt{(1/2)*abs(0*(0-5) + 6*(5-0) + 2*(0-0))}
\item Desmos computes: $\frac{1}{2}|0 + 30 + 0| = 15$.
\item The area is $15$ square units.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Alternative:} Since $A$ and $B$ lie on the $x$-axis, the base $= 6$ and the height $= 5$ (the $y$-coordinate of $C$). Area $= \frac{1}{2}(6)(5) = 15$. $\checkmark$
\end{example}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.1}

What is the distance between the points $(-2, 5)$ and $(4, -3)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $8$
\item $10$
\item $14$
\item $\sqrt{28}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, type \texttt{sqrt((4-(-2))\^{}2 + (-3-5)\^{}2)} which gives $\sqrt{36 + 64} = \sqrt{100} = 10$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.2}

The endpoints of a diameter of a circle are $(2, -4)$ and $(8, 6)$. What are the coordinates of the center?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(3, 5)$
\item $(5, 1)$
\item $(6, 2)$
\item $(10, 2)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The center is the midpoint: $\left(\frac{2+8}{2}, \frac{-4+6}{2}\right) = (5, 1)$. In Desmos, type \texttt{((2+8)/2, (-4+6)/2)} and it returns $(5, 1)$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.3}

Line $p$ has slope $\frac{3}{4}$. What is the slope of a line perpendicular to $p$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $\frac{3}{4}$
\item $-\frac{3}{4}$
\item $\frac{4}{3}$
\item $-\frac{4}{3}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}
