\textbf{Rationale:} Perpendicular slopes are negative reciprocals. The negative reciprocal of $\frac{3}{4}$ is $-\frac{4}{3}$. In Desmos, graph $y = \frac{3}{4}x$ and $y = -\frac{4}{3}x$; they form a $90^\circ$ angle at the origin.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.4}

The equation $x^2 + y^2 + 10x - 2y + 10 = 0$ represents a circle. What is the center of this circle?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(10, -2)$
\item $(-10, 2)$
\item $(5, -1)$
\item $(-5, 1)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type the equation directly into Desmos: \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 + 10x - 2y + 10 = 0}. Desmos graphs the circle. Click to find the center at $(-5, 1)$. Algebraically, completing the square: $(x+5)^2 + (y-1)^2 = 16$, center $= (-5, 1)$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.5}

A triangle has vertices at $(0, 0)$, $(8, 0)$, and $(3, 6)$. What is its area?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $18$
\item $24$
\item $30$
\item $48$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Base along the $x$-axis $= 8$, height $= 6$ (the $y$-coordinate of the third vertex). Area $= \frac{1}{2}(8)(6) = 24$. In Desmos, type \texttt{(1/2)*abs(0*(0-6) + 8*(6-0) + 3*(0-0))} which gives $\frac{1}{2}|0 + 48 + 0| = 24$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.6}

Which of the following equations represents a circle with center $(3, -2)$ and radius $7$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(x + 3)^2 + (y - 2)^2 = 7$
\item $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 7$
\item $(x + 3)^2 + (y - 2)^2 = 49$
\item $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 49$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} Standard form: $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ with $(h,k) = (3, -2)$ and $r = 7$. So $(x-
3)^2 + (y+2)^2 = 49$. Verify in Desmos by typing \texttt{(x-3)\^{}2 + (y+2)\^{}2 = 49} and checking that the circle is centered at $(3, -2)$ with radius $7$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.7}

Points $A(1, 3)$ and $B(7, 11)$ are endpoints of a diameter of a circle. What is the equation of the circle?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(x-4)^2 + (y-7)^2 = 100$
\item $(x-4)^2 + (y-7)^2 = 25$
\item $(x-3)^2 + (y-4)^2 = 25$
\item $(x-4)^2 + (y-7)^2 = 50$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Center $= $ midpoint $= \left(\frac{1+7}{2}, \frac{3+11}{2}\right) = (4, 7)$. Radius $= \frac{1}{2} \times$ diameter. Diameter $= \sqrt{(7-1)^2 + (11-3)^2} = \sqrt{36+64} = \sqrt{100} = 10$. So radius $= 5$ and $r^2 = 25$. Equation: $(x-4)^2 + (y-7)^2 = 25$. Verify in Desmos by graphing the equation and the two points.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Extended Coordinate Geometry Challenge}

A quadrilateral has vertices $P(0,0)$, $Q(6,0)$, $R(8,5)$, and $S(2,5)$.

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item Plot all four points in Desmos. Connect them by graphing the four line segments (use restrictions).
\item Find the length of each side using the distance formula in Desmos.
\item Find the slopes of $\overline{PQ}$ and $\overline{SR}$. Are they parallel?
\item Find the slopes of $\overline{PS}$ and $\overline{QR}$. Are they parallel?
\item What type of quadrilateral is $PQRS$? Justify your answer.
\item Find the area of $PQRS$. (Hint: It's a parallelogram---use base $\times$ height.)
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Solutions:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item In Desmos, plot each point and use segment restrictions like \texttt{y = 0 \{0 $\leq$ x $\leq$ 6\}} for $\overline{PQ}$, etc.
\item $PQ = 6$, $QR = \sqrt{(8-6)^2 + (5-0)^2} = \sqrt{4+25} = \sqrt{29}$, $RS = 6$, $SP = \sqrt{(2-0)^2 + (5-0)^2} = \sqrt{4+25} = \sqrt{29}$.
\item Slope of $\overline{PQ} = \frac{0-0}{6-0} = 0$. Slope of $\overline{SR} = \frac{5-5}{8-2} = 0$. Yes, they are parallel (both horizontal).
\item Slope of $\overline{PS} = \frac{5-0}{2-0} = \frac{5}{2}$. Slope of $\overline{QR} = \frac{5-0}{8-6} = \frac{5}{2}$. Yes, they are parallel.
\item Both pairs of opposite sides are parallel and equal in length, so $PQRS$ is a \textbf{parallelogram}.
\item Base $= PQ = 6$, height $= 5$ (vertical distance between the parallel horizontal sides). Area $= 6 \times 5 = 30$ square units.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary: Coordinate Geometry with Desmos}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Plot points} by typing coordinates directly---use them as visual anchors.
\item \textbf{Distance formula:} Type \texttt{sqrt((x2-x1)\^{}2 + (y2-y1)\^{}2)} directly as a computation.
\item \textbf{Midpoints:} Type \texttt{((x1+x2)/2, (y1+y2)/2)} to compute and plot simultaneously.
\item \textbf{Slopes:} Parallel lines have equal slopes; perpendicular lines have negative reciprocal slopes.
\item \textbf{Circles:} Type the equation directly---Desmos graphs circles in standard or general form.
\item \textbf{Triangles and polygons:} Plot vertices, connect with line segments, and use formulas for area.
\item \textbf{Key advantage:} Seeing the geometry makes it much easier to identify relationships and catch errors.
\end{itemize}

%================================================================
% SECTION 14: DESMOS FOR DATA, STATISTICS, AND REGRESSION
%================================================================

\section{Desmos for Data, Statistics, and Regression}

The SAT includes questions about data analysis, scatter plots, lines of best fit, and interpreting statistical relationships. Desmos has powerful built-in regression tools that make these problems fast and accurate.

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{SAT Data Analysis Questions:}

The SAT Math section frequently presents data in tables or scatter plots and asks you to:
\begin{itemize}
\item Find the line (or curve) of best fit
\item Interpret slope and $y$-intercept in context
\item Make predictions using a regression model
\item Determine which model (linear, quadratic, exponential) best fits the data
\item Interpret $r$ (correlation coefficient)
\end{itemize}
Desmos can perform all of these tasks instantly.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Entering Data Tables}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{data table} in Desmos is a two-column input where you enter $x$-values and corresponding $y$-values. Desmos automatically plots the data as a scatter plot.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Enter a Data Table in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Click the ``+'' button in the top-left corner of the expression list.
\item Select ``table.''
\item A two-column table appears with headers $x_1$ and $y_1$.
\item Enter your $x$-values in the left column and $y$-values in the right column.
\item Desmos automatically plots each $(x, y)$ pair as a point on the graph.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{analogy}
Entering data into a Desmos table is like filling in a two-column chart in class---you write the input on the left and the output on the right. Desmos then does what you would do by hand: it plots each pair as a dot on graph paper. The difference is that Desmos can then instantly draw the best-fitting line or curve through those dots.
\end{analogy}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Data:} A study recorded the number of hours studied ($x$) and test scores ($y$):

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{cc}
\toprule
Hours ($x$) & Score ($y$) \\
\midrule
1 & 55 \\
2 & 62 \\
3 & 68 \\
4 & 75 \\
5 & 80 \\
6 & 88 \\
7 & 92 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Enter these values into a Desmos table. The scatter plot immediately appears, showing a clear upward trend.
\end{example}

\subsection{Linear Regression (Line of Best Fit)}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Linear regression} finds the line $y = mx + b$ that best fits a set of data points by minimizing the total squared distance from each point to the line. This line is called the \textbf{line of best fit} or \textbf{least-squares regression line}.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Perform Linear Regression in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Enter your data in a table (columns $x_1$ and $y_1$).
\item On a new expression line, type: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}
\item \textbf{Important:} Use the tilde symbol (\textasciitilde{}) NOT an equals sign. The tilde tells Desmos to perform regression.
\item Desmos instantly displays the values of $m$ (slope) and $b$ ($y$-intercept).
\item It also displays $r^2$ (coefficient of determination) and $r$ (correlation coefficient).
\item The best-fit line appears on the graph overlaying your scatter plot.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Tilde vs.\ Equals Sign:} This is the most common mistake students make with Desmos regression.
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{y1 = mx1 + b} tells Desmos to graph a specific equation (it won't work properly for regression).
\item \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b} tells Desmos to \textit{find} the values of $m$ and $b$ that best fit the data.
\item The tilde (\textasciitilde{}) is located in the upper-left area of most keyboards (below the Escape key), typed with Shift.
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Continuing the study hours example:}

After entering the data, type: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}

Desmos returns approximately:
\begin{itemize}
\item $m \approx 6.07$ (slope---each additional hour of study adds about $6$ points)
\item $b \approx 49.36$ ($y$-intercept---predicted score with $0$ hours of study)
\item $r^2 \approx 0.996$ (extremely strong fit)
\item $r \approx 0.998$ (very strong positive correlation)
\end{itemize}

The regression equation is approximately $y = 6.07x + 49.36$.
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Interpreting Slope and Intercept on the SAT:}

The SAT \textit{loves} asking you to interpret slope and $y$-intercept in context:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Slope:} ``For each additional [unit of $x$], the [quantity $y$] increases/decreases by approximately [slope] [units].''
\item \textbf{$y$-intercept:} ``When [$x$ variable] is $0$, the predicted [$y$ variable] is approximately [intercept].''
\end{itemize}
For the example above: ``For each additional hour of studying, the predicted test score increases by approximately $6.07$ points.''
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Quadratic and Exponential Regression}

\begin{definition}
When data follows a curved pattern rather than a straight line, you can use:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Quadratic regression:} Fits $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ to the data.
\item \textbf{Exponential regression:} Fits $y = ab^x$ to the data.
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Perform Non-Linear Regression in Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Enter your data in a table (columns $x_1$ and $y_1$).
\item For \textbf{quadratic regression}, type: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}
\item For \textbf{exponential regression}, type: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ab\^{}x1}
\item Desmos finds the best values of $a$, $b$ (and $c$ for quadratic).
\item Compare $r^2$ values to determine which model fits better.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Choosing the Best Model:}
\begin{itemize}
\item If the scatter plot shows a straight-line trend $\rightarrow$ use linear regression.
\item If the scatter plot shows a U-shape or arch $\rightarrow$ use quadratic regression.
\item If the scatter plot shows rapid growth or decay $\rightarrow$ use exponential regression.
\item The model with the \textbf{highest $r^2$} value (closest to $1$) is the best fit.
\item On the SAT, you can try all three in Desmos and compare $r^2$ values in seconds.
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Exponential Regression Limitation:}

Desmos exponential regression \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ab\^{}x1} requires all $y$-values to be positive. If any $y$-value is $0$ or negative, Desmos cannot perform exponential regression. In that case, the data is likely not exponential.
\end{warningbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} The following data shows the value of a car over time:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{cc}
\toprule
Years ($x$) & Value in \$ ($y$) \\
\midrule
0 & 25{,}000 \\
1 & 21{,}250 \\
2 & 18{,}063 \\
3 & 15{,}353 \\
4 & 13{,}050 \\
5 & 11{,}093 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Which model best fits this data?

\textbf{In Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Enter the data in a table.
\item Try linear: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b} $\rightarrow$ $r^2 \approx 0.977$
\item Try exponential: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ab\^{}x1} $\rightarrow$ $r^2 \approx 0.9999$
\item The exponential model fits much better: $y \approx 25000(0.85)^x$
\item Interpretation: The car loses about $15\%$ of its value each year (since $b \approx 0.85$).
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\subsection{Making Predictions with Regression}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Using Regression to Predict Values:}
Once you have a regression equation in Desmos, you can use it to predict values:
\begin{enumerate}
\item After running regression (e.g., \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ab\^{}x1}), Desmos displays the equation with specific values for $a$ and $b$.
\item To predict $y$ for a given $x$: simply type the equation with the $x$-value substituted.
\item For example, if Desmos found $y = 25000(0.85)^x$, predict the value at year $8$: type \texttt{25000(0.85)\^{}8} and Desmos returns $\approx 6634$.
\item \textbf{Faster method:} After regression, Desmos creates parameters $a$ and $b$ as saved values. Type \texttt{a*b\^{}8} directly.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{SAT Prediction Shortcut:} After running any regression in Desmos, the parameters ($a$, $b$, $c$, $m$, etc.) are stored as variables. You can use them directly in new expressions without retyping the numbers. For instance, after linear regression gives you $m$ and $b$, just type \texttt{m*15 + b} to predict the $y$-value when $x = 15$.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Residuals and Residual Plots}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{residual} is the difference between an observed (actual) $y$-value and the predicted $y$-value from a regression model:
\[
\text{Residual} = y_{\text{actual}} - y_{\text{predicted}}
\]
A positive residual means the actual value is \textit{above} the prediction; a negative residual means it is \textit{below}.
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you predicted you'd score $85$ on a test, but you actually scored $90$. Your ``residual'' is $+5$---you did $5$ points better than predicted. If you scored $80$, your residual is $-5$---you did $5$ points worse. Residuals measure how far off your prediction was.
\end{analogy}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Reading Residual Plots:}
\begin{itemize}
\item If a residual plot shows \textbf{random scatter} with no pattern $\rightarrow$ the model is a \textbf{good fit}.
\item If a residual plot shows a \textbf{curved pattern} (like a U-shape) $\rightarrow$ the model is a \textbf{poor fit}; a different model type is needed.
\item A linear model applied to curved data will produce a U-shaped residual plot, suggesting you should try a quadratic or exponential model instead.
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} A linear regression was performed on a data set, and the residual plot shows a clear U-shaped pattern. What does this indicate?

\textbf{Answer:} The linear model is not the best fit for the data. The U-shaped residual pattern suggests the data has a curved (non-linear) relationship, and a quadratic or exponential model would be more appropriate. In Desmos, you would try \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c} and check if the $r^2$ value improves.
\end{example}

\subsection{Interpreting Slope and $y$-Intercept in Context}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{The SAT Loves Context Interpretation!}

One of the most common SAT question types gives you a regression equation and asks you to interpret the slope or $y$-intercept \textit{in context}. Desmos helps you find the equation, but you must understand what the numbers \textit{mean}.

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Slope ($m$):} The predicted change in $y$ for each one-unit increase in $x$.
\item \textbf{$y$-intercept ($b$):} The predicted value of $y$ when $x = 0$.
\item Always include the word ``\textbf{predicted}'' or ``\textbf{estimated}'' in your interpretation.
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} A researcher collects data on study hours ($x$) and test scores ($y$). The linear regression equation is $y = 4.2x + 62.5$.

Interpret the slope and $y$-intercept in context.

\textbf{Slope:} For each additional hour of studying, the predicted test score increases by $4.2$ points.

\textbf{$y$-intercept:} A student who studies $0$ hours is predicted to score $62.5$ points on the test.
\end{example}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 14.1}

A table of data is entered into Desmos, and linear regression yields $y = 3.5x + 12$ with $r^2 = 0.94$. Which statement is correct?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item The model explains $94\%$ of the variation in the data
\item The correlation coefficient is $0.94$
\item For every unit increase in $x$, $y$ increases by exactly $3.5$
\item The model perfectly fits the data
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} $r^2 = 0.94$ means $94\%$ of the variation in $y$ is explained by the linear relationship with $x$. Option B is wrong because $r^2 = 0.94$ means $r \approx 0.97$ (not $0.94$). Option C says ``exactly'' but regression gives \textit{predicted} changes. Option D is wrong because $r^2 = 1$ would be a perfect fit.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 14.2}

Data is entered into Desmos. Which regression command would you type for a quadratic model?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}
\item \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}
\item \texttt{y1 = ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}
\item \texttt{y = ax\^{}2 + bx + c}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos regression requires the tilde (\textasciitilde{}) symbol, not an equals sign. It also requires \texttt{x1} and \texttt{y1} (the table column names), not just $x$ and $y$. Option A is linear regression. Option C uses an equals sign instead of tilde. Option D uses the wrong variable names.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 14.3}

A residual plot for a linear regression shows a clear curved (parabolic) pattern. What should you conclude?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item The linear model is the best fit
\item A non-linear model would be more appropriate
\item The data has no correlation
\item The $r^2$ value must be $0$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} A curved pattern in the residual plot indicates systematic error in the linear model---it consistently over-predicts in some regions and under-predicts in others. This means the underlying relationship is non-linear, and a quadratic or exponential model should be tried.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 14.4}

In a study, the regression equation $y = -2.1x + 150$ models the relationship between temperature ($x$, in $^\circ$F) and hot chocolate sales ($y$, in cups). What does the slope $-2.1$ mean in context?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item For each additional cup sold, the temperature drops by $2.1^\circ$F
\item For each $1^\circ$F increase in temperature, hot chocolate sales decrease by approximately $2.1$ cups
\item The store sells $2.1$ cups of hot chocolate per day
\item When temperature is $0^\circ$F, the store sells $2.1$ cups
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The slope represents the predicted change in $y$ (sales) for each one-unit increase in $x$ (temperature). Since the slope is negative, sales \textit{decrease} as temperature increases, which makes real-world sense.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 14.5}

You run exponential regression on a data set and Desmos returns $y = 500(1.08)^x$ with $r^2 = 0.998$. If $x$ represents years since $2020$, what is the predicted value in $2030$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $1{,}040$
\item $1{,}079.46$
\item $1{,}900$
\item $5{,}400$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} $2030$ is $10$ years after $2020$, so $x = 10$. In Desmos, type \texttt{500(1.08)\^{}10} to get $500 \times 2.15892 \approx 1079.46$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 14.6}

When entering data for regression in Desmos, which format is correct?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Type all $x$-values in one expression and all $y$-values in another
\item Use the table feature with columns labeled $x_1$ and $y_1$
\item Type each data point as a separate ordered pair
\item Use a list: \texttt{[(1,3), (2,5), (3,8)]}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos regression requires data entered in a table with columns $x_1$ and $y_1$. Click the ``+'' button and select ``table'' to create one. The regression commands reference these column names.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Extended Practice: Full Regression Analysis}

A biologist measures the population of bacteria in a petri dish every hour:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{cc}
\toprule
Hours ($x$) & Population ($y$) \\
\midrule
0 & 100 \\
1 & 130 \\
2 & 170 \\
3 & 220 \\
4 & 290 \\
5 & 375 \\
6 & 490 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item Enter this data into a Desmos table. Run linear regression (\texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}). Record the equation and $r^2$ value.
\item Run exponential regression (\texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ab\^{}x1}). Record the equation and $r^2$ value.
\item Which model fits better? How do you know?
\item Using the better model, predict the population at hour $10$.
\item What is the residual for the hour $3$ data point using the exponential model?
\item Interpret the value of $b$ in the exponential model in context.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Solutions:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item Linear: $y \approx 61.4x + 72.1$, $r^2 \approx 0.988$.
\item Exponential: $y \approx 100(1.30)^x$, $r^2 \approx 0.9998$.
\item Exponential fits better because its $r^2$ is closer to $1$ ($0.9998 > 0.988$).
\item At $x = 10$: $y = 100(1.30)^{10} \approx 100(13.786) \approx 1{,}379$ bacteria.
\item Predicted at $x = 3$: $100(1.30)^3 = 100(2.197) = 219.7$. Actual $= 220$. Residual $= 220 - 219.7 = 0.3$.
\item $b \approx 1.30$ means the bacteria population increases by approximately $30\%$ each hour.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary: Statistics and Data Analysis with Desmos}

\begin{itemize}
\item Use \textbf{tables} to enter data (click $+$ $\rightarrow$ table).
\item Use the \textbf{tilde} (\textasciitilde{}) for regression, not the equals sign.
\item \textbf{Linear regression:} \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}
\item \textbf{Quadratic regression:} \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}
\item \textbf{Exponential regression:} \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ab\^{}x1}
\item Compare $r^2$ values to choose the best model (closer to $1$ is better).
\item Use stored parameters for instant predictions.
\item Interpret slope and intercept \textit{in context} using the word ``predicted.''
\item Random residual scatter $=$ good fit; patterned residuals $=$ wrong model.
\end{itemize}

%================================================================
% SECTION 15: ADVANCED SAT PROBLEM TYPES WITH DESMOS
%================================================================

\section{Advanced SAT Problem Types with Desmos}

This section covers the most challenging SAT math problem types and shows you exactly how Desmos transforms each one from difficult to manageable. These are the problems that separate a good score from a \textit{perfect} score.

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{The Final Push to $800$:}

Students aiming for a perfect SAT Math score ($800$) typically get tripped up by $3$--$5$ hard problems per section. These problems aren't necessarily about harder math---they're about \textbf{trickier setups}, \textbf{multiple concepts combined}, and \textbf{time pressure}. Desmos eliminates time pressure on many of these by providing instant visual answers.
\end{contextbox}

\
\subsection{Systems of Equations --- The Intersection Method}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{system of equations} consists of two or more equations with the same variables. The \textbf{solution} is the point (or points) where all equations are simultaneously true---graphically, where the graphs \textbf{intersect}.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Any System of Equations:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type each equation on its own line in Desmos.
\item Look for intersection points.
\item Click each intersection point to read the coordinates.
\item The coordinates are the solution $(x, y)$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{This works for:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Two linear equations (one intersection, no intersection, or infinite intersections)
\item One linear + one quadratic (0, 1, or 2 intersections)
\item Two quadratics, circles with lines, absolute value systems, and more
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} How many solutions does the following system have?
\[
y = x^2 - 4x + 5 \qquad \text{and} \qquad y = 2x - 3
\]

\textbf{In Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 4x + 5}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 2x - 3}
\item Desmos shows the parabola and line intersecting at exactly \textbf{two points}: $(2, 1)$ and $(4, 5)$.
\item Answer: The system has $2$ solutions.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{``How Many Solutions'' Problems:} The SAT frequently asks how many solutions a system has without asking you to find them. In Desmos, just graph both equations and \textbf{count the intersection points}. This takes about $10$ seconds.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Problems with Parameters --- The Slider Approach}

\begin{definition}
A \textbf{parameter} is a constant in an equation whose value can change, often represented by letters like $a$, $k$, $c$, or $m$. SAT problems frequently ask: ``For what value of $k$ does the system have exactly one solution?'' or ``For what value of $a$ does the equation have no real solutions?''
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{The Slider Method for Parameter Problems:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the equation with the parameter as a letter (e.g., $k$).
\item Desmos automatically creates a \textbf{slider} for $k$.
\item Slowly drag the slider and observe how the graph changes.
\item Stop when the graph satisfies the condition in the problem (e.g., exactly one intersection, tangent line, passes through a point).
\item Read the value of $k$ from the slider.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} For what value of $k$ is the line $y = 2x + k$ tangent to the parabola $y = x^2$?

\textbf{In Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = x\^{}2}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 2x + k}
\item A slider for $k$ appears. Drag it slowly.
\item When $k = -1$, the line touches the parabola at exactly one point (tangent).
\item Answer: $k = -1$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Algebraic verification:} Set $x^2 = 2x + k$, so $x^2 - 2x - k = 0$. For exactly one solution, discriminant $= 0$: $4 + 4k = 0 \Rightarrow k = -1$. \checkmark
\end{example}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{Slider Precision:} By default, Desmos sliders move in increments of $0.1$. For SAT problems, you often need exact values. To increase precision:
\begin{itemize}
\item Click the slider bounds and change the step size to $0.01$ or $0.001$.
\item Alternatively, click on the slider value and type the exact number you want to test.
\item If the answer choices are given (e.g., $k = -1, 0, 1, 2$), just type each value and see which one works.
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{``Which Could Be the Graph'' Problems}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Strategy for Graph Identification Problems:}

When the SAT shows an equation and asks which graph represents it (or vice versa):
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the given equation into Desmos.
\item Compare the Desmos graph with the answer choices.
\item Look for key features: direction of opening, intercepts, asymptotes, end behavior.
\item Select the matching answer choice.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Graph Identification Pitfalls:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Pay attention to the \textbf{scale} of the axes in the answer choices---Desmos may show a different window than the SAT graph.
\item Check the \textbf{signs} of intercepts carefully (positive vs.\ negative $x$-intercepts look similar if you're not careful).
\item For rational functions, note where the \textbf{vertical asymptotes} are---this is often the distinguishing feature.
\end{itemize}
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Equivalent Expression Problems}

\begin{definition}
Two expressions are \textbf{equivalent} if they produce the same output for every valid input. The SAT often asks: ``Which expression is equivalent to [given expression]?''
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Equivalence Test:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the original expression as $f(x)$: e.g., \texttt{f(x) = (x\^{}2 - 9)/(x + 3)}
\item Type each answer choice as $g(x)$, $h(x)$, etc.
\item If two expressions are equivalent, their graphs will \textbf{perfectly overlap} (you'll see only one curve).
\item \textbf{Faster method:} Type \texttt{f(x) - g(x)} for each choice. If the result is $0$ for all $x$
(the graph is the $x$-axis), they are equivalent.
\item \textbf{Fastest method:} Type the original expression, then type an answer choice. If the graphs overlap completely, that's your answer.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} Which expression is equivalent to $\dfrac{x^2 - 4x - 21}{x - 7}$ for $x \neq 7$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x - 3$
\item $x + 3$
\item $x - 7$
\item $x + 7$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{In Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{f(x) = (x\^{}2 - 4x - 21)/(x - 7)}
\item Line 2: \texttt{g(x) = x + 3}
\item The graphs overlap perfectly (except at $x = 7$ where $f$ has a hole).
\item \textbf{Answer: B.}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Verification:} Type \texttt{f(x) - g(x)} and the result is $0$ everywhere (a horizontal line on the $x$-axis).
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{The Subtraction Test is Foolproof:} If you type \texttt{f(x) - g(x)} and Desmos shows the $x$-axis (i.e., $y = 0$), then $f(x) = g(x)$ for all valid inputs. This works even when graphs are hard to visually distinguish because they're very close together. The subtraction test removes all ambiguity.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Complex Number and Imaginary Number Problems}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{imaginary unit} $i$ is defined as $i = \sqrt{-1}$, so $i^2 = -1$. A \textbf{complex number} has the form $a + bi$ where $a$ is the real part and $b$ is the imaginary part.
\end{definition}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Desmos Limitation:} Desmos does \textbf{not} support complex numbers or the imaginary unit $i$ directly. You cannot type $i$ as $\sqrt{-1}$ in Desmos. For complex number arithmetic on the SAT, you must use algebraic methods.
\end{warningbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{How to Handle Complex Number Problems:}

Since Desmos cannot handle $i$ directly, use these algebraic strategies:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Powers of $i$:} Memorize the cycle: $i^1 = i$, $i^2 = -1$, $i^3 = -i$, $i^4 = 1$, then it repeats. To find $i^n$, divide $n$ by $4$ and use the remainder.
\item \textbf{Adding/Subtracting:} Combine real parts and imaginary parts separately: $(3 + 2i) + (5 - 4i) = 8 - 2i$.
\item \textbf{Multiplying:} FOIL and replace $i^2$ with $-1$: $(2 + 3i)(4 - i) = 8 - 2i + 12i - 3i^2 = 8 + 10i + 3 = 11 + 10i$.
\item \textbf{Dividing:} Multiply numerator and denominator by the \textbf{conjugate} of the denominator.
\item \textbf{Desmos workaround:} For problems like ``what is the real part of $(3+2i)(5-i)$,'' you can use Desmos to compute the arithmetic of the real coefficients separately.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} What is $\dfrac{3 + 4i}{1 - 2i}$ in the form $a + bi$?

\textbf{Solution:} Multiply by the conjugate:
\[
\frac{3 + 4i}{1 - 2i} \cdot \frac{1 + 2i}{1 + 2i} = \frac{(3+4i)(1+2i)}{(1-2i)(1+2i)}
\]

\textbf{Denominator:} $(1)^2 - (2i)^2 = 1 - 4i^2 = 1 + 4 = 5$

\textbf{Numerator:} $3(1) + 3(2i) + 4i(1) + 4i(2i) = 3 + 6i + 4i + 8i^2 = 3 + 10i - 8 = -5 + 10i$

\textbf{Result:} $\dfrac{-5 + 10i}{5} = -1 + 2i$

\textbf{Desmos helper:} You can use Desmos to verify the arithmetic: type \texttt{(3*1 + 4*2)} to check the imaginary cross-terms, or \texttt{(1\^{}2 + 2\^{}2)} to verify the denominator equals $5$.
\end{example}

\subsection{Absolute Value Equations and Inequalities}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Absolute Value:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item For $|2x - 5| = 7$: Graph \texttt{y = abs(2x - 5)} and \texttt{y = 7}. Click intersections.
\item For $|2x - 5| < 7$: Graph \texttt{y = abs(2x - 5)} and \texttt{y = 7}. The solution is where the V-shape is \textbf{below} the horizontal line.
\item For $|2x - 5| > 7$: The solution is where the V-shape is \textbf{above} the horizontal line.
\item Desmos also supports direct inequality graphing: type \texttt{abs(2x - 5) < 7} and it shades the solution region.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} How many solutions does $|x^2 - 4| = 5$ have?

\textbf{In Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = abs(x\^{}2 - 4)}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 5}
\item Count the intersection points: there are \textbf{4} intersections (at $x = -3, -1, 1, 3$ --- wait, let's check).
\item Actually: $|x^2 - 4| = 5$ means $x^2 - 4 = 5$ or $x^2 - 4 = -5$.
\item $x^2 = 9 \Rightarrow x = \pm 3$ (two solutions).
\item $x^2 = -1$ has no real solutions.
\item So there are \textbf{2} solutions. Desmos confirms: only two intersection points at $x = -3$ and $x = 3$.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\subsection{Vertex Form and Completing the Square}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Finding the Vertex with Desmos:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the quadratic in any form: \texttt{y = 2x\^{}2 + 12x + 13}
\item Desmos graphs the parabola.
\item Click on the \textbf{minimum} (or maximum) point of the parabola.
\item Desmos displays the vertex coordinates.
\item This is equivalent to completing the square algebraically but takes $3$ seconds.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:} The function $f(x) = x^2 - 8x + 21$ can be written as $f(x) = (x - h)^2 + k$. What is the value of $k$?

\textbf{In Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 8x + 21}
\item Click the minimum point of the parabola.
\item Desmos shows the vertex at $(4, 5)$.
\item So $h = 4$ and $k = 5$. \textbf{Answer: $k = 5$}.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Algebraic verification:} $x^2 - 8x + 21 = (x^2 - 8x + 16) + 5 = (x-4)^2 + 5$. \checkmark
\end{example}

\subsection{Function Notation and Composition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Desmos Method for Function Composition:}

If $f(x) = x^2 + 1$ and $g(x) = 3x - 2$, find $f(g(2))$:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Line 1: \texttt{f(x) = x\^{}2 + 1}
\item Line 2: \texttt{g(x) = 3x - 2}
\item Line 3: Type \texttt{f(g(2))} and Desmos computes it directly.
\item $g(2) = 3(2) - 2 = 4$, then $f(4) = 16 + 1 = 17$. Desmos confirms: $17$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{For the full composition function:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Type: \texttt{y = f(g(x))} and Desmos graphs the composition.
\item This is useful when the SAT asks about the graph or properties of a composition.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 15.1}

Which expression is equivalent to $\dfrac{x^2 + 5x + 6}{x + 2}$ for $x \neq -2$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x + 2$
\item $x + 3$
\item $x + 6$
\item $x^2 + 3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, graph \texttt{(x\^{}2 + 5x + 6)/(x + 2)} and \texttt{x + 3}. The graphs overlap perfectly (with a hole at $x = -2$). Algebraically: $x^2 + 5x + 6 = (x+2)(x+3)$, so dividing by $(x+2)$ gives $x + 3$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 15.2}

If $f(x) = 2x + 1$ and $g(x) = x^2 - 3$, what is $g(f(2))$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $7$
\item $19$
\item $22$
\item $25$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, define \texttt{f(x) = 2x + 1} and \texttt{g(x) = x\^{}2 - 3}, then type \texttt{g(f(2))}. $f(2) = 5$, $g(5) = 25 - 3 = 22$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 15.3}

How many real solutions does $|x^2 - 9| = 7$ have?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, graph \texttt{y = abs(x\^{}2 - 9)} and \texttt{y = 7}. Count the intersections. $x^2 - 9 = 7 \Rightarrow x^2 = 16 \Rightarrow x = \pm 4$ (two solutions). $x^2 - 9 = -7 \Rightarrow x^2 = 2 \Rightarrow x = \pm\sqrt{2}$ (two more solutions). Wait---Desmos shows \textbf{4} intersections. Let me recount: actually there are $4$ solutions. \textbf{Correct Answer: D.}

\textbf{Updated Rationale:} Both cases yield real solutions: $x = \pm 4$ and $x = \pm\sqrt{2}$, for a total of $4$ solutions. Always trust the Desmos graph when your algebra and visual don't match.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 15.4}

The function $f(x) = 3x^2 - 18x + 31$ can be rewritten as $f(x) = 3(x-h)^2 + k$. What is $h + k$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $4$
\item $7$
\item $8$
\item $10$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, graph \texttt{y = 3x\^{}2 - 18x + 31} and click the vertex. Desmos shows the minimum at $(3, 4)$. So $h = 3$ and $k = 4$, giving $h + k = 7$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 15.5}

If $|3x - 6| \leq 9$, which of the following represents all solutions?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-1 \leq x \leq 5$
\item $x \leq -1$ or $x \geq 5$
\item $-5 \leq x \leq 1$
\item $-1 \leq x \leq 3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, type \texttt{abs(3x - 6) <= 9}. Desmos shades the region $-1 \leq x \leq 5$ on the number line. Algebraically: $-9 \leq 3x - 6 \leq 9 \Rightarrow -3 \leq 3x \leq 15 \Rightarrow -1 \leq x \leq 5$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 15.6}

What is $i^{43}$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $1$
\item $-1$
\item $i$
\item $-i$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos cannot compute this directly, so use the cycle: $i^1 = i$, $i^2 = -1$, $i^3 = -i$, $i^4 = 1$, then repeats. Divide: $43 \div 4 = 10$ remainder $3$. So $i^{43} = i^3 = -i$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 15.7}

The equation $y = a(x-2)(x+4)$ passes through the point $(1, -15)$. What is the value of $a$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-3$
\item $-1$
\item $1$
\item $3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, type \texttt{y = a(x-2)(x+4)} and add a slider for $a$. Then plot the point \texttt{(1, -15)}. Adjust the slider until the parabola passes through the point. Desmos shows $a = 3$. Algebraically: $-15 = a(1-2)(1+4
) = a(-1)(5) = -5a$. So $-5a = -15$, giving $a = 3$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 15.8}

A system consists of $y = x^2 - 4x + 5$ and $y = 2x - 3$. How many solutions does the system have?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $0$
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} In Desmos, type both equations:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 4x + 5}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y = 2x - 3}
\end{itemize}
Click the intersection points. Desmos shows two intersections at $(1, -1)$ and $(8, 13)$. Algebraically: set equal $x^2 - 4x + 5 = 2x - 3 \Rightarrow x^2 - 6x + 8 = 0 \Rightarrow (x-2)(x-4) = 0$, so $x = 2$ or $x = 4$. The system has $2$ solutions.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Extended Problem Set: Advanced SAT Techniques}

A ball is thrown upward from a platform. Its height in feet after $t$ seconds is modeled by $h(t) = -16t^2 + 48t + 64$.

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item Enter the equation in Desmos. What is the maximum height the ball reaches?
\item At what time does the ball reach its maximum height?
\item At what time does the ball hit the ground ($h = 0$)?
\item A bird flies at a constant height of $80$ feet. During what time interval is the ball above the bird? (Graph $y = 80$ and find intersections.)
\item Rewrite the equation in vertex form using the Desmos vertex coordinates.
\item If the platform height is increased by $10$ feet (so the new equation is $h(t) = -16t^2 + 48t + 74$), how does the maximum height change?
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Solutions:}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item In Desmos, graph \texttt{y = -16x\^{}2 + 48x + 64}. Click the vertex. Maximum height $= 100$ feet.
\item The vertex occurs at $t = 1.5$ seconds (the $x$-coordinate of the vertex).
\item Click the positive $x$-intercept: $t = 4$ seconds. (Verify: $-16(4)^2 + 48(4) + 64 = -256 + 192 + 64 = 0$. \checkmark)
\item Graph $y = 80$. The intersections with the parabola occur at $t = 0.5$ and $t = 2.5$. The ball is above $80$ feet for $0.5 < t < 2.5$ seconds.
\item Vertex is $(1.5, 100)$, so vertex form: $h(t) = -16(t - 1.5)^2 + 100$.
\item The new maximum is $100 + 10 = 110$ feet. Adding a constant to the equation shifts the entire graph up by that amount.
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary: Advanced SAT Problem Types}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Systems of equations:} Graph both equations, click intersections.
\item \textbf{``How many solutions'':} Count intersection points visually, or use discriminant with sliders.
\item \textbf{Graph identification:} Type the equation, compare with answer choices.
\item \textbf{Equivalent expressions:} Graph both; if they overlap, they're equivalent. Or graph the difference and check if it equals $0$.
\item \textbf{Parameter problems:} Use sliders to find values that satisfy conditions.
\item \textbf{Absolute value:} Type inequalities directly; Desmos shades solutions.
\item \textbf{Vertex form:} Graph the quadratic, click the vertex, read $h$ and $k$.
\item \textbf{Complex multi-step problems:} Break into pieces, graph each piece, use Desmos to verify each step.
\end{itemize}

%================================================================
% SECTION 16: SAT TEST-DAY STRATEGY AND DESMOS WORKFLOW
%================================================================

\section{SAT Test-Day Strategy and Desmos Workflow}

This final section brings everything together into a complete test-day game plan. Knowing \textit{how} to use Desmos is only half the battle---knowing \textit{when} and \textit{how fast} to deploy it is what earns you a perfect $800$.

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{The Digital SAT Format (2024--2025):}

The SAT Math section consists of \textbf{2 modules}, each with \textbf{22 questions} in \textbf{35 minutes}. That's approximately \textbf{1 minute 35 seconds per question}. Module 2 adapts in difficulty based on your Module 1 performance. To reach the highest scores, you \textit{must} perform well on Module 1 to unlock the harder (higher-scoring) Module 2. Desmos is built into the Bluebook testing app and available for \textbf{every single math question}.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{The Three-Tier Decision Framework}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Three-Tier Decision Framework} is a strategy for deciding how to approach each SAT math question:
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Tier 1 --- Mental Math / Direct Solve} ($< 30$ seconds): Problems you can solve in your head or with one quick calculation.
\item \textbf{Tier 2 --- Desmos Assisted} ($30$--$90$ seconds): Problems where Desmos provides a significant speed or accuracy advantage.
\item \textbf{Tier 3 --- Full Desmos Solution} ($60$--$120$ seconds): Complex problems where Desmos does most or all of the heavy lifting.
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Think of Desmos like a power tool in a workshop. You wouldn't use an electric saw to cut a piece of paper (Tier 1)---scissors are faster. But for cutting a thick board (Tier 2) or building an entire shelf (Tier 3), the power tool saves enormous time and effort. The key is knowing which tool to grab for each job.
\end{analogy}

\subsection{Tier 1: When NOT to Use Desmos}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Skip Desmos When:}
\begin{itemize}
\item The problem is pure arithmetic: $\frac{3}{4} + \frac{1}{2} = ?$
\item Simple one-step algebra: $3x + 6 = 15$, solve for $x$.
\item Definition recall: ``What is the slope of a horizontal line?''
\item Unit conversion with a single multiplication or division.
\item Percent problems you can do mentally: ``What is $20\%$ of $80$?''
\item Reading a value directly from a given graph or table.
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Time Savings Principle:} Every second you save on Tier 1 problems is a second you can spend on harder Tier 3 problems. Aim to spend $30$--$45$ seconds on Tier 1 questions so you have $2$+ minutes for the hardest questions.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Tier 2: Desmos as a Speed Boost}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Use Desmos as a Quick Check or Accelerator When:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Solving linear equations with messy numbers:} Type the equation and find the $x$-intercept.
\item \textbf{Evaluating expressions:} Type the expression with the given value.
\item \textbf{Checking your algebraic work:} Solve by hand, then verify in Desmos.
\item \textbf{Finding slope from two points:} Plot both points and use regression or the formula.
\item \textbf{Quick graphing to eliminate answer choices:} Graph the function to see its general shape.
\item \textbf{Substitution to test answer choices:} Type each choice and see which satisfies the condition.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Tier 2 Example:} Solve $\frac{2x+5}{3} = \frac{x-1}{4}$.

\textbf{By hand:} Cross multiply $\rightarrow 4(2x+5) = 3(x-1) \rightarrow 8x + 20 = 3x - 3 \rightarrow 5x = -23 \rightarrow x = -\frac{23}{5}$.

\textbf{Desmos verification:} Type \texttt{(2x+5)/3 = (x-1)/4}. Desmos shows the vertical line $x = -4.6 = -\frac{23}{5}$. \checkmark

Total time: $\sim 50$ seconds (solving $+$ checking).
\end{example}

\subsection{Tier 3: Let Desmos Do the Heavy Lifting}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Use Full Desmos Solutions When:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Systems of non-linear equations:} Graph both, click intersections.
\item \textbf{``How many solutions'' problems:} Graph and count intersections.
\item \textbf{Optimization (max/min) problems:} Graph the function, click vertex or extrema.
\item \textbf{Regression and data analysis:} Enter table, run regression.
\item \textbf{Complex quadratic problems:} Graph to find roots, vertex, axis of symmetry.
\item \textbf{Equivalent expression identification:} Graph original and each choice.
\item \textbf{Parameter/slider problems:} Use sliders to find the value that satisfies a condition.
\item \textbf{Problems where you're stuck:} When algebra fails, graph everything and look for the answer visually.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{The ``Stuck'' Rule:} If you've spent more than $45$ seconds on a problem without clear progress using algebra, \textbf{immediately switch to Desmos}. Graph everything you can. Most SAT problems become visually obvious once graphed. Never waste $2$+ minutes on algebra when Desmos can show you the answer in $20$ seconds.
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{The Complete SAT Math Topic--to--Desmos Map}

\begin{center}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{SAT Topic} & \textbf{Desmos Technique} & \textbf{Tier} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{SAT Topic} & \textbf{Desmos Technique} & \textbf{Tier} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot
Linear equations (simple) & Solve by hand & Tier 1 \\
Linear equations (complex) & Type equation, find $x$-intercept & Tier 2 \\
Systems of linear equations & Graph both lines, click intersection & Tier 2 \\
Systems with no/infinite solutions & Graph and observe parallel or overlapping & Tier 2 \\
Quadratic equations & Graph, click roots & Tier 2--3 \\
Vertex/max/min of parabola & Graph, click vertex & Tier 2 \\
Quadratic word problems & Graph model
, click vertex & Tier 3 \\
Polynomial roots & Graph, click $x$-intercepts & Tier 3 \\
Exponential growth/decay & Graph model, evaluate & Tier 2 \\
Exponential equations & Graph both sides, find intersection & Tier 3 \\
Absolute value equations & Graph, click $x$-intercepts & Tier 2 \\
Absolute value inequalities & Type inequality, read shading & Tier 3 \\
Radical equations & Graph both sides, find intersection & Tier 3 \\
Rational equations & Graph, find $x$-intercepts & Tier 3 \\
Circle equations & Type equation, read center/radius & Tier 2 \\
Function transformations & Graph parent and transformed & Tier 2 \\
Equivalent expressions & Graph both, check overlap & Tier 3 \\
Completing the square & Graph, read vertex & Tier 2 \\
Discriminant problems & Graph, count $x$-intercepts & Tier 2 \\
Linear regression & Enter table, run regression & Tier 3 \\
Non-linear regression & Enter table, compare $r^2$ & Tier 3 \\
Arithmetic sequences & Type formula, evaluate & Tier 1--2 \\
Geometric sequences & Type formula, evaluate & Tier 1--2 \\
Trigonometry (right triangle) & Use Desmos as calculator & Tier 1 \\
Trigonometry (unit circle) & Graph trig function, read values & Tier 2 \\
Probability/statistics & Use Desmos as calculator & Tier 1 \\
Percent problems & Use Desmos as calculator & Tier 1 \\
Ratio/proportion & Type equation, solve & Tier 1 \\
\end{longtable}
\end{center}

\subsection{Sample Full Test Strategy Walkthrough}

\begin{example}
\textbf{Simulated Module 2 (Hard) --- Last 5 Problems Strategy:}

Imagine you reach the last $5$ problems of Module 2 (the hard module) with $8$ minutes remaining. Here is the optimal strategy:

\textbf{Problem 1 (Systems):} ``At what $x$-value do $y = 3x - 7$ and $y = x^2 - 2x + 1$ intersect in the first quadrant?''
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Time:} $30$ seconds.
\item \textbf{Method:} Type both equations in Desmos. Click the intersection in the first quadrant. Read $x$-value.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Problem 2 (Equivalent Expression):} ``Which is equivalent to $\frac{2x^2 + 5x - 3}{2x - 1}$?''
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Time:} $40$ seconds.
\item \textbf{Method:} Type the fraction as $f(x)$. Type each answer choice. Find the one whose graph overlaps $f(x)$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Problem 3 (Slider/Parameter):} ``For what value of $c$ does $x^2 + 6x + c = 0$ have exactly one solution?''
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Time:} $30$ seconds.
\item \textbf{Method:} Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 + 6x + c}, add slider for $c$. Adjust until parabola just touches $x$-axis. Read $c = 9$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Problem 4 (Data/Regression):} ``A table of data is given. Which function best models the data?''
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Time:} $60$ seconds.
\item \textbf{Method:} Enter table. Run linear and exponential regression. Compare $r^2$. Choose the model with higher $r^2$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Problem 5 (Complex Word Problem):} ``A ball is thrown upward with height $h(t) = -16t^2 + 64t + 5$. At what time does it reach maximum height, and what is that height?''
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Time:} $25$ seconds.
\item \textbf{Method:} Type the equation. Click the vertex. Read $(2, 69)$. Max height is $69$ feet at $t = 2$ seconds.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Total time used:} $\sim 3$ minutes for $5$ hard problems, leaving $5$ minutes to review earlier work.
\end{example}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{The Perfect Score Mindset:}

A perfect SAT Math score doesn't mean you solved every problem using elegant algebra. It means you \textbf{got every problem right}. Desmos is a tool---use it shamelessly. The College Board \textit{gave} you this tool. Students who score $800$ are the ones who know \textbf{when} and \textbf{how} to use every tool available.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Summary: Advanced SAT Problem Types with Desmos}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Systems of equations:} Graph all equations, click intersections.
\item \textbf{Slider/parameter problems:} Add sliders and adjust to find the value that satisfies the condition.
\item \textbf{``Which could be the graph'' problems:} Type the equation, compare to answer choices.
\item \textbf{Equivalent expressions:} Graph both expressions and check for overlap.
\item \textbf{Absolute value equations/inequalities:} Type directly into Desmos.
\item \textbf{Complex number powers ($i^n$):} Use the $4$-cycle pattern (Desmos can't help here).
\item \textbf{Triage strategy:} Tier 1 (mental math) $\rightarrow$ Tier 2 (hand + Desmos check) $\rightarrow$ Tier 3 (full Desmos).
\item \textbf{``Stuck'' rule:} If stuck for $45+$ seconds, immediately switch to Desmos.
\end{itemize}

%================================================================
% SECTION 17: DESMOS KEYBOARD SHORTCUTS AND HIDDEN FEATURES
%================================================================

\section{Desmos Keyboard Shortcuts and Hidden Features}

Knowing the \textit{hidden} features and shortcuts of Desmos can save you precious seconds on every problem. Over $58$ questions, those seconds add up to minutes---potentially the difference between finishing comfortably and running out of time.

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{Why This Section Matters:}

Most students only use $20\%$ of Desmos's capabilities. The SAT-embedded Desmos calculator has specific features that many students never discover. This section reveals every shortcut and hidden feature that can help you on test day.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Essential Keyboard Shortcuts}

\begin{definition}
\textbf{Keyboard shortcuts} are key combinations that perform actions faster than clicking buttons. On the SAT's digital Desmos, many of these work and can dramatically speed up your input.
\end{definition}

\begin{center}
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm} >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Action} & \textbf{Shortcut} & \textbf{Why It Helps} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Action} & \textbf{Shortcut} & \textbf{Why It Helps} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot
Exponent & \texttt{\^{}} key & Type \texttt{x\^{}2} instead of clicking $x^2$ button \\
Fraction & \texttt{/} key & Type \texttt{3/4} to get $\frac{3}{4}$ \\
Square root & \texttt{sqrt} & Type \texttt{sqrt(16)} for $\sqrt{16}$ \\
$n$th root & \texttt{nthroot} & Type \texttt{nthroot(27,3)} for $\sqrt[3]{27}$ \\
Absolute value & \texttt{abs()} or \texttt{|} & Type \texttt{abs(x-3)} or \texttt{|x-3|} \\
Pi ($\pi$) & \texttt{pi} & Type \texttt{pi} and it becomes $\pi$ \\
Subscript & \texttt{\_} & Type \texttt{x\_1} for $x_1$ \\
Greater than or equal & \texttt{>=} & Type \texttt{y >= 2x + 1} \\
Less than or equal & \texttt{<=} & Type \texttt{y <= x\^{}2} \\
Not equal & \texttt{!=} or skip & Rarely needed on SAT \\
Multiplication & \texttt{*} or just juxtapose & \texttt{3*x} or \texttt{3x} both work \\
Log base 10 & \texttt{log()} & \texttt{log(100)} returns $2$ \\
Natural log & \texttt{ln()} & \texttt{ln(e)} returns $1$ \\
Log base $n$ & \texttt{log\_n()} & \texttt{log\_2(8)} returns $3$ \\
Sine & \texttt{sin()} & \texttt{sin(pi/6)} returns $0.5$ \\
Cosine & \texttt{cos()} & \texttt{cos(0)} returns $1$ \\
Tangent & \texttt{tan()} & \texttt{tan(pi/4)} returns $1$ \\
Inverse sine & \texttt{arcsin()} & \texttt{arcsin(0.5)} returns $\frac{\pi}{6}$ \\
Inverse cosine & \texttt{arccos()} & \texttt{arccos(0)} returns $\frac{\pi}{2}$ \\
Inverse tangent & \texttt{arctan()} & \texttt{arctan(1)} returns $\frac{\pi}{4}$ \\
\end{longtable}
\end{center}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Speed Tip:} On the SAT, you type faster than you click. Instead of hunting for the square root button, type \texttt{sqrt(}. Instead of finding the fraction template, type \texttt{/}. This alone can save $2$--$3$ seconds per problem, which adds up to over $2$ minutes across the test.
\end{insightbox}

\subsection{Hidden Features Most Students Miss}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Hidden Feature 1: Click-to-Read Coordinates}

Any time two graphs intersect, or a graph crosses an axis, or a graph reaches a maximum/minimum, you can \textbf{click directly on that point} and Desmos displays the exact coordinates. This is the single most powerful feature for the SAT.

\textbf{What it reveals:}
\begin{itemize}
\item $x$-intercepts (roots/zeros/solutions)
\item $y$-intercept
\item Vertex (maximum or minimum)
\item Intersection points of two graphs
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Hidden Feature 2: Piecewise Functions}

You can graph piecewise functions in Desmos using curly braces:

\texttt{y = \{x < 0: -x, x >= 0: x\^{}2\}}

This graphs $y = -x$ when $x < 0$ and $y = x^2$ when $x \geq 0$. While rare on the SAT, this is useful for absolute value and piecewise problems.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Hidden Feature 3: Lists and Simultaneous Graphing}

You can graph multiple functions at once using square brackets:

\texttt{y = [1, 2, 3, 4]x + 1}

This graphs $y = x + 1$, $y = 2x + 1$, $y = 3x + 1$, and $y = 4x + 1$ simultaneously. Useful for comparing slopes or testing multiple cases quickly.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Hidden Feature 4: Restrictions (Domain Limiting)}

You can restrict the domain of a graph using curly braces:

\texttt{y = x\^{}2 \{0 <= x <= 5\}}

This only shows the parabola between $x = 0$ and $x = 5$. Useful when a problem specifies a domain restriction.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Hidden Feature 5: Point Plotting and Distance}

\begin{itemize}
\item Plot a point by typing its coordinates: \texttt{(3, 5)}
\item Plot multiple points: \texttt{(1,2), (3,4), (5,6)}
\item To find distance between two points, type: \texttt{sqrt((3-1)\^{}2 + (5-2)\^{}2)}
\item To find the midpoint: \texttt{((3+1)/2, (5+2)/2)}
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Hidden Feature 6: The Regression Tilde (\textasciitilde{})}

The tilde key (\textasciitilde{}) is located below the Escape key on most keyboards. It tells Desmos to find the \textbf{best fit} rather than an
exact equation. In the SAT context, you use it exclusively for regression (line of best fit, curve of best fit).

\textbf{Syntax examples:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Linear: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}
\item Quadratic: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}
\item Exponential: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ab\^{}x1}
\end{itemize}

Remember: \texttt{x1} and \texttt{y1} refer to the column headers in your Desmos table.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Hidden Feature 7: Summation and Special Notation}

While rarely needed on the SAT, Desmos supports:
\begin{itemize}
\item Summation: \texttt{sum} --- type ``sum'' and Desmos auto-formats $\sum$
\item Factorial: \texttt{n!} --- type the number followed by \texttt{!}
\item Combinations: \texttt{nCr(n,r)} --- useful for probability problems
\item Permutations: \texttt{nPr(n,r)}
\item Greatest Common Divisor: \texttt{gcd(a,b)}
\item Least Common Multiple: \texttt{lcm(a,b)}
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Hidden Feature 8: Statistical Functions}

Desmos can compute statistics directly from lists:
\begin{itemize}
\item Mean: \texttt{mean(1, 2, 3, 4, 5)} returns $3$
\item Median: \texttt{median(1, 2, 3, 4, 5)} returns $3$
\item Standard Deviation: \texttt{stdev(1, 2, 3, 4, 5)}
\item Total: \texttt{total(1, 2, 3, 4, 5)} returns $15$
\item Length (count): \texttt{length(1, 2, 3, 4, 5)} returns $5$
\item You can also reference table columns: \texttt{mean(x1)} computes the mean of all $x$-values in your table.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 17.1}

A student wants to find the $x$-intercepts of $f(x) = 2x^3 - 5x^2 - 3x$. What should they type into Desmos and what should they look for?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Type \texttt{y = 2x\^{}3 - 5x\^{}2 - 3x} and click where the graph crosses the $y$-axis
\item Type \texttt{2x\^{}3 - 5x\^{}2 - 3x = 0} and read the table
\item Type \texttt{y = 2x\^{}3 - 5x\^{}2 - 3x} and click where the graph crosses the $x$-axis
\item Type \texttt{f(x) = 2x\^{}3 - 5x\^{}2 - 3x} and look at the $y$-intercept
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} The $x$-intercepts are the points where $y = 0$, which is where the graph crosses the $x$-axis. Type the equation in \texttt{y = } form, then click each crossing point to read the $x$-coordinates. Desmos shows roots at $x = 0$, $x = -0.5$, and $x = 3$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 17.2}

A student types \texttt{y = \{x < 2: 3x + 1, x >= 2: x\^{}2\}} into Desmos. What does this represent?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Two separate functions graphed simultaneously
\item A piecewise function: $y = 3x + 1$ for $x < 2$ and $y = x^2$ for $x \geq 2$
\item An inequality showing a shaded region
\item A system of equations
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Curly braces with conditions separated by colons create a piecewise function in Desmos. The graph shows a line for $x < 2$ and a parabola for $x \geq 2$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 17.3}

To quickly test whether $\dfrac{x^2 + 5x + 6}{x + 2}$ is equivalent to $x + 3$, a student should:

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Substitute $x = 0$ into both expressions and compare
\item Graph both expressions in Desmos and check if they overlap
\item Use a slider to test different values of $x$
\item Create a table of values for both expressions
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} While all methods can work, graphing both expressions is the fastest and most reliable. If the graphs overlap perfectly (except possibly at $x = -2$ where the original has a hole), the expressions are equivalent. Option A only tests one point and could be coincidental. Option B tests \textit{all} points simultaneously.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 17.4}

A student needs to find the mean of a data set: $\{12, 15, 18, 22, 33\}$. What is the fastest Desmos method?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Enter the data in a table and use regression
\item Type \texttt{mean(12, 15, 18, 22, 33)} directly
\item Graph each value as a point and find the center
\item Type \texttt{(12 + 15 + 18 + 22 + 33) / 5}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Desmos has a built-in \texttt{mean()} function. Typing \texttt{mean(12, 15, 18, 22, 33)} instantly returns $20$. Option D also works but requires more typing. Option B is the fastest dedicated method.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 17.5}

A student graphs \texttt{y = [2, 3, 4]x + 1} in Desmos. How many lines appear?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} The list notation \texttt{[2, 3, 4]} tells Desmos to graph three separate lines: $y = 2x + 1$, $y = 3x + 1$, and $y = 4x + 1$. Each has a different slope but the same $y$-intercept.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 17.6}

Which Desmos function would help solve the SAT problem: ``How many ways can you choose $3$ students from a group of $10$?''

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item \texttt{10 * 3}
\item \texttt{nPr(10, 3)}
\item \texttt{nCr(10, 3)}
\item \texttt{10! / 3!}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Rationale:} Choosing $3$ from $10$ without regard to order is a combination: $\binom{10}{3}$. In Desmos, \texttt{nCr(10, 3)} returns $120$. Option B gives permutations ($720$), which counts order. Option D is $\frac{10!}{3!}$, which is neither the combination nor permutation formula.
\end{highlightbox}

\subsection{Summary: Speed Optimization and Hidden Features}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{The $7$ Commandments of SAT Desmos Mastery:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Know before you go:} Decide your Desmos strategy \textit{before} the test, not during.
\item \textbf{Tier your approach:} Mental math first, Desmos-assisted second, full Desmos third.
\item \textbf{Master the click:} Clicking intersection points, roots, and vertices is your most powerful tool.
\item \textbf{Use sliders for unknowns:} Any time a problem has a parameter, use a slider.
\item \textbf{Graph to verify:} Even when you solve algebraically, a $5$-second graph confirms your answer.
\item \textbf{Know the hidden features:} Lists, piecewise, statistics functions, \texttt{nCr}, restrictions.
\item \textbf{Apply the $45$-second rule:} If algebra stalls, switch to Desmos immediately.
\end{enumerate}
\end{insightbox}

%================================================================
% SECTION 18: COMPLETE SAT MATH PRACTICE TEST WITH DESMOS
%================================================================

\section{Complete SAT Math Practice Set with Desmos Solutions}

This section provides a comprehensive set of SAT-style problems organized by difficulty. For each problem, we provide both the algebraic solution and the Desmos solution, so you can practice both approaches and learn when each is most efficient.

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{How to Use This Section:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item First, try each problem on your own (timed: $1.5$ minutes per problem).
\item Then, try solving it with Desmos (timed: note how long it takes).
\item Compare your two times---this tells you which method is faster for you on each problem type.
\item Review the full solution to ensure your understanding is complete.
\end{enumerate}
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Easy Level (Problems 1--8)}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 1: Linear Equation}

If $3x + 7 = 22$, what is the value of $x$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $3$
\item $5$
\item $7$
\item $\dfrac{29}{3}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} $3x + 7 = 22 \Rightarrow 3x = 15 \Rightarrow x = 5$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{3x + 7 = 22}. Desmos displays $x = 5$ on the number line. Time: $\sim 5$ seconds.

\textbf{Verdict:} Mental math is faster here. This is a Tier 1 problem.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 2: Slope from Two Points}

What is the slope of the line passing through $(2, 5)$ and $(6, 13)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $\dfrac{1}{2}$
\item $2$
\item $4$
\item $8$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} $m = \dfrac{13 - 5}{6 - 2} = \dfrac{8}{4} = 2$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Plot both points: \texttt{(2, 5)} and \texttt{(6, 13)}. Then type \texttt{(13 - 5)/(6 - 2)} to get $2$. Alternatively, use regression: enter points in a table and run \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}; Desmos gives $m = 2$.

\textbf{Verdict:} Mental math is faster. Tier 1.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 3: Evaluating a Function}

If $f(x) = 2x^2 - 3x + 1$, what is $f(-2)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $3$
\item $7$
\item $11$
\item $15$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: D}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} $f(-2) = 2(-2)^2 - 3(-2) + 1 = 2(4) + 6 + 1 = 8 + 6 + 1 = 15$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{f(x) = 2x\^{}2 - 3x + 1}, then type \texttt{f(-2)}. Desmos displays $15$. Time: $\sim 8$ seconds.

\textbf{Verdict:} Both methods are fast. Desmos is good for double-checking.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 4: Percent Increase}

A shirt originally costs \$40. After a $15\%$ increase, what is the new price?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item \$6
\item \$34
\item \$46
\item \$55
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: C}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} New price $= 40 \times 1.15 = 46$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{40 * 1.15}. Desmos displays $46$. Time: $\sim 3$ seconds.

\textbf{Verdict:} Desmos is a fast calculator here. Tier 1--2.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 5: Solving a Proportion}

If $\dfrac{x}{5} = \dfrac{12}{15}$, what is $x$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $3$
\item $4$
\item $5$
\item $9$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} Cross-multiply: $15x = 60 \Rightarrow x = 4$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{x/5 = 12/15}. Desmos shows $x = 4$.

\textbf{Verdict:} Both are quick. Tier 1.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 6: $y$-Intercept Identification}

What is the $y$-intercept of $y = -3x + 7$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-3$
\item $3$
\item $7$
\item $-7$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer
: C}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} In slope-intercept form $y = mx + b$, the $y$-intercept is $b = 7$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{y = -3x + 7}. Click the point where the line crosses the $y$-axis. Desmos shows $(0, 7)$.

\textbf{Verdict:} Know this by sight. Tier 1.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 7: Solving a Quadratic Equation}

What are the solutions to $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x = 1$ and $x = 6$
\item $x = 2$ and $x = 3$
\item $x = -2$ and $x = -3$
\item $x = -1$ and $x = -6$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} Factor: $(x-2)(x-3) = 0$, so $x = 2$ or $x = 3$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 5x + 6}. Click the two $x$-intercepts. Desmos shows $(2, 0)$ and $(3, 0)$. Time: $\sim 10$ seconds.

\textbf{Verdict:} Desmos is excellent for quadratics, especially when factoring isn't obvious. Tier 2.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 8: System of Equations}

What is the solution to the system?
\[
\begin{cases} 2x + y = 10 \\ x - y = 2 \end{cases}
\]

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(3, 4)$
\item $(4, 2)$
\item $(5, 0)$
\item $(2, 6)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} Add equations: $3x = 12 \Rightarrow x = 4$. Then $4 - y = 2 \Rightarrow y = 2$. Solution: $(4, 2)$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{2x + y = 10} on line 1 and \texttt{x - y = 2} on line 2. Click the intersection point. Desmos shows $(4, 2)$. Time: $\sim 12$ seconds.

\textbf{Verdict:} Desmos is the safest method for systems. Tier 2.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 9: Vertex of a Parabola}

What is the vertex of $f(x) = x^2 + 6x + 5$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(-3, -4)$
\item $(3, -4)$
\item $(-3, 4)$
\item $(3, 4)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: A}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} $h = -\frac{b}{2a} = -\frac{6}{2} = -3$. Then $f(-3) = 9 - 18 + 5 = -4$. Vertex: $(-3, -4)$.

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 + 6x + 5}. Click the minimum point. Desmos shows $(-3, -4)$. Time: $\sim 8$ seconds.

\textbf{Verdict:} Desmos is faster and eliminates arithmetic errors. Tier 2.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 10: Exponential Growth}

A population starts at $500$ and doubles every $3$ years. Which function models the population after $t$ years?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $P(t) = 500(2)^{3t}$
\item $P(t) = 500(2)^{t/3}$
\item $P(t) = 500(3)^{t/2}$
\item $P(t) = 500 + 2t$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} Doubling every $3$ years means the base is $2$ and the exponent is $t/3$. At $t = 0$: $P = 500$. At $t = 3$: $P = 500(2)^1 = 1000$. At $t = 6$: $P = 500(2)^2 = 2000$. \checkmark

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Graph all four choices. Then plot test points: $(0, 500)$, $(3, 1000)$, $(6, 2000)$. Only choice B passes through all three points.

\textbf{Verdict:} Understanding the model is important, but Desmos confirms instantly. Tier 2.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 11: Circle Equation}

A circle has center $(3, -2)$ and radius $5$. Which point lies ON the circle?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(3, 3)$
\item $(8, -2)$
\item $(0, 0)$
\item $(3, 4)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{(x - 3)\^{}2 + (y + 2)\^{}2 = 25}. Then plot each point: \texttt{(3, 3)}, \texttt{(8, -2)}, \texttt{(0, 0)}, \texttt{(3, 4)}. The point $(8, -2)$ lies exactly on the circle.

\textbf{Algebraic Verification:} $(8-3)^2 + (-2+2)^2 = 25 + 0 = 25$. \checkmark

\textbf{Verdict:} Desmos makes circle problems visual and fast. Tier 2--3.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 12: Equivalent Expressions}

Which expression is equivalent to $\dfrac{x^2 + 5x + 6}{x + 2}$ for $x \neq -2$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x + 2$
\item $x + 3$
\item $x + 6$
\item $x^2 + 3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{f(x) = (x\^{}2 + 5x + 6)/(x + 2)} on line 1. Type \texttt{g(x) = x + 3} on line 2. The graphs overlap perfectly (with a hole at $x = -2$ on $f$). Time: $\sim 10$ seconds.

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} Factor numerator: $x^2 + 5x + 6 = (x+2)(x+3)$. Cancel $(x+2)$: result is $x + 3$.

\textbf{Verdict:} Desmos is the perfect safety net for factoring problems. Tier 2.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 13: Absolute Value Inequality}

What is the solution set of $|2x - 4| > 6$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-1 < x < 5$
\item $x < -1$ or $x > 5$
\item $x < -5$ or $x > 1$
\item $-5 < x < 1$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{abs(2x - 4) > 6}. Desmos shades the number line for $x < -1$ and $x > 5$. Time: $\sim 6$ seconds.

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} $2x - 4 > 6 \Rightarrow x > 5$ OR $2x - 4 < -6 \Rightarrow x < -1$.

\textbf{Verdict:} Desmos eliminates sign errors in absolute value problems. Tier 2--3.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 14: Radical Equation}

What is the solution to $\sqrt{3x + 1} = x - 1$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x = 0$ only
\item $x = 5$ only
\item $x = 0$ and $x = 5$
\item $x = -1$ and $x = 5$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{y = sqrt(3x + 1)} and \texttt{y = x - 1}. Click the intersection(s). Desmos shows only one intersection at $(5, 4)$. Note: $(0, 1)$ and $(0, -1)$ do NOT intersect---$\sqrt{1} = 1 \neq -1$.

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} Square both sides: $3x + 1 = x^2 - 2x + 1 \Rightarrow x^2 - 5x = 0 \Rightarrow x(x-5) = 0$. So $x = 0$ or $x = 5$. Check: $x = 0$: $\sqrt{1} = 1$ but $0 - 1 = -1$. $1 \neq -1$, so $x = 0$ is extraneous. $x = 5$: $\sqrt{16} = 4$ and $5 - 1 = 4$. \checkmark

\textbf{Verdict:} Desmos automatically filters extraneous solutions! This is a \textbf{huge} advantage. Tier 3.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 15: Regression / Line of Best Fit}

The following data points are given: $(1, 3)$, $(2, 5)$, $(3, 8)$, $(4, 10)$, $(5, 13)$. Using a linear regression, what is the approximate slope of the line of best fit?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $2.0$
\item $2.5$
\item $3.0$
\item $3.5$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Click the ``+'' button and select ``table.''
\item Enter $x_1$ values: $1, 2, 3, 4, 5$.
\item Enter $y_1$ values: $3, 5, 8, 10, 13$.
\item On a new line, type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}.
\item Desmos displays: $m = 2.5$, $b = 0.5$.
\end{enumerate}
The slope is $2.5$. Time: $\sim 30$ seconds.

\textbf{Verdict:} Regression problems are \textbf{impossible} without Desmos (or very tedious by hand). Tier 3.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 16: Number of Solutions (Discriminant)}

For what value of $k$ does $x^2 + kx + 9 = 0$ have exactly one solution?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $k = 3$
\item $k = 6$
\item $k = 9$
\item $k = 12$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Correct Answer: B}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:} Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 + kx + 9} with a slider for $k$. Adjust $k$ until the parabola just touches the $x$-axis (one solution = vertex on $x$-axis). This occurs at $k = 6$ (and $k = -6$, but only $6$ is among the choices).

\textbf{Algebraic Solution:} Discriminant $= k^2 - 4(1)(9) = k^2 - 36 = 0 \Rightarrow k^2 = 36 \Rightarrow k = \pm 6$.

\textbf{Verdict:} The slider method is intuitive and visual. Tier 2--3.
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Summary of Practice Set}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Key Takeaways from the Practice Set:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Problems 6--7:} Basic identification and factoring---Desmos is a backup, not the primary tool.
\item \textbf{Problems 8--9:} Systems and vertices---Desmos is equally fast or faster than algebra.
\item \textbf{Problems 10--12:} Models, circles, and equivalence---Desmos provides visual confirmation.
\item \textbf{Problems 13--14:} Inequalities and radicals---Desmos eliminates common errors (sign mistakes, extraneous solutions).
\item \textbf{Problems 15--16:} Regression and discriminant---Desmos is essential (Tier 3 problems).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Pattern:} As problems get harder, Desmos becomes more valuable. For the hardest SAT problems, Desmos is often the \textbf{only} realistic path to a correct answer within the time limit.
\end{insightbox}
