A store sells notebooks for \$3 each and pens for \$1 each. Maria has \$15 to spend and wants to buy at least 7 items total. Which system of inequalities represents this situation, where $n$ = notebooks and $p$ = pens?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $3n + p \leq 15$ and $n + p \geq 7$
\item $3n + p \geq 15$ and $n + p \leq 7$
\item $n + 3p \leq 15$ and $n + p \geq 7$
\item $3n + p \leq 15$ and $n + p \leq 7$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Strategy:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Think about the constraints: cost $\leq$ budget, items $\geq$ minimum.
\item Graph option A: \texttt{3n + p <= 15} and \texttt{n + p >= 7} (using $x$ for $n$, $y$ for $p$).
\item Test a reasonable point like $(2, 5)$: Cost $= 3(2) + 5 = 11 \leq 15$ \checkmark, Items $= 2 + 5 = 7 \geq 7$ \checkmark.
\item The overlapping region makes sense (affordable combinations with enough items).
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: A}
\end{example}

\subsubsection{Strategy A4: ``Which Expression is Equivalent'' Questions}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{The Universal Equivalence Check:} When the SAT asks ``which expression is equivalent to [something],'' you can \textbf{always} verify by graphing both expressions and checking if they produce identical graphs.

\textbf{Method:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph the original expression as $y = \text{[original]}$.
\item Graph each answer choice one at a time.
\item The choice whose graph perfectly overlaps the original is the answer.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Even faster:} Substitute a specific value (like $x = 1$ or $x = 2$) into the original and each answer choice. The matching output reveals the answer.

In Desmos: Type the original, note $f(1)$. Then type each choice and check which gives the same value at $x = 1$.
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question A1:}

If $3(2x - 4) + 5 = ax + b$ for all values of $x$, what is the value of $a + b$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-1$
\item $1$
\item $-7$
\item $-1$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{y = 3(2x - 4) + 5}. Desmos simplifies the graph to a line. Read the slope ($a = 6$) and $y$-intercept ($b = -7$). So $a + b = 6 + (-7) = -1$.

\textbf{Answer: A} ($a + b = -1$)
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question A2:}

A system of equations has no solution. One equation is $y = 4x + 3$. Which could be the other equation?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $y = 4x + 7$
\item $y = -4x + 3$
\item $y = \frac{1}{4}x + 3$
\item $y = 4x + 3$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Graph \texttt{y = 4x + 3}, then graph each choice. The one that is parallel (same slope, different intercept) and never intersects is the answer.

Choice A: $y = 4x + 7$ has slope 4 (same) and intercept 7 (different). Parallel. No solution. \checkmark

Choice D has infinite solutions (same line). Choices B and C have different slopes (they intersect).

\textbf{Answer: A}
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question A3:}

What is the solution to the system?
\[
\begin{cases} y = -2x + 8 \\ y = x - 1 \end{cases}
\]

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(3, 2)$
\item $(2, 4)$
\item $(3, 5)$
\item $(-1, 10)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type both equations. Click intersection. Desmos shows $(3, 2)$.

\textbf{Answer: A}
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question A4:}

If $\frac{x}{3} + \frac{x}{6} = 5$, what is the value of $x$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $6$
\item $10$
\item $15$
\item $30$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{y = x/3 + x/6} and \texttt{y = 5}. Click intersection. The $x$-coordinate is $10$.

\textbf{Answer: B}
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question A5:}

Maria earns \$12 per hour at her job and \$15 per hour tutoring. She works a combined total of 30 hours per week and earns \$396. How many hours does she tutor?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $8$
\item $12$
\item $18$
\item $22$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Let $x$ = job hours, $y$ = tutoring hours.
\begin{itemize}
\item Type \texttt{x + y = 30}
\item Type \texttt{12x + 15y = 396}
\item Click intersection: $(18, 12)$
\end{itemize}
She tutors $y = 12$ hours.

\textbf{Answer: B}
\end{highlightbox}

\subsection{Domain 2: Advanced Math (13--15 questions, ~35\%)}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{What This Domain Covers:} Equivalent expressions, nonlinear equations, systems with nonlinear equations, function notation, quadratics, polynomials, exponential functions, and radical/rational equations. This is where Desmos gives you the \textbf{biggest competitive advantage} because these problems are the hardest to solve by hand.
\end{contextbox}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Advanced Math Desmos Rule:} For \textbf{every} nonlinear equation, graph both sides and find intersections. For \textbf{every} ``find the zeros/roots'' question, graph the function and click the $x$-intercepts. For \textbf{every} vertex question, graph the parabola and click the vertex.
\end{theorem}

\subsubsection{Strategy AM1: Solving Quadratic Equations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Three Desmos Methods for Quadratics:}

Given: $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$

\textbf{Method 1 --- Graph and find $x$-intercepts:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 5x + 6}.
\item Click both $x$-intercepts: $(2, 0)$ and $(3, 0)$.
\item Solutions: $x = 2$ and $x = 3$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Method 2 --- Graph both sides:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Rearrange to $x^2 = 5x - 6$.
\item Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2} and \texttt{y = 5x - 6}.
\item Click intersections.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Method 3 --- Direct substitution of answer choices:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{f(x) = x\^{}2 - 5x + 6}.
\item Check each answer choice: \texttt{f(2)} gives $0$ \checkmark, \texttt{f(3)} gives $0$ \checkmark.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Recommendation:} Method 1 is fastest for most SAT questions.
\end{stepbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy AM2: Vertex and Minimum/Maximum of Quadratics}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Finding the Vertex Instantly:}

Given: $f(x) = 2x^2 - 12x + 22$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = 2x\^{}2 - 12x + 22}.
\item Click the lowest point (vertex) on the parabola.
\item Desmos shows $(3, 4)$.
\item \textbf{Minimum value} of $f$ is $4$, occurring at $x = 3$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{SAT Variations:}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``What is the minimum value of $f$?'' $\rightarrow$ Answer: $4$ (the $y$-coordinate of the vertex).
\item ``For what value of $x$ does $f$ reach its minimum?'' $\rightarrow$ Answer: $3$ (the $x$-coordinate).
\item ``Write $f$ in vertex form.'' $\rightarrow$ $f(x) = 2(x - 3)^2 + 4$ (read directly from vertex).
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy AM3: Polynomial and Rational Equations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Solving Higher-Degree Equations:}

Given: $x^3 - 4x^2 + x + 6 = 0$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = x\^{}3 - 4x\^{}2 + x + 6}.
\item Click all $x$-intercepts.
\item Desmos shows $x = -1$, $x = 2$, $x = 3$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Why Desmos dominates here:} Solving cubic equations by hand requires synthetic division and factoring --- easily 2--3 minutes. Desmos does it in 10 seconds.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Solving Rational Equations:}

Given: $\dfrac{3}{x-1} + \dfrac{2}{x+2} = 1$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = 3/(x-1) + 2/(x+2)} in line 1.
\item Type \texttt{y = 1} in line 2.
\item Click the intersection(s).
\item Desmos shows the solution(s) instantly.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Critical check:} Make sure the solution doesn't make any denominator zero (i.e., $x \neq 1$ and $x \neq -2$).
\end{stepbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy AM4: Function Composition and Transformation}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SA
T Problem --- Composition:}

If $f(x) = x^2 + 1$ and $g(x) = 3x - 2$, what is $f(g(2))$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $5$
\item $10$
\item $17$
\item $37$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{f(x) = x\^{}2 + 1} in line 1.
\item Type \texttt{g(x) = 3x - 2} in line 2.
\item Type \texttt{f(g(2))} in line 3.
\item Desmos immediately displays $17$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{By Hand Verification:} $g(2) = 3(2) - 2 = 4$. Then $f(4) = 4^2 + 1 = 17$. \checkmark

\textbf{Answer: C}
\end{example}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Transformation Identification with Desmos:}

If the SAT asks ``Which equation represents $f(x) = x^2$ shifted 3 right and 5 up?''

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2} (the parent function --- shown in blue).
\item Type \texttt{y = (x - 3)\^{}2 + 5} (your predicted answer).
\item \textbf{Visually confirm:} Does the vertex move from $(0, 0)$ to $(3, 5)$? If yes, your transformation is correct.
\item If unsure between answer choices, graph each choice and see which one has vertex at $(3, 5)$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy AM5: Exponential and Radical Equations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Exponential Equations:}

Given: $2^{x+1} = 3^{x-1}$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = 2\^{}(x+1)} in line 1.
\item Type \texttt{y = 3\^{}(x-1)} in line 2.
\item Click the intersection.
\item Desmos shows the solution immediately.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Why Desmos is essential:} Solving this by hand requires logarithms: $\frac{x+1}{x-1} = \frac{\ln 3}{\ln 2}$, which is messy and time-consuming. Desmos gives the decimal answer in seconds.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Radical Equations:}

Given: $\sqrt{2x + 5} = x - 1$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = sqrt(2x + 5)} in line 1.
\item Type \texttt{y = x - 1} in line 2.
\item Click the intersection(s).
\item \textbf{Critical:} Check that each solution satisfies $x - 1 \geq 0$ (since the square root output is non-negative) and $2x + 5 \geq 0$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Extraneous Solution Alert:} Radical equations can produce extraneous solutions when solved algebraically. Desmos \textbf{only shows real intersections}, so it automatically filters out extraneous solutions. This is a massive advantage.
\end{stepbox}

\subsection{Domain 3: Problem-Solving and Data Analysis (5--7 questions, ~15\%)}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{What This Domain Covers:} Ratios, rates, proportions, percentages, unit conversion, data interpretation (tables, bar charts, histograms, scatterplots, line graphs), probability, statistics (mean, median, standard deviation), and study design. \textbf{Note:} This domain appears \textbf{only in Module 2} (calculator module), so Desmos is always available.
\end{contextbox}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Data Analysis Desmos Rule:} Use Desmos for:
\begin{itemize}
\item Computing statistics from data sets (mean, median, standard deviation)
\item Creating regression models from scatterplot data
\item Solving proportion and percentage equations
\item Verifying probability calculations
\end{itemize}
Desmos is \textbf{less helpful} for conceptual questions about study design, bias, and data interpretation --- those require understanding, not computation.
\end{theorem}

\subsubsection{Strategy DA1: Statistics from Data Sets}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Computing Statistics Instantly:}

Given data: $\{12, 15, 18, 18, 20, 22, 25, 30\}$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{L = [12, 15, 18, 18, 20, 22, 25, 30]}.
\item \texttt{mean(L)} $\rightarrow$ Desmos shows $20$.
\item \texttt{median(L)} $\rightarrow$ Desmos shows $19$ (average of 18 and 20).
\item \texttt{stdev(L)} $\rightarrow$ Desmos shows the standard deviation.
\item \texttt{total(L)} $\rightarrow$ Desmos shows $160$.
\item \texttt{length(L)} $\rightarrow$ Desmos shows $8$.
\item \texttt{min(L)} $\rightarrow 12$, \texttt{max(L)} $\rightarrow 30$.
\item \textbf{Range:} \texttt{max(L) - min(L)} $\rightarrow 18$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{SAT Statistics Shortcut:} When the SAT gives you a frequency table (like ``5 students scored 80, 8 students scored 85, 3 students scored 90''), you can enter the data as a list with repetitions:

\texttt{L = [80, 80, 80, 80, 80, 85, 85, 85, 85, 85, 85, 85, 85, 90, 90, 90]}

Or use a more efficient method --- create two lists and use the \texttt{total} function:

\texttt{scores = [80, 85, 90]}

\texttt{freq = [5, 8, 3]}

\texttt{total(scores * freq) / total(freq)} gives the weighted mean.
\end{insightbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy DA2: Regression from Scatterplot Data}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{When the SAT Gives You a Scatterplot or Table:}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Create a table in Desmos with $x$-values in column 1 and $y$-values in column 2.
\item Try different regression models:
\begin{itemize}
\item Linear: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}
\item Quadratic: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}
\item Exponential: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a * b\^{}x1}
\end{itemize}
\item Compare $R^2$ values --- the model with $R^2$ closest to $1$ fits best.
\item Use the regression equation to make predictions.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{SAT Question Types:}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``What is the line of best fit?'' $\rightarrow$ Use linear regression.
\item ``Based on the line of best fit, predict $y$ when $x = 15$.'' $\rightarrow$ Read from regression or substitute.
\item ``What does the slope of the line of best fit represent?'' $\rightarrow$ This is conceptual; Desmos helps confirm the slope value but you must interpret it in context.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy DA3: Percentages and Proportions}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Percentage Problems --- Let Desmos Compute:}

\textbf{Type 1: Finding a percentage of a number}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``What is 35\% of 240?'' $\rightarrow$ Type \texttt{0.35 * 240} $= 84$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Type 2: Finding what percentage one number is of another}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``42 is what percent of 168?'' $\rightarrow$ Type \texttt{42/168 * 100} $= 25$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Type 3: Percentage increase/decrease}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``A price increases from \$80 to \$92. What is the percent increase?'' $\rightarrow$ Type \texttt{(92 - 80)/80 * 100} $= 15\%$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Type 4: Finding the original value}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``After a 20\% discount, the price is \$64. What was the original price?'' $\rightarrow$ Type \texttt{64/0.80} $= 80$.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{Common SAT Percentage Trap:}

A 20\% increase followed by a 20\% decrease does \textbf{NOT} return to the original value!

\textbf{Desmos check:} Type \texttt{100 * 1.20 * 0.80} $= 96$, not $100$.

The net effect is a 4\% decrease. The SAT loves testing this misconception.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Domain 4: Geometry and Trigonometry (5--7 questions, ~15\%)}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{What This Domain Covers:} Area, volume, lines/angles/triangles, right triangle trigonometry, circles (equations and properties), and basic trigonometric functions. This domain appears \textbf{only in Module 2}, so Desmos is always available.
\end{contextbox}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{Geometry Desmos Rule:} Desmos is most useful for:
\begin{itemize}
\item Graphing circles and finding intersection points
\item Computing trigonometric values (sin, cos, tan)
\item Solving equations involving $\pi$, radians, and trig functions
\item Checking area/volume calculations
\end{itemize}
Desmos is \textbf{less useful} for spatial reasoning and geometric proofs --- those require conceptual understanding.
\end{theorem}

\subsubsection{Strategy G1: Circle Equations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Graphing Circles in Desmos:}

The standard form of a circle is $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$.

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the equation directly: \texttt{(x - 3)\^{}2 + (y + 2)\^{}2 = 25}.
\item Desmos graphs the circle with center $(3, -2)$ and radius $5$.
\item Click on the circle to see key points.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{SAT Applications:}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``What is the center and radius?'' $\rightarrow$ Read from the graph or equation.
\item ``Does the point $(6, 2)$ lie on, inside, or outside the circle?'' $\rightarrow$ Plot the point and visually check, OR type \texttt{(6-3)\^{}2 + (2+2)\^{}2} and compare to $25$.
\item ``Where does the circle intersect $y = x + 1$?'' $\rightarrow$ Graph both and click intersections.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Circle Problem:}

The equation $x^2 + y^2 - 6x + 4y - 12 = 0$ represents a circle. What is the radius?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $3$
\item $4$
\item $5$
\item $25$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Strategy:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the equation exactly as given: \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 - 6x + 4y - 12 = 0}.
\item Desmos graphs the circle.
\item Click on the circle and identify the center (appears to be at $(3, -2)$).
\item Measure from center to any point on the circle, or complete the square mentally.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{By Hand:} Complete the square:
\begin{align*}
(x^2 - 6x + 9) + (y^2 + 4y + 4) &= 12 + 9 + 4 \\
(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 &= 25
\end{align*}
Radius $= \sqrt{25} = 5$.

\textbf{Answer: C}
\end{example}

\subsubsection{Strategy G2: Trigonometric Calculations}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{DEGREE vs.\ RADIAN MODE:}

Before doing \textbf{any} trig calculation in Desmos, check your angle mode!
\begin{itemize}
\item Click the \textbf{wrench icon} (settings) in the top right.
\item Choose \textbf{Degrees} or \textbf{Radians} depending on the problem.
\item \textbf{SAT default:} Most SAT problems use \textbf{degrees} for right triangle trig and \textbf{radians} for trig function graphs and unit circle problems.
\item \textbf{If in doubt:} Read the problem carefully. If you see $30°$, $45°$, $60°$, use degrees. If you see $\frac{\pi}{6}$, $\frac{\pi}{4}$, $\frac{\pi}{3}$, use radians.
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Right Triangle Trig with Desmos:}

Given: A right triangle with hypotenuse $13$ and one leg $5$. Find the angle opposite the leg of length $5$.

\begin{enumerate}
\item Set Desmos to \textbf{Degree mode}.
\item The sine of the unknown angle $= \frac{\text{opposite}}{\text{hypotenuse}} = \frac{5}{13}$.
\item Type \texttt{arcsin(5/13)} (or equivalently \texttt{sin\^{}\{-1\}(5/13)}).
\item Desmos shows $\approx 22.62°$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Inverse Trig Functions in Desmos:}
\begin{itemize}
\item $\sin^{-1}$: Type \texttt{arcsin(value)} or \texttt{sin\^{}\{-1\}(value)}
\item $\cos^{-1}$: Type \texttt{arccos(value)}
\item $\tan^{-1}$: Type \texttt{arctan(value)}
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Trig Problem:}

In a right triangle, $\sin(\theta) = \frac{7}{25}$. What is $\cos(\theta)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $\frac{24}{25}$
\item $\frac{7}{24}$
\item $\frac{18}{25}$
\item $\frac{25}{24}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Set Desmos to \textbf{Degree mode}.
\item Type \texttt{arcsin(7/25)} to find $\theta \approx 16.26°$.
\item Type \texttt{cos(16.26)} and Desmos shows $\approx 0.96 = \frac{24}{25}$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Alternative Desmos Method (Faster):}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{cos(arcsin(7/25))}.
\item Desmos directly shows $0.96 = \frac{24}{25}$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{By Hand:} Using Pythagorean identity: $\cos^2(\theta) = 1 - \sin^2(\theta) = 1 - \frac{49}{625} = \frac{576}{625}$. So $\cos(\theta) = \frac{24}{25}$.

\textbf{Answer: A}
\end{example}

\subsubsection{Strategy G3: Arc Length and Sector Area}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Circle Sector Calculations in Desmos:}

\textbf{Key Formulas:}
\begin{itemize}
\item Arc length: $s = r\theta$ (where $\theta$ is in radians)
\item Sector area: $A = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$ (where $\theta$ is in radians)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Desmos Approach:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item If the angle is given in degrees, first convert: type \texttt{(angle * pi)/180} to get radians.
\item Then compute: type \texttt{r * theta} for arc length or \texttt{(1/2) * r\^{}2 * theta} for sector area.
\item Desmos handles all the arithmetic instantly.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:} Find the arc length of a sector with radius $10$ and central angle $72°$.
\begin{enumerate}
\item Convert: \texttt{(72 * pi)/180} $= \frac{2\pi}{5} \approx 1.2566$
\item Arc length: \texttt{10 * (72 * pi)/180} $= 4\pi \approx 12.57$
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy G4: Volume and Surface Area}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{SAT Geometry Formulas + Desmos = Speed:}

The SAT provides a reference sheet with volume formulas. Your job is simply to \textbf{plug values into Desmos} and compute. Common formulas:
\begin{itemize}
\item Cylinder: $V = \pi r^2 h$
\item Cone: $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$
\item Sphere: $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$
\item Rectangular prism: $V = lwh$
\item Pyramid: $V = \frac{1}{3}Bh$ (where $B$ is the base area)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Desmos Tip:} Type \texttt{pi} for $\pi$. Desmos gives exact answers involving $\pi$ and decimal approximations.
\end{insightbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Volume Problem:}

A cylinder has a radius of $4$ inches and a height of $9$ inches. A cone has the same radius and height. What is the difference in their volumes, in cubic inches?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $48\pi$
\item $96\pi$
\item $144\pi$
\item $192\pi$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{pi * 4\^{}2 * 9} for the cylinder volume. Desmos shows $144\pi$.
\item Type \texttt{(1/3) * pi * 4\^{}2 * 9} for the cone volume. Desmos shows $48\pi$.
\item Type \texttt{pi * 4\^{}2 * 9 - (1/3) * pi * 4\^{}2 * 9}. Desmos shows $96\pi$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: B}

\textbf{Time Target:} 15 seconds with Desmos.
\end{example}

\subsection{Domain 4: Problem-Solving and Data Analysis (5--7 questions, ~15\%)}

\begin{contextbox}
\textbf{What This Domain Covers:} Ratios, rates, proportions, percentages, units, data interpretation (tables, scatterplots, bar graphs), probability, statistics (mean, median, standard deviation), and study design. \textbf{Note:} Desmos is available for these questions and is extremely helpful for computation-heavy problems.
\end{contextbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy D1: Percentage and Ratio Calculations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Percentage Problems --- Let Desmos Be Your Calculator:}

\textbf{Common SAT Percentage Patterns:}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``What is 35\% of 240?'' $\rightarrow$ Type \texttt{0.35 * 240} $= 84$
\item ``15 is what percent of 60?'' $\rightarrow$ Type \texttt{15/60 * 100} $= 25$
\item ``A price increases from \$80 to \$92. What is the percent increase?'' $\rightarrow$ Type \texttt{(92 - 80)/80 * 100} $= 15$
\item ``After a 20\% decrease, the price is \$160. What was the original?'' $\rightarrow$ Type \texttt{160/0.80} $= 200$
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Desmos Advantage:} No mental math errors. Type the expression exactly and get the answer.
\end{stepbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy D2: Statistical Measures with Lists}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Full Statistics Workflow in Desmos:}

Given a data set: $\{12, 15, 18, 18, 20, 22, 25, 30\}$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{L = [12, 15, 18, 18, 20, 22, 25, 30]}.
\item \texttt{mean(L)} $= 20$
\item \texttt{median(L)} $= 19$ (average of 18 and 20)
\item \texttt{stdev(L)} $\approx 5.39$ (population standard deviation)
\item \texttt{stdevp(L)} $\approx 5.39$ (same as stdev in Desmos --- population)
\item \texttt{total(L)} $= 160$
\item \texttt{length(L)} $= 8$
\item \texttt{min(L)} $= 12$, \texttt{max(L)} $= 30$
\item \textbf{Range:} Type \texttt{max(L) - min(L)} $= 18$
\item \textbf{Quartiles:} Type \texttt{quantile(L, 0.25)} for Q1 and \texttt{quantile(L, 0.75)} for Q3.
\item \textbf{IQR:} Type \texttt{quantile(L, 0.75) - quantile(L, 0.25)}.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Statistics Problem:}

A teacher recorded quiz scores: $\{72, 78, 81, 85, 85, 88, 90, 95, 98\}$. If the score of $72$ is removed, which measure of center changes the most?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Mean
\item Median
\item Mode
\item Range
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{A = [72, 78, 81, 85, 85, 88, 90, 95, 98]}.
\item Type \texttt{B = [78, 81, 85, 85, 88, 90, 95, 98]}.
\item Compare:
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{mean(A)} $\approx 85.78$, \texttt{mean(B)} $\approx 87.50$ (change of $1.72$)
\item \texttt{median(A)} $= 85$, \texttt{median(B)} $= 86.5$ (change of $1.5$)
\item Mode stays at $85$ for both.
\end{itemize}
\item The mean changes the most because it is most affected by removing an outlier (the low score of 72).
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: A}
\end{example}

\subsubsection{Strategy D3: Probability Calculations}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Probability with Desmos:}

Most SAT probability problems involve reading a two-way frequency table and computing:
$$P(\text{event}) = \frac{\text{favorable outcomes}}{\text{total outcomes}}$$

\textbf{Desmos Role:} Use Desmos as a fraction calculator to avoid arithmetic errors.

\textbf{Example:} If 42 out of 300 surveyed students prefer jazz, what is the probability?
\begin{itemize}
\item Type \texttt{42/300}. Desmos shows $0.14$.
\item If the SAT wants a fraction: $\frac{42}{300} = \frac{7}{50}$.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Conditional Probability:}
$$P(A \mid B) = \frac{P(A \text{ and } B)}{P(B)} = \frac{\text{both A and B}}{\text{total in B}}$$

Type the fraction directly into Desmos.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Two-Way Table Problem:}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{l|cc|c}
\toprule
& \textbf{Prefer Dogs} & \textbf{Prefer Cats} & \textbf{Total} \\
\midrule
\textbf{Male} & 45 & 30 & 75 \\
\textbf{Female} & 35 & 40 & 75 \\
\midrule
\textbf{Total} & 80 & 70 & 150 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Given that a randomly selected person is female, what is the probability that she prefers cats?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $\frac{40}{150}$
\item $\frac{40}{75}$
\item $\frac{40}{70}$
\item $\frac{70}{150}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item This is conditional probability: $P(\text{cats} \mid \text{female})$.
\item Denominator = total females $= 75$.
\item Numerator = females who prefer cats $= 40$.
\item Type \texttt{40/75}. Desmos shows $\approx 0.5333 = \frac{8}{15}$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: B} ($\frac{40}{75} = \frac{8}{15}$)
\end{example}

\subsubsection{Strategy D4: Scatterplot Analysis and Line of Best Fit}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Scatterplot + Regression in Desmos:}

When the SAT gives you a scatterplot and asks for the line of best fit or a prediction:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Read coordinates} from the scatterplot (approximate if needed).
\item \textbf{Enter data} into a Desmos table.
\item \textbf{Run regression:} Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b} for linear.
\item \textbf{Read the equation:} Desmos gives you $m$ (slope) and $b$ ($y$-intercept).
\item \textbf{Make predictions:} Substitute the desired $x$-value into the regression equation.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Interpreting Slope and Intercept:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Slope $m$:} ``For each increase of 1 in $x$, $y$ increases/decreases by $m$.''
\item \textbf{$y$-intercept $b$:} ``When $x = 0$, the predicted $y$ is $b$.''
\item \textbf{$R^2$:} Closer to 1 means better fit. $R^2 > 0.9$ is a strong fit.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\subsubsection{Strategy D5: Unit Conversion and Rates}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{Unit Conversion --- Desmos as a Safety Net:}

The SAT loves unit conversion problems. Set up the conversion as multiplication of fractions and type the entire expression into Desmos.

\textbf{Example:} Convert 45 miles per hour to feet per second.
$$45 \;\frac{\text{miles}}{\text{hour}} \times \frac{5280 \;\text{feet}}{1 \;\text{mile}} \times \frac{1 \;\text{hour}}{3600 \;\text{seconds}}$$

Type into Desmos: \texttt{45 * 5280 / 3600} $= 66$ feet per second.

\textbf{SAT Tip:} Always write out the unit fractions on scratch paper to make sure units cancel, then type the numerical calculation into Desmos.
\end{insightbox}

%% ================================================================
%% SECTION 12: THE 30-SECOND DESMOS DECISION FRAMEWORK
%% ================================================================

\newpage
\section{The 30-Second Desmos Decision Framework}

\begin{contextbox}
On the Digital SAT, you have approximately \textbf{1 minute and 35 seconds per question}. Spending too long deciding \emph{whether} to use Desmos wastes precious time. This section gives you an instant decision framework.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{The 5-Second Decision Tree}

\begin{criticalbox}
\textbf{When You See a Math Question, Ask Yourself:}

\textbf{Step 1:} Does this question involve an equation, expression, or function?
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{YES
} $\rightarrow$ Go to Step 2.
\item \textbf{NO} (pure concept, definition, or reading comprehension) $\rightarrow$ \textbf{Skip Desmos}, solve conceptually.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 2:} Can I type it directly into Desmos?
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{YES} $\rightarrow$ \textbf{Use Desmos}. Type it in and read the answer.
\item \textbf{NO} (requires setup or translation) $\rightarrow$ Go to Step 3.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Step 3:} Can I set it up in under 30 seconds?
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{YES} $\rightarrow$ \textbf{Use Desmos}. Set up the equation/graph and solve.
\item \textbf{NO} $\rightarrow$ \textbf{Solve by hand} or use a hybrid approach.
\end{itemize}
\end{criticalbox}

\subsection{The Color-Coded Question Classification}

\begin{theorem}
\textbf{The Traffic Light System for Desmos Usage:}

\textbf{\textcolor{green!60!black}{GREEN --- Always Use Desmos} (saves 30+ seconds):}
\begin{itemize}
\item Solving any equation for $x$
\item Finding intersections of two functions
\item Graphing to find vertex, zeros, or intercepts
\item Systems of equations (any method)
\item Evaluating complex expressions or compositions
\item Regression and data fitting
\item Checking answer choices by substitution
\end{itemize}

\textbf{\textcolor{yellow!70!black}{YELLOW --- Sometimes Use Desmos} (depends on complexity):}
\begin{itemize}
\item Simple arithmetic (faster mentally if straightforward)
\item Reading graphs already provided (but Desmos can verify)
\item Probability questions (usually conceptual, but Desmos helps with calculations)
\item Geometry area/volume (Desmos helps with computation, not setup)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{\textcolor{red!70!black}{RED --- Rarely Use Desmos} (conceptual understanding needed):}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``Which statement is true about\ldots'' questions
\item Interpreting what a variable represents in context
\item Choosing the correct equation to model a scenario (before solving)
\item Questions about statistical concepts (bias, sampling methods)
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Time Management with Desmos}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{The 30-Second Rule:}

If you've been typing in Desmos for more than 30 seconds without progress, \textbf{STOP}. Re-read the question. You may be:
\begin{itemize}
\item Misinterpreting what the question asks
\item Setting up the wrong equation
\item Overcomplicating a simple problem
\item Using Desmos when a conceptual approach is faster
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Reset and try a different approach.} The SAT rewards flexibility, not stubbornness.
\end{insightbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Optimal Time Allocation per Question:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Read the question:} 10--15 seconds
\item \textbf{Decide: Desmos or by hand?} 3--5 seconds (use the traffic light system)
\item \textbf{Execute:} 30--60 seconds
\item \textbf{Verify answer:} 10--15 seconds (use Desmos to check if you solved by hand)
\item \textbf{Total target:} Under 95 seconds per question
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.1}

A student encounters the equation $\frac{x^2 - 9}{x + 3} = 5$ on the SAT. According to the 30-Second Decision Framework, what should they do?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Solve by hand using factoring only
\item Graph $y = \frac{x^2 - 9}{x + 3}$ and $y = 5$ in Desmos and find the intersection
\item Skip the problem and come back later
\item Substitute all answer choices into the original equation mentally
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} This is a GREEN-light Desmos problem --- it involves solving an equation. Graph both sides and click the intersection. Desmos handles the algebra instantly. Note: $\frac{x^2 - 9}{x + 3} = \frac{(x-3)(x+3)}{x+3} = x - 3$ when $x \neq -3$, so $x - 3 = 5$ gives $x = 8$. But Desmos gets you there without needing to see the factoring trick.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.2}

Which of the following SAT question types would be classified as RED (rarely use Desmos)?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Find the solutions to $x^2 - 7x + 10 = 0$
\item A survey of 500 randomly selected adults found that 62\% prefer coffee. Which conclusion is valid?
\item What is the $y$-intercept of $f(x) = 3(2)^x - 5$?
\item Find the intersection of $y = 2x + 1$ and $y = -x + 7$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Option B is about interpreting a statistical study and drawing valid conclusions --- this is purely conceptual and requires understanding of sampling, bias, and inference. Desmos cannot help with this. Options A, C, and D are all GREEN-light Desmos problems.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 12.3}

A student has been typing in Desmos for 45 seconds and the graph doesn't look right. According to the time management strategy, what should they do?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item Keep trying different things in Desmos until something works
\item Stop, re-read the question, and consider a different approach
\item Close Desmos and solve entirely by hand
\item Skip the question permanently
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} The 30-Second Rule says to stop and re-read. The student may be misinterpreting the question or setting up the wrong equation. Re-reading often reveals a simpler approach. Option C is too extreme --- Desmos might still help after a reset. Option D wastes a question unnecessarily.
\end{highlightbox}

%% ================================================================
%% SECTION 13: ADVANCED DESMOS POWER MOVES
%% ================================================================

\newpage
\section{Advanced Desmos Power Moves for a Perfect Score}

This section covers the elite-level Desmos techniques that separate 750+ scorers from the rest. These ``power moves'' handle the hardest SAT math questions --- the ones that most students either skip or spend 3+ minutes solving by hand.

\subsection{Power Move 1: The Answer Choice Eliminator}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Answer Choice Eliminator} is a Desmos strategy where you systematically test each multiple-choice answer by substituting it into the original equation or condition. Instead of solving forward (equation $\rightarrow$ answer), you work backward (answer $\rightarrow$ verification).
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you lost your key and you have 4 keys on a ring. Instead of figuring out which key is correct by studying the lock mechanism (solving the equation), you just try each key one by one. The one that opens the door is the answer. Desmos lets you ``try each key'' in seconds.
\end{analogy}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{The Answer Choice Eliminator --- Step by Step:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Read the question} and identify the equation or condition that must be satisfied.
\item \textbf{Type the equation or function} into Desmos.
\item \textbf{Substitute each answer choice:}
\begin{itemize}
\item If the answer is a value of $x$: evaluate $f(\text{choice})$ and see if it satisfies the condition.
\item If the answer is an equation: graph it and see if it matches the required behavior.
\item If the answer is a coordinate: plot the point and check if it lies on the curve.
\end{itemize}
\item \textbf{The choice that works is the answer.}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{When to use this:} When solving forward seems complicated, messy, or time-consuming. Especially effective for:
\begin{itemize}
\item Equations with absolute values, radicals, or fractions
\item ``Which of the following is equivalent to\ldots'' questions
\item Questions where the algebra is ugly but checking is clean
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Hard Problem:}

If $\sqrt
{2x+5} = x - 1$, what is the value of $x$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-2$
\item $2$
\item $-1$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Eliminator Strategy:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{f(x) = sqrt(2x+5)} in line 1.
\item Type \texttt{g(x) = x - 1} in line 2.
\item Now test each choice by checking if $f(\text{choice}) = g(\text{choice})$:
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{f(-2)} $= \sqrt{1} = 1$ and \texttt{g(-2)} $= -3$. Not equal. \ding{55}
\item \texttt{f(2)} $= \sqrt{9} = 3$ and \texttt{g(2)} $= 1$. Not equal. \ding{55}
\item \texttt{f(-1)} $= \sqrt{3} \approx 1.73$ and \texttt{g(-1)} $= -2$. Not equal. \ding{55}
\item \texttt{f(4)} $= \sqrt{13} \approx 3.61$ and \texttt{g(4)} $= 3$. Not equal. \ding{55}
\end{itemize}
\item \textbf{Wait!} None seem to work by quick mental math. Let's use Desmos properly.
\item Graph both functions and click the intersection.
\item Desmos shows the intersection at approximately $(x, y)$ --- read the $x$-value.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Better approach:} Type \texttt{sqrt(2x+5) = x - 1} directly. Desmos shows the solution. Or graph both and click the intersection.

\textbf{Key Lesson:} When the Eliminator doesn't give a clean answer, \textbf{switch to graphing immediately}. Don't waste time.
\end{example}

\subsection{Power Move 2: The Slider Method for Unknown Constants}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Slider Method} uses Desmos sliders to find the value of an unknown constant in an equation by visually matching a required condition (such as having exactly one solution, passing through a specific point, or having a specific $y$-intercept).
\end{definition}

\begin{analogy}
Imagine you're tuning a guitar string. You turn the tuning peg slowly until the sound matches the note you want. Desmos sliders work the same way --- you ``tune'' a constant until the graph matches the condition the SAT problem describes.
\end{analogy}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{The Slider Method --- Step by Step:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Identify the unknown constant} in the problem (often called $a$, $b$, $c$, $k$, etc.).
\item \textbf{Type the equation} into Desmos using that constant as a variable.
\item Desmos will \textbf{automatically offer to create a slider} for the unknown constant. Click ``add slider.''
\item \textbf{Adjust the slider} until the graph satisfies the condition:
\begin{itemize}
\item ``Exactly one solution'' $\rightarrow$ the curves are tangent (touch at exactly one point).
\item ``Passes through $(3, 7)$'' $\rightarrow$ the curve goes through that point.
\item ``Has a maximum of 10'' $\rightarrow$ the vertex $y$-coordinate is 10.
\item ``No real solutions'' $\rightarrow$ the curves don't intersect.
\end{itemize}
\item \textbf{Read the slider value} --- that's your answer.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Hard Problem:}

For what value of $k$ does the system below have exactly one solution?
\begin{align*}
y &= x^2 - 4x + k \\
y &= 2x - 5
\end{align*}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-1$
\item $0$
\item $4$
\item $8$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Slider Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 4x + k} in line 1. Click ``add slider'' for $k$.
\item Type \texttt{y = 2x - 5} in line 2.
\item Slowly adjust the $k$ slider.
\item Watch the parabola move up and down. Find the value of $k$ where the parabola just \textbf{touches} the line at exactly one point (tangent).
\item Desmos shows this happens at $k = 4$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Verification:} Set the equations equal: $x^2 - 4x + 4 = 2x - 5 \Rightarrow x^2 - 6x + 9 = 0 \Rightarrow (x-3)^2 = 0$. Exactly one solution. \checkmark

\textbf{Answer: C}
\end{example}

\subsection{Power Move 3: The Regression Backdoor}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Regression Backdoor} is a technique where you use Desmos regression to find an equation that fits given data points, even when the SAT doesn't explicitly ask for regression. This is especially powerful for finding equations of parabolas, exponentials, or lines when given specific points.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Finding a Quadratic Through Three Points:}

If the SAT tells you a parabola passes through $(1, 3)$, $(2, 8)$, and $(4, 28)$, and asks for the equation:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Create a Desmos table with columns $x_1$ and $y_1$.
\item Enter the points: $(1, 3)$, $(2, 8)$, $(4, 28)$.
\item Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c} in an expression line.
\item Desmos instantly gives you $a$, $b$, and $c$.
\item Write the equation: $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ with the values Desmos found.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{This works for ANY function type:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Linear (2 points):} \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}
\item \textbf{Quadratic (3 points):} \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}
\item \textbf{Exponential (2+ points):} \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a * b\^{}x1}
\item \textbf{Power function:} \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a * x1\^{}b}
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:}

A ball is thrown upward. Its height $h$ in feet at time $t$ seconds is recorded:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline
$t$ (seconds) & $h$ (feet) \\
\hline
0 & 4 \\
1 & 52 \\
2 & 68 \\
3 & 52 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Which equation best models the height?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $h = -16t^2 + 64t + 4$
\item $h = -16t^2 + 48t + 4$
\item $h = -32t^2 + 64t + 4$
\item $h = -16t^2 + 64t - 4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Regression Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Create a table: enter $(0, 4)$, $(1, 52)$, $(2, 68)$, $(3, 52)$.
\item Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}.
\item Desmos gives: $a = -16$, $b = 64$, $c = 4$.
\item The equation is $h = -16t^2 + 64t + 4$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: A}
\end{example}

\subsection{Power Move 4: The Piecewise and Absolute Value Visualizer}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Absolute Value Equations --- Graph Both Sides:}

Given: $|2x - 7| = 3x + 1$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = abs(2x - 7)} in line 1.
\item Type \texttt{y = 3x + 1} in line 2.
\item Click the intersection(s).
\item Read the $x$-value(s).
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Why Desmos is essential here:} Absolute value equations can have 0, 1, or 2 solutions, and students often find ``extraneous'' solutions when solving by hand. Desmos shows you \textbf{only the real intersections}, automatically eliminating extraneous solutions.
\end{stepbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Piecewise Functions:}

Desmos can graph piecewise functions using curly brace notation:

\texttt{y = \{x < 0: -x, x >= 0: x\^{}2\}}

This graphs $y = -x$ for $x < 0$ and $y = x^2$ for $x \geq 0$.

\textbf{SAT Application:} When given a piecewise function and asked for a specific value, type the piecewise into Desmos and evaluate directly.
\end{stepbox}

\subsection{Power Move 5: The System Solver for Complex Systems}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Non-Linear Systems --- Just Graph Both:}

Given the system:
\begin{align*}
y &= x^2 - 3x + 2 \\
y &= -x + 5
\end{align*}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 3x + 2} in line 1.
\item Type \texttt{y = -x + 5} in line 2.
\item Click both intersections.
\item Desmos shows the solutions as coordinate pairs.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{SAT Variations:}
\begin{itemize}
\item ``How many solutions does the system have?'' $\rightarrow$ Count intersections.
\item ``What is the sum of the $x$-coordinates?'' $\rightarrow$ Add the $x$-values of the intersections.
\item ``What is the product of the $y$-coordinates?'' $\rightarrow$ Multiply the $y$-values.
\end{itemize}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Hard Problem:}

How many solutions does the system have?
\begin{align*}
x^2 + y^2 &= 25 \\
y &= x^2 - 5
\end{align*}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 = 25} (this graphs a circle of radius 5).
\item Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 5} (this graphs a parabola).
\item Count the intersection points visually.
\item Desmos shows \textbf{3} intersection points.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: C}
\end{example}

\subsection{Power Move 6: The Equivalent Expression Verifier}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Equivalent Expression Verifier} tests whether two expressions are algebraically equivalent by graphing both and checking if they produce identical graphs, or by evaluating both at several values and checking for identical outputs.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Method 1 --- Graph Comparison:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph the original expression as \texttt{y = [original]}.
\item Graph each answer choice on separate lines.
\item The answer choice whose graph \textbf{perfectly overlaps} the original is the equivalent expression.
\item \textbf{Visual cue:} If two graphs are identical, you'll see only one line (they overlap completely).
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Method 2 --- Numerical Verification:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Define \texttt{f(x) = [original expression]}.
\item Define \texttt{g(x) = [answer choice]}.
\item Evaluate both at 3--4 different $x$-values: \texttt{f(1)}, \texttt{g(1)}, \texttt{f(2)}, \texttt{g(2)}, etc.
\item If all outputs match, the expressions are equivalent.
\item \textbf{Test at least 3 values} to be safe (two expressions can agree at 1--2 points by coincidence).
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Problem:}

Which expression is equivalent to $\dfrac{x^2 - 9}{x + 3}$ for $x \neq -3$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x - 3$
\item $x + 3$
\item $x^2 - 3$
\item $x - 9$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Verifier:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type \texttt{f(x) = (x\^{}2 - 9)/(x + 3)}.
\item Type \texttt{g(x) = x - 3}.
\item Both graphs appear as the same line (with a hole at $x = -3$ for $f$).
\item Confirm: \texttt{f(5) = 2} and \texttt{g(5) = 2}. \texttt{f(0) = -3} and \texttt{g(0) = -3}. Match!
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: A}
\end{example}

\begin{warningbox}
\textbf{The Coincidence Trap:}

Two different expressions can give the same output at one or two specific values. For example, $x^2$ and $2x$ both equal $4$ when $x = 2$. Always test at \textbf{at least 3 different values} (including negative numbers and zero) to confirm equivalence. Better yet, use the graph overlay method.
\end{warningbox}

\subsection{Power Move 7: The ``Let Desmos Do the Algebra'' Technique}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{The Ultimate Mindset Shift:}

Many hard SAT problems are designed to be algebraically messy on purpose. The test-
makers want you to spend 3 minutes wrestling with fractions, radicals, and multi-step algebra. But Desmos can do that algebra for you. Your job is to \textbf{translate the problem into something Desmos can handle}, then let the calculator do the heavy lifting.
\end{insightbox}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{The ``Let Desmos Do the Algebra'' Framework:}

\textbf{Scenario 1 --- Simplifying Complex Expressions:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type the complex expression into Desmos as a function: \texttt{f(x) = [messy expression]}.
\item Type each answer choice as \texttt{g(x) = [choice]}.
\item The one that produces the same graph is the simplified form.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Scenario 2 --- Solving Multi-Step Equations:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Instead of isolating the variable step by step, graph both sides.
\item The intersection gives you the answer instantly.
\item No algebra mistakes, no sign errors, no lost minus signs.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Scenario 3 --- Finding Unknown Constants:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item If a problem says ``the equation $2x^2 + bx + 5 = 0$ has a solution at $x = 1$,'' type \texttt{2(1)\^{}2 + b(1) + 5 = 0} into Desmos.
\item Desmos won't solve this directly, but you can type \texttt{y = 2x\^{}2 + bx + 5} with a \textbf{slider for $b$}.
\item Adjust the slider until the graph passes through $x = 1$ on the $x$-axis.
\item Read the value of $b$ from the slider.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Hard Problem --- Finding a Constant:}

The function $f(x) = x^3 - 6x^2 + kx + 10$ has a factor of $(x - 5)$. What is the value of $k$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-1$
\item $3$
\item $5$
\item $13$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Strategy:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item If $(x-5)$ is a factor, then $f(5) = 0$.
\item Type \texttt{f(x) = x\^{}3 - 6x\^{}2 + kx + 10} with a slider for $k$.
\item Type \texttt{f(5)} on a new line.
\item Adjust the $k$ slider until \texttt{f(5) = 0}.
\item The slider shows $k = 3$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Quick Verification by Hand:} $f(5) = 125 - 150 + 5k + 10 = 5k - 15 = 0 \Rightarrow k = 3$. \checkmark

\textbf{Answer: B}
\end{example}

\subsection{Power Move 8: The Regression Shortcut for Pattern Problems}

\begin{definition}
The \textbf{Regression Shortcut} uses Desmos's built-in regression feature to find the equation that fits a set of data points. When the SAT gives you a table of values and asks for the equation, regression finds it instantly.
\end{definition}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Regression for ``Find the Equation'' Problems:}

\textbf{Given a table:}
\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline
$x$ & $y$ \\
\hline
0 & 3 \\
1 & 9 \\
2 & 27 \\
3 & 81 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Click the ``+'' button and select \textbf{Table}.
\item Enter the $x$ and $y$ values.
\item On a new line, type the regression model:
\begin{itemize}
\item For exponential: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a * b\^{}x1}
\item For linear: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}
\item For quadratic: \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c}
\end{itemize}
\item Desmos shows: $a = 3$, $b = 3$, so the equation is $y = 3 \cdot 3^x$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{How to Choose the Right Regression Model:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{Constant differences} between consecutive $y$-values $\rightarrow$ \textbf{Linear} (\texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b})
\item \textbf{Constant ratios} between consecutive $y$-values $\rightarrow$ \textbf{Exponential} (\texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a*b\^{}x1})
\item \textbf{Constant second differences} $\rightarrow$ \textbf{Quadratic} (\texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} ax1\^{}2 + bx1 + c})
\item \textbf{Not sure?} Try all three and see which one has $R^2$ closest to $1$.
\end{itemize}
\end{insightbox}

\begin{example}
\textbf{SAT Data Problem:}

A biologist recorded the following population data:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline
Year ($t$) & Population \\
\hline
0 & 500 \\
1 & 600 \\
2 & 720 \\
3 & 864 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Which function best models this data?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $P(t) = 500 + 120t$
\item $P(t) = 500(1.2)^t$
\item $P(t) = 500(0.8)^t$
\item $P(t) = 120t^2 + 500$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Solution:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Enter the data in a table.
\item Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a * b\^{}x1}.
\item Desmos gives $a = 500$, $b = 1.2$.
\item The model is $P(t) = 500(1.2)^t$.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Quick Check:} $\frac{600}{500} = 1.2$, $\frac{720}{600} = 1.2$, $\frac{864}{720} = 1.2$. Constant ratio confirms exponential growth.

\textbf{Answer: B}
\end{example}

\subsection{Power Move 9: The Inequality Shading Trick}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{Solving Inequalities and Systems of Inequalities:}

\textbf{Single Inequality:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Instead of typing \texttt{y = 2x + 3}, type \texttt{y > 2x + 3} or \texttt{y < 2x + 3}.
\item Desmos automatically \textbf{shades} the solution region.
\item To check if a point is in the solution set, see if it falls in the shaded region.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{System of Inequalities:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Type each inequality on a separate line:
\begin{itemize}
\item Line 1: \texttt{y > 2x + 3}
\item Line 2: \texttt{y < -x + 7}
\item Line 3: \texttt{x > 0}
\end{itemize}
\item The \textbf{overlapping shaded region} is the solution set.
\item To find which point satisfies all inequalities, check which answer choice falls in the overlap.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{SAT Application:} ``Which point is in the solution set of the system?''
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph all inequalities.
\item Plot each answer choice as a point: \texttt{(2, 5)}, \texttt{(3, 1)}, etc.
\item The point that falls in the \textbf{darkest shaded region} (where all shadings overlap) is the answer.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\subsection{Power Move 10: The ``Zoom and Trace'' Precision Technique}

\begin{stepbox}
\textbf{When You Need Extreme Precision:}

Sometimes the SAT asks for values that are very close together, or you need to distinguish between answer choices like $3.5$ and $3.6$. Desmos's default view might not be precise enough.

\textbf{Zoom In:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Graph the relevant function(s).
\item Use the \textbf{scroll wheel} (or pinch gesture on a trackpad) to zoom in on the area of interest.
\item Click directly on intersection points, vertices, or intercepts.
\item Desmos shows coordinates with high precision.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Adjust the Window:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Click the \textbf{wrench icon} (settings).
\item Manually set $x$-min, $x$-max, $y$-min, $y$-max to focus on the relevant region.
\item This is especially useful for problems where the interesting behavior happens in a small range.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Use the Number Line for Single-Variable Problems:}
\begin{enumerate}
\item If you just need to evaluate an expression, type it directly.
\item Desmos shows the exact value (or a precise decimal).
\item No graphing needed --- just use Desmos as a scientific calculator.
\end{enumerate}
\end{stepbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.1:}

The equation $x^4 - 5x^2 + 4 = 0$ has how many real solutions?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $0$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: D}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type \texttt{y = x\^{}4 - 5x\^{}2 + 4} into Desmos. The graph crosses the $x$-axis at 4 points: $x = -2, -1, 1, 2$. Count the $x$-intercepts to find the number of real solutions.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.2:}

Which expression is equivalent to $\dfrac{x^2 + 5x + 6}{x^2 - 4}$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $\dfrac{x+3}{x-2}$
\item $\dfrac{x+3}{x+2}$
\item $\dfrac{x+2}{x-2}$
\item $\dfrac{x-3}{x-2}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type \texttt{f(x) = (x\^{}2 + 5x + 6)/(x\^{}2 - 4)} and \texttt{g(x) = (x+3)/(x-2)} into Desmos. Both graphs overlap perfectly (same curve, same asymptotes). Confirm: $f(0) = 6/(-4) = -1.5$ and $g(0) = 3/(-2) = -1.5$. Match!
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.3:}

A slider for $a$ is set up in Desmos with the equation $y = a(x-2)^2 + 3$. When the parabola passes through the point $(0, 11)$, what is the value of $a$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type \texttt{y = a(x-2)\^{}2 + 3} with a slider for $a$. Plot the point \texttt{(0, 11)}. Adjust the slider until the parabola passes through the point. The slider shows $a = 2$. Verify: $2(0-2)^2 + 3 = 2(4) + 3 = 11$. \checkmark
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.4:}

The table below shows values of a function $f$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
$x$ & $0$ & $1$ & $2$ & $3$ & $4$ \\
\hline
$f(x)$ & $2$ & $6$ & $18$ & $54$ & $162$ \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Which function models this data?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $f(x) = 2(3)^x$
\item $f(x) = 3(2)^x$
\item $f(x) = 2x + 4$
\item $f(x) = 2x^2 + 2$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Enter the data in a Desmos table. Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a*b\^{}x1}. Desmos gives $a = 2$, $b = 3$. The function is $f(x) = 2(3)^x$. Quick check: $f(0) = 2$, $f(1) = 6$, $f(2) = 18$. All match.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.5:}

The system of inequalities $y \geq x + 1$ and $y \leq -2x + 10$ is graphed. Which point is in the solution set?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(5, 2)$
\item $(2, 4)$
\item $(0, 0)$
\item $(4, 6)$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type \texttt{y >= x + 1} and \texttt{y <= -2x + 10} into Desmos. Plot each point. Only $(2, 4)$ falls in the overlapping shaded region. Verify: $4 \geq 2 + 1 = 3$ \checkmark and $4 \leq -2(2) + 10 = 6$ \checkmark.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.6:}

What is the value of $f(g(3))$ if $f(x) = 2x - 1$ and $g(x) = x^2 + 2$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $21$
\item $22$
\item $23$
\item $25$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type \texttt{f(x) = 2x - 1} and \texttt{g(x) = x\^{}2 + 2} into Desmos. Then type \texttt{f(g(3))}. Desmos computes: $g(3) = 9 + 2 = 11$, then $f(11) = 2(11) - 1 = 21$.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.7:}

A circle has the equation $(x-4)^2 + (y+1)^2 = 36$. What is the $y$-coordinate of the topmost point on the circle?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $5$
\item $6$
\item $7$
\item $-7$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: A}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type \texttt{(x-4)\^{}2 + (y+1)\^{}2 = 36} into Desmos. The circle appears with center $(4, -1)$ and radius $6$. The topmost point is at $y = -1 + 6 = 5$. Click on the top of the circle to confirm.
\end{highlightbox}

\begin{highlightbox}
\textbf{Practice Question 13.8:}

For what value of $c$ does the equation $2x^2 - 8x + c = 0$ have exactly one real solution?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $4$
\item $8$
\item $16$
\item $32$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Answer: B}

\textbf{Rationale:} Type \texttt{y = 2x\^{}2 - 8x + c} with a slider for $c$. Adjust the slider until the parabola just touches the $x$-axis (one solution = vertex on the $x$-axis). This occurs at $c = 8$. Verify with discriminant: $b^2 - 4ac = 64 - 4(2)(8) = 64 - 64 = 0$. One solution confirmed.
\end{highlightbox}

%% ================================================================
%% SECTION 14: SAT QUESTION TYPE MASTER REFERENCE
%% ================================================================

\newpage
\section{SAT Question Type Master Reference}

\begin{contextbox}
This section is your \textbf{ultimate cheat sheet}. It maps every major SAT Math question type to the exact Desmos strategy you should use. Print this section, memorize it, and practice each strategy until it becomes automatic.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Algebra Question Types}

\begin{longtable}{|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm}|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}|}
\hline
\textbf{Question Type} & \textbf{Desmos Strategy} & \textbf{What to Type} \\
\hline
\endfirsthead
\hline
\textbf{Question Type} & \textbf{Desmos Strategy} & \textbf{What to Type} \\
\hline
\endhead
Solve a linear equation & Direct solve & Type both sides as $y =$ lines; find intersection \\
\hline
Solve a quadratic equation & Graph zeros & Type \texttt{y = [quadratic]}; click $x$-intercepts \\
\hline
System of 2 linear equations & Graph intersection & Type both equations; click intersection point \\
\hline
Number of solutions (linear system) & Visual check & Graph both lines: parallel = 0, same = infinite, cross = 1 \\
\hline
Number of solutions (quadratic-linear) & Count intersections & Graph both; count intersection points \\
\hline
Absolute value equation & Graph both sides & Type \texttt{y = |expression|} and \texttt{y = value}; find intersections \\
\hline
Radical equation & Graph both sides & Type \texttt{y = sqrt(expression)} and \texttt{y = [other side]} \\
\hline
Rational equation & Graph both sides & Type both sides; find intersections (ignore vertical asymptotes) \\
\hline
Equivalent expressions & Graph overlay & Graph original and each choice; matching graph = answer \\
\hline
Exponential growth/decay & Regression or evaluate & Enter data in table; run \texttt{y1\textasciitilde{}ab\^{}x1} \\
\hline
Literal equations (solve for variable) & Slider method & Use sliders for known values; read unknown \\
\hline
\end{longtable}

\subsection{Functions Question Types}

\begin{longtable}{|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm}|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}|}
\hline
\textbf{Question Type} & \textbf{Desmos Strategy} & \textbf{What to Type} \\
\hline
\endfirsthead
\hline
\textbf{Question Type} & \textbf{Desmos Strategy} & \textbf{What to Type} \\
\hline
\endhead
Evaluate $f(a)$ & Direct evaluation & Type \texttt{f(x) = [rule]}; then type \texttt{f(a)} \\
\hline
Find $x$ when $f(x) = k$ & Horizontal line & Graph $f(x)$ and \texttt{y = k}; find intersections \\
\hline
Domain of a function & Graph inspection & Graph $f(x)$; observe where it exists \\
\hline
Range of a function & Graph inspection & Graph $f(x)$; observe $y$-values covered \\
\hline
Composition $f(g(x))$ & Nested typing & Type \texttt{f(x)}, \texttt{g(x)}, then \texttt{f(g(value))} \\
\hline
Maximum/minimum value & Graph vertex & Graph the function; click the highest/lowest point \\
\hline
Increasing/decreasing intervals & Visual inspection & Graph $f(x)$; identify where curve goes up/down \\
\hline
Transformations & Compare graphs & Graph parent and transformed; observe shifts/stretches \\
\hline
End behavior & Zoom out & Graph function; zoom out to see long-term trend \\
\hline
Vertex form of quadratic & Graph and click & Type quadratic; click vertex to read $(h, k)$ \\
\hline
\end{longtable}

\subsection{Geometry and Trigonometry Question Types}

\begin{longtable}{|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm}|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}|}
\hline
\textbf{Question Type} & \textbf{Desmos Strategy} & \textbf{What to Type} \\
\hline
\endfirsthead
\hline
\textbf{Question Type} & \textbf{Desmos Strategy} & \textbf{What to Type} \\
\hline
\endhead
Circle equation & Graph directly & Type equation; identify center and radius visually \\
\hline
Line-circle intersection & Graph both & Type circle and line; click intersections \\
\hline
Distance between points & Distance formula & Type \texttt{sqrt((x2-x1)\^{}2 + (y2-y1)\^{}2)} with values \\
\hline
Midpoint & Direct compute & Type \texttt{((x1+x2)/2, (y1+y2)/2)} \\
\hline
Trig evaluation & Direct compute & Set degree/radian mode; type \texttt{sin(value)}, etc. \\
\hline
Inverse trig & Direct compute & Type \texttt{arcsin(value)}, \texttt{arccos(value)}, etc. \\
\hline
Right triangle trig & Compute ratios & Type the ratio; use inverse trig to find angle \\
\hline
Trig identity verification & Graph overlay & Graph both sides; check if graphs overlap \\
\hline
Arc length / sector area & Compute formula & Type the formula with values substituted \\
\hline
Coordinate geometry (slope) & Direct compute & Type \texttt{(y2-y1)/(x2-x1)} with values \\
\hline
\end{longtable}

\subsection{Statistics and Data Analysis Question Types}

\begin{longtable}{|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4cm}|>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}|}
\hline
\textbf{Question Type} & \textbf{Desmos Strategy} & \textbf{What to Type} \\
\hline
\endfirsthead
\hline
\textbf{Question Type} & \textbf{Desmos Strategy} & \textbf{What to Type} \\
\hline
\endhead
Mean of a data set & Direct compute & Type values in a list $L$; type \texttt{mean(L)} \\
\hline
Median of a data set & Direct compute & Type values in list $L$; type \texttt{median(L)} \\
\hline
Standard deviation & Direct compute & Type values in list $L$; type \texttt{stdev(L)} \\
\hline
Line of best fit & Regression & Enter data in table; type \texttt{y1\textasciitilde{}mx1+b} \\
\hline
Quadratic regression & Regression & Enter data in table; type \texttt{y1\textasciitilde{}ax1\^{}2+bx1+c} \\
\hline
Exponential regression & Regression & Enter data in table; type \texttt{y1\textasciitilde{}ab\^{}x1} \\
\hline
Prediction from model & Evaluate regression & After regression, type \texttt{y = mx + b} with values from Desmos; evaluate at desired $x$ \\
\hline
Interpreting slope & Read from regression & Slope $m$ from \texttt{y1\textasciitilde{}mx1+b} is the rate of change \\
\hline
Residuals & Graph comparison & Compare data points to regression line visually \\
\hline
\end{longtable}

\begin{insightbox}
\textbf{How to Use This Reference:}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Before the SAT:} Study this table until you can instantly match question types to strategies.
\item \textbf{During the SAT:} When you see a question, mentally classify it (``This is a system of equations problem''), then recall the strategy (``Graph both, click intersection'').
\item \textbf{Practice:} Do at least 5 real SAT problems for each question type using the Desmos strategy listed.
\end{enumerate}
\end{insightbox}

%% SECTION 15: COMPLETE TIMED PRACTICE SET

\newpage
\section{Complete Timed Practice Set --- SAT Desmos Simulation}

\begin{contextbox}
This section contains \textbf{22 practice problems} simulating a full SAT Math module. For each problem, you should:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Time yourself:} Aim for 1 minute 35 seconds or less per problem.
\item \textbf{Use Desmos for every applicable problem.}
\item \textbf{Record your Desmos keystrokes} so you build muscle memory.
\item \textbf{Check your answers} against the detailed solutions that follow.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Target:} Complete all 22 problems in under 35 minutes.
\end{contextbox}

\subsection{Practice Problems}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 1:} What is the solution to the equation $3(x - 4) = 2x + 5$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $7$
\item $12$
\item $17$
\item $-7$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 2:} If $f(x) = x^3 - 4x + 2$, what is $f(-2)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $2$
\item $-2$
\item $6$
\item $10$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 3:} How many real solutions does the equation $x^2 + 4x + 5 = 0$ have?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $0$
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 4:} The line $y = 2x + b$ passes through the point $(3, 11)$. What is the value of $b$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $3$
\item $5$
\item $7$
\item $11$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 5:} What is the vertex of the parabola $y = -2(x - 3)^2 + 7$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $(3, 7)$
\item $(-3, 7)$
\item $(3, -7)$
\item $(-3, -7)$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 6:} A system of equations is given:
\begin{align*}
y &= 3x - 1 \\
y &= -x + 7
\end{align*}
What is the $x$-coordinate of the solution?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $1$
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 7:} Which of the following is equivalent to $\dfrac{x^2 - 16}{x - 4}$ for $x \neq 4$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x - 4$
\item $x + 4$
\item $x^2 + 4$
\item $x - 16$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 8:} The function $g(x) = 500(0.85)^x$ models the value of a computer in dollars after $x$ years. What is the value after 3 years, to the nearest dollar?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $\$307$
\item $\$319$
\item $\$354$
\item $\$425$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 9:} What are the solutions to $|2x - 6| = 10$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $x = 8$ and $x = -2$
\item $x = 8$ and $x = 2$
\item $x = -8$ and $x = 2$
\item $x = -8$ and $x = -2$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 10:} The data set is: $\{12, 15, 18, 18, 20, 22, 25\}$. What is the mean?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $18$
\item $18.57$
\item $19$
\item $20$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 11:} For what value of $k$ does the system below have no solution?
\begin{align*}
y &= 3x + 5 \\
y &= kx - 2
\end{align*}

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $k = -3$
\item $k = 3$
\item $k = 5$
\item $k = -2$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 12:} A circle has the equation $x^2 + y^2 = 49$. What is the radius?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $7$
\item $49$
\item $\
sqrt{7}$
\item $24.5$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 13:} If $f(x) = 3x^2 - 2x + 1$, what is $f(-2)$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $9$
\item $13$
\item $17$
\item $21$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{f(x) = 3x\^{}2 - 2x + 1}, then type \texttt{f(-2)}. Desmos outputs $17$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 14:} The graph of $y = a \cdot b^x$ passes through the points $(0, 4)$ and $(2, 36)$. What is the value of $b$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $2$
\item $3$
\item $4$
\item $9$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Create a table with $(0, 4)$ and $(2, 36)$. Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a * b\^{}x1}. Desmos returns $a = 4$, $b = 3$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 15:} What is the sum of the solutions of $x^2 - 5x - 14 = 0$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-5$
\item $5$
\item $7$
\item $14$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{x\^{}2 - 5x - 14 = 0}. Desmos shows $x = 7$ and $x = -2$. Sum $= 7 + (-2) = 5$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 16:} A line passes through the points $(1, 3)$ and $(4, 15)$. What is the equation of the line?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $y = 4x - 1$
\item $y = 4x + 1$
\item $y = 3x$
\item $y = 5x - 2$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Enter both points in a table. Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}. Desmos gives $m = 4$, $b = -1$, so $y = 4x - 1$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 17:} If $\log_3(x) = 4$, what is the value of $x$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $12$
\item $64$
\item $81$
\item $256$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{3\^{}4} and Desmos shows $81$. Alternatively, type \texttt{log(x)/log(3) = 4} and Desmos highlights $x = 81$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 18:} The function $h(t) = -16t^2 + 64t + 5$ models the height of a ball in feet after $t$ seconds. What is the maximum height?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $64$ feet
\item $69$ feet
\item $80$ feet
\item $85$ feet
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{h(t) = -16t\^{}2 + 64t + 5}. Click on the vertex of the parabola. Desmos shows the maximum at $(2, 69)$. Maximum height $= 69$ feet.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 19:} Which of the following represents the solution set of $x^2 - 4x - 12 \leq 0$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $-2 \leq x \leq 6$
\item $x \leq -2$ or $x \geq 6$
\item $-6 \leq x \leq 2$
\item $x \leq -6$ or $x \geq 2$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Type \texttt{x\^{}2 - 4x - 12 <= 0}. Desmos shades the region on the $x$-axis from $-2$ to $6$. The solution is $-2 \leq x \leq 6$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
\textbf{Problem 20:} A table shows:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
$x$ & $1$ & $2$ & $3$ & $4$ \\
\hline
$y$ & $5$ & $11$ & $17$ & $23$ \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

What is the value of $y$ when $x = 10$?

\begin{enumerate}[label=\Alph*)]
\item $53$
\item $59$
\item $65$
\item $71$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Desmos Method:} Enter the table data. Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}. Desmos gives $m = 6$, $b = -1$. So $y = 6x - 1$. Type \texttt{6(10) - 1 = 59}.
\end{exercise}

%% ================================================================
%% SECTION 15: SOLUTIONS TO PRACTICE PROBLEMS
%% ================================================================

\newpage
\section{Solutions to All Practice Problems}

\subsection{Solutions for Section 14: Comprehensive Practice Set}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 1: Answer --- C ($x = 5$ and $x = -1$)}

Type \texttt{y = x\^{}2 - 4x - 5} into Desmos. The parabola crosses the $x$-axis at $x = 5$ and $x = -1$. These are the solutions.

\textbf{Verification:} $(5)^2 - 4(5) - 5 = 25 - 20 - 5 = 0$ \checkmark \quad $(-1)^2 - 4(-1) - 5 = 1 + 4 - 5 = 0$ \checkmark
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 2: Answer --- B ($y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 4$)}

A line perpendicular to $y = 2x + 3$ has slope $m = -\frac{1}{2}$ (negative reciprocal of $2$). Using point $(2, 3)$: $y - 3 = -\frac{1}{2}(x - 2)$, so $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 4$.

\textbf{Desmos Verification:} Graph both lines. They intersect at a right angle, and the new line passes through $(2, 3)$.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 3: Answer --- B ($2$)}

Type \texttt{y = x\^{}3 - 6x\^{}2 + 11x - 6} into Desmos. The graph crosses the $x$-axis at $x = 1$, $x = 2$, and $x = 3$. The smallest positive root greater than 1 is $x = 2$.

Alternatively, since the roots are $1, 2, 3$, the answer is $2$.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 4: Answer --- B ($b = 5$)}

Type \texttt{y = 2x + b} with a slider for $b$. Plot the point $(3, 11)$. Adjust $b$ until the line passes through the point. Slider shows $b = 5$.

\textbf{Algebraic check:} $11 = 2(3) + b \Rightarrow 11 = 6 + b \Rightarrow b = 5$ \checkmark
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 5: Answer --- A ($(3, 7)$)}

The equation $y = -2(x-3)^2 + 7$ is in vertex form $y = a(x-h)^2 + k$. The vertex is $(h, k) = (3, 7)$.

\textbf{Desmos Verification:} Type the equation and click on the highest point of the parabola. Desmos shows $(3, 7)$.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 6: Answer --- B ($x = 2$)}

Type \texttt{y = 3x - 1} and \texttt{y = -x + 7} into Desmos. The lines intersect at $(2, 5)$. The $x$-coordinate is $2$.

\textbf{Algebraic check:} $3x - 1 = -x + 7 \Rightarrow 4x = 8 \Rightarrow x = 2$ \checkmark
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 7: Answer --- B ($x + 4$)}

Type \texttt{f(x) = (x\^{}2 - 16)/(x - 4)} and \texttt{g(x) = x + 4} into Desmos. Both graphs are identical (a straight line with a hole at $x = 4$ for $f$).

\textbf{Algebraic check:} $\frac{x^2 - 16}{x - 4} = \frac{(x-4)(x+4)}{x-4} = x + 4$ for $x \neq 4$ \checkmark
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 8: Answer --- A ($\$307$)}

Type \texttt{g(x) = 500(0.85)\^{}x} into Desmos. Then type \texttt{g(3)}. Desmos shows:

$500(0.85)^3 = 500(0.614125) = 307.0625 \approx \$307$
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 9: Answer --- A ($x = 8$ and $x = -2$)}

Type \texttt{y = |2x - 6|} and \texttt{y = 10} into Desmos. The V-shaped graph intersects the horizontal line at $x = 8$ and $x = -2$.

\textbf{Algebraic check:}
\begin{itemize}
\item $2x - 6 = 10 \Rightarrow 2x = 16 \Rightarrow x = 8$ \checkmark
\item $2x - 6 = -10 \Rightarrow 2x = -4 \Rightarrow x = -2$ \checkmark
\end{itemize}
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 10: Answer --- B ($18.57$)}

Type \texttt{mean(12, 15, 18, 18, 20, 22, 25)} into Desmos (or compute manually).

$\frac{12 + 15 + 18 + 18 + 20 + 22 + 25}{7} = \frac{130}{7} \approx 18.57$
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 11: Answer --- B ($k = 3$)}

A system has no solution when the lines are parallel (same slope, different $y$-intercept). The first line has slope $3$. For no solution, $k = 3$ (same slope) and the $y$-intercepts are different ($5 \neq -2$).

\textbf{Desmos Verification:} Type \texttt{y = 3x + 5} and \texttt{y = 3x - 2}. The lines are parallel --- they never intersect.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 12: Answer --- A ($7$)}

The equation $x^2 + y^2 = 49$ is a circle centered at the origin with radius $r = \sqrt{49} = 7$.

\textbf{Desmos Verification:} Type \texttt{x\^{}2 + y\^{}2 = 49}. The circle appears. Hover over the rightmost point to see $(7, 0)$, confirming radius $= 7$.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 13: Answer --- C ($17$)}

Type \texttt{f(x) = 3x\^{}2 - 2x + 1} into Desmos. Then type \texttt{f(-2)}.

$f(-2) = 3(-2)^2 - 2(-2) + 1 = 3(4) + 4 + 1 = 12 + 4 + 1 = 17$
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 14: Answer --- B ($b = 3$)}

Enter the points $(0, 4)$ and $(2, 36)$ in a table. Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} a * b\^{}x1}. Desmos returns $a = 4$ and $b = 3$.

\textbf{Check:} $4 \cdot 3^0 = 4$ \checkmark \quad $4 \cdot 3^2 = 4 \cdot 9 = 36$ \checkmark
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 15: Answer --- B ($5$)}

Type \texttt{x\^{}2 - 5x - 14 = 0} into Desmos. Solutions: $x = 7$ and $x = -2$.

Sum $= 7 + (-2) = 5$.

\textbf{Shortcut:} By Vieta's formulas, the sum of roots of $x^2 + bx + c = 0$ is $-b$. Here $-(-5) = 5$.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 16: Answer --- A ($y = 4x - 1$)}

Enter $(1, 3)$ and $(4, 15)$ in a Desmos table. Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}. Desmos gives $m = 4$, $b = -1$.

\textbf{Check:} Slope $= \frac{15 - 3}{4 - 1} = \frac{12}{3} = 4$. Using point $(1, 3)$: $3 = 4(1) + b \Rightarrow b = -1$. Equation: $y = 4x - 1$ \checkmark
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 17: Answer --- C ($81$)}

$\log_3(x) = 4$ means $3^4 = x$. Type \texttt{3\^{}4} into Desmos. Result: $81$.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 18: Answer --- B ($69$ feet)}

Type \texttt{h(t) = -16t\^{}2 + 64t + 5} into Desmos. Click on the vertex (highest point) of the parabola. Desmos shows the vertex at $(2, 69)$.

Maximum height $= 69$ feet at $t = 2$ seconds.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 19: Answer --- A ($-2 \leq x \leq 6$)}

Type \texttt{x\^{}2 - 4x - 12 <= 0} into Desmos. The shaded region on the number line extends from $x = -2$ to $x = 6$.

\textbf{Check roots:} $x^2 - 4x - 12 = (x - 6)(x + 2) = 0$ gives $x = 6$ and $x = -2$. Since the parabola opens up, the expression is $\leq 0$ between the roots.
\end{solution}

\begin{solution}
\textbf{Problem 20: Answer --- B ($59$)}

Enter the table data into Desmos. Type \texttt{y1 \textasciitilde{} mx1 + b}. Desmos gives $m = 6$, $b = -1$.

$y = 6(10) - 1 = 60 - 1 = 59$
\end{solution}
